Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Definition and Ruling of Nikah: Hanafi Position and Dispute with Shafi'i
Defines nikah linguistically and legally, states its ruling, and contrasts Hanafi and Shafi'i views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of nikah, its ruling, and explain the practical consequence of the linguistic dispute with the Shafi'i school regarding marriage prohibitions and oaths.

Core Idea
Nikah in Hanafi literal meaning is intercourse, affecting marriage prohibitions and oaths.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the Book of Nikah by establishing the legal status of marriage and its definition. The author states that marriage is an emphasized Sunnah, becoming wajib when one fears falling into sin. He then delves into a linguistic analysis of the word 'nikah', presenting four views and highlighting the practical difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools in the interpretation of Quran 4:22. Understanding this linguistic foundation is key to correctly applying marriage-related rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling of nikah
Read the opening sentence: 'يرد على ملك المتعة قصدا وهو سنة وعند التوقان واجب'. Determine that marriage is sunnah by default but wajib when one's desire is strong (tawaqan).
Note the shift from sunnah to wajib based on the individual's condition.
2
Understand the linguistic definitions of 'nikah'
The passage presents four scholarly views. Focus on the Hanafi view: nikah is literal for intercourse (wat') and metaphorical for contract. Contrast with the Shafi'i view that it is literal for contract.
The Hanafi view is attributed to 'our scholars' (مشايخنا) and supported by lexicons.
3
Locate the thamarah al-khilaf in Quran 4:22
The passage cites the verse 'ولا تنكحوا ما نكح آباؤكم من النساء'. Apply the Hanafi interpretation: nikah means intercourse, so the verse prohibits marrying any woman with whom the father had intercourse, including from zina. The Shafi'i view limits the prohibition to women under a valid marriage contract.
This is the practical consequence (ثمرة) of the linguistic dispute.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle to oath examples
Analyze the example: a husband says to his wife 'إن نكحتك فأنت طالق'. According to Hanafi, 'nikah' here means intercourse, so if he divorces her irrevocably and then remarries, the oath is not broken. For a stranger woman, 'nikah' refers to the contract because intercourse is impossible.
This shows how the linguistic definition guides legal interpretation in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح
يرد على ملك المتعة قصدا وهو سنة وعند التوقان واجب وينعقد بإيجاب
كتاب النكاح
ذكره بعد العبادات لأنه أقرب إليها حتى كان الاشتغال به أفضل من التخلي لنوافل العبادات . وقدم على الجهاد لاشتماله على المصالح الدينية والدنيوية وأمر المناسبة سهل. واختلف في معناه لغة على أربعة أقوال : فقيل مشترك بين الوطء والعقد وهو ظاهر ما في الصحاح فإنه قال : النكاح الوطء، وقد يكون العقد تقول نكحتها ونكحت هي أي تزوجت وهي ناكح في بني فلان أي ذات زوج، والمراد بالمشترك اللفظي . وقيل حقيقة في العقد مجاز في الوطء، ونسبه الأصوليون إلى الشافعي في بحث متى أمكن العمل بالحقيقة سقط المجاز. وقيل بالعكس وعليه مشايخنا صرحوا به كما في فتح القدير، وجزم به في المغرب، وذكر الأصوليون أن ثمرة الاختلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي تظهر في حرمة موطوءة الأب من الزنا أخذاً من قوله تعالى ولا تنكحوا ما نكح آباؤكم من النساء﴾ [النساء : ٢٢] فلما كان حقيقة في العقد عنده لم تحرم موطوءته من الزنا، ولما كان حقيقة في الوطء عندنا الشامل للوطء عندنا الحلال والحرام حرمت وحرمت معقودة الأب بغير وطء بالإجماع. وتفرع على أصلنا ما لو قال لامرأته إن نكحتك فأنت طالق فإنه للوطء، فلو أبانها ثم تزوجها لم يحنث . ولا يرد علينا ما لو قال لأجنبية ذلك فإنه للعقد لتعذر الوطء شرعاً فكانت حقيقة مهجورة كما في الكشف . ولذا لو قال ذلك لمن لا تحل له أبداً بأن قال إن نكحتك فعبدي . انصرف إلى النكاح كما في المحيط ، وقيل حقيقة في الضم صرح به مشايخنا أيضاً لكن قال في فتح القدير : إنه لا منافاة بين كلامهم لأن الوطء من أفراد الضم والموضوع للأعم حقيقة في كل من أفراده
حر
الفساد
كتاب النكاح
قوله : (حتى كان الاشتغال به أفضل الخ أي الاشتغال بالنكاح وما يشتمل عليه من القيام بالمصالح وإعفاف الحرام عن نفسه وتربية الولد ونحو ذلك . قاله فيل النهر. وسيأتي الاستدلال على
The Book of Marriage. It responds to the ownership of pleasure intentionally, and it is sunnah, and when desire is strong it is wajib, and it is concluded by offer. The Book of Marriage. It was mentioned after acts of worship because it is closer to them, so much so that engaging in it is better than devoting oneself to supererogatory acts of worship. And it was given precedence over jihad because it encompasses both religious and worldly benefits, and the matter of appropriateness is easy. And there is a difference of opinion regarding its meaning linguistically on four sayings: It is said it is a homonym between intercourse and contract, and that is apparent from what is in al-Sihah, for he said: al-nikah is intercourse, and it may be the contract; you say 'I married her' (nakhahtuha) and 'she married' (nakahat hi) i.e. she got married, and she is a wife among the tribe of so-and-so, i.e. a woman with a husband. And what is intended is the verbal homonym. And it is said it is literal for contract and metaphorical for intercourse, and the usul scholars attribute this to al-Shafi'i in the discussion when it is possible to act on the literal meaning, the metaphorical is dropped. And it is said the opposite, and it is the view of our scholars as they stated in Fath al-Qadir, and it is asserted in al-Mughrib. And the usul scholars mention that the fruit of disagreement between us and al-Shafi'i appears in the prohibition of the woman with whom the father committed zina, taking from His statement: 'And do not marry those whom your fathers married among women' [al-Nisa:22]. For when it is literal for contract in his view, the woman with whom he committed zina is not prohibited. And when it is literal for intercourse in our view, which includes both lawful and unlawful intercourse, she is prohibited. And the woman who was contracted by the father without intercourse is prohibited by consensus. And branches from our principle: if a man says to his wife, 'If I marry you (in nakahtuki), you are divorced,' then it is for intercourse. So if he divorces her irrevocably, then marries her, he is not liable to the oath. And it is not an objection against us if he says that to a stranger woman, for it is for the contract because intercourse is legally impossible, so the literal meaning is abandoned as in al-Kashshaf. And if he says that to one who is eternally unlawful for him, e.g. if he says, 'If I marry you, my slave is free,' it is interpreted as marriage as in al-Muhit. And it is said it is literal for inclusion (damm), as our scholars also stated. But in Fath al-Qadir it says: there is no contradiction between their statements because intercourse is an individual of inclusion, and the term is literal for the general, literal in every one of its individuals. (Marginal note) 'Hurr al-fasad' (the free of corruption). The Book of Marriage. His statement: 'So much so that engaging in it is better' etc. i.e. engagement in marriage and what it comprises of fulfilling interests, safeguarding oneself from the unlawful, raising children, and the like. He said it in al-Nahr. And the evidence will come on...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage from the beginning of the Book of Marriage (Nikah) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the legal status of marriage and the linguistic meaning of the term 'nikah'. The author states that marriage is a means to lawful sexual pleasure; it is originally Sunnah, but becomes wajib when one fears falling into sin. It is concluded by offer and acceptance. The author explains why the chapter on marriage is placed after acts of worship: marriage itself is an act of worship and engaging in it is better than supererogatory prayers. It precedes jihad because it combines both religious and worldly benefits. Then the author delves into the linguistic meaning of 'nikah', presenting four opinions: (1) it is a homonym for both intercourse and contract; (2) it is literal for contract and metaphorical for intercourse (attributed to al-Shafi'i); (3) it is literal for intercourse and metaphorical for contract (the Hanafi position); (4) it is literal for 'inclusion' (damm). The practical consequence (thamarah) of the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement is in the interpretation of Quran 4:22: 'Do not marry those whom your fathers married.' For Shafi'i, since nikah means contract, the verse prohibits marriage to women with whom the father had a marriage contract, not women with whom he committed zina. For Hanafi, since nikah means intercourse (including unlawful), the verse prohibits marriage to any woman with whom the father had intercourse, even if from zina. Both agree that a woman under a valid marriage contract with the father is prohibited by consensus. The text then illustrates the Hanafi principle with an example: if a husband says to his wife, 'If I have intercourse with you (in nakahtuki), you are divorced,' the term means intercourse, so if he divorces her irrevocably and then remarries her, he does not break his oath. However, if he says this to a stranger woman (where intercourse is impossible), the term is interpreted as marriage. Similarly, if he says it to a woman who is eternally forbidden (e.g., his mahram), it is interpreted as marriage. The passage concludes by noting the view that nikah is literal for 'inclusion' and harmonizing the opinions.

Key Terms
نكاح (nikah): Linguistically, in Hanafi, it is literal for intercourse (al-wat') and metaphorical for contract; legally, it is a contract that renders sexual relations lawful.
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse; in the Hanafi view, nikah's literal meaning is wat'.
عقد (aqd): Marriage contract; in Shafi'i view, it is the literal meaning of nikah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man commits zina with a woman, is she prohibited for his son to marry based on Quran 4:22?
A) Yes, because nikah includes unlawful intercourse.
B) No, because the verse only applies to marriage contracts.
C) Yes, but only if the father had a valid marriage with her.
D) No, because zina does not create any prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's linguistic treatment of nikah as literal for intercourse has significant consequences for marriage prohibitions and oaths.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Linguistic Meaning of Nikah and Its Impact on Prohibition (Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute)
Linguistic definition, ruling, and cross-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that nikah is haqiqah for wat' (intercourse), explain the usul principle preferring majaz over ishtirak, and apply it to the verse 'لا تنكحوا ما نكح آباؤكم' to derive the broader prohibition.

Core Idea
Nikah means intercourse, not contract — so the Quran bans marrying any woman your father had sex with, even in zina.

This lesson opens the Book of Nikah with a fundamental question: what does the word 'nikah' actually mean? The answer directly affects how we understand the Quranic prohibition of marrying women one's father 'married.' The Hanafi school holds a distinctive view that the word literally means sexual intercourse, while the Shafi'i school takes it as the marriage contract. We will explore the linguistic evidence, the usul principle that resolves the dispute, and the real-world consequences of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the ruling and meaning of nikah
Identify the hukm (sunnah, wajib at need) and the linguistic classification (haqiqah for wat', majaz for 'aqd).
The ruling is sunnah muakkadah, but becomes wajib when one fears sin.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school considers nikah as haqiqah for the marriage contract ('aqd) and majaz for intercourse.
This leads to a narrower interpretation of the Quranic verse.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic proof
The Hanafi proof rests on the usul principle that when a word is ambiguous between ishtirak (commonality) and majaz (metaphor), majaz is preferable because it is more eloquent and common.
The passage cites Al-Nasafi in Sharh al-Manar and Al-Bada'i'.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute changes the scope of the prohibition in verse 4:22. For Hanafis, it includes women fornicated with by the father; for Shafi'is, only those married by valid contract.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is that nikah is haqiqah for wat', supported by Qadi Khan and Fath al-Qadir.
The ruling of nikah itself remains sunnah, wajib at need.

Source Text

كتاب النكاح يرد على ملك المتعة قصدا وهو سنة وعند التوقان واجب وينعقد بإيجاب
قوله : (حتى كان الاشتغال به أفضل الخ أي الاشتغال بالنكاح وما يشتمل عليه من القيام بالمصالح وإعفاف الحرام عن نفسه وتربية الولد ونحو ذلك . قاله في النهر. وسيأتي الاستدلال على

كتاب النكاح
۱۳۷
كإنسان في زيد فهو من قبيل المشترك المعنوي إلى آخر ما ذكره وهو مردود، فإن الوطء مغاير للضم ولذا قال في المغرب : وقولهم النكاح الضم مجاز كإطلاقه على العقد إلا أن إطلاقه على الضم من باب تسمية المسبب باسم السبب وإطلاقه على العقد بالعكس. ومما يدل على مغايرة القولين أن صاحب المحيط ذكر أنه حقيقة في الضم الشامل للوطء والعقد باعتبار ضم الإيجاب إلى القبول فهو حقيقة في العقد أيضاً، وعلى القول الثالث مجاز فيه. وصحح في المجتبى ما في المغرب كما في التبيين ورجح في غاية البيان الأول بأن الأصل في الكلام الحقيقة والمشترك مستعمل في الموضوع الأصلي دون المجاز ا.هـ. وهو غفلة عما في الأصول فإن الأصح أنه إذا دار لفظ بين الاشتراك والمجاز فالمجاز أولى لأنه أبلغ وأغلب، والاشتراك يخل بالتفاهم ويحتاج إلى قرينتين كما ذكره النسفي في شرح المنار . وقال في البدائع : إنه الحق والمتحقق الاستعمال في كل من هذه المعاني الثلاثة لكن الشأن في تعيين المعنى الحقيقي له. وأما معناه شرعاً ففي فتح القدير : حيث أطلق في الكتاب والسنة مجرداً عن القرائن فهو للوطء فقد تساوى المعنى اللغوي والشرعي ولذا قال قاضيخان : إنه في اللغة والشرع حقيقة في الوطء مجاز في العقد. وأما ما ذكره المصنف وغيره من أنه للعقد الخاص فهو معناه في اصطلاح الفقهاء ولذا قال في المجتبى : إنه في عرف الفقهاء العقد. فقول من قال إنه في الشرع اسم للعقد الخاص كما في التبيين محمول على أن المراد أنه في عرف الفقهاء وهم أهل الشرع فلا مخالفة. وسبب مشروعيته مع أن الأصل في النكاح الخطر وإباحته للضرورة كما الكشف تعلق بقاء العالم به المقدر في العلم الأزلى على الوجه الأكمل وإلا فيمكن بقاء
أفضليته بوجوه أربعة وحققه في الفتح بما لا مزيد عليه قوله : (وهو مردود) قال في النهر : قد يمنع بأن الوطء نفسه ضم وقد جعل في المحيط الضم أعم من ضم الجسم إلى الجسم والقول إلى القول فيكون مشتركاً معنوياً أيضاً غير أن المتبادر من لفظ الضم تعلقه بالأجسام لا الأقوال لأنها أعراض بتلاشي : : الأول منها قبل وجود الثاني فلا يصادف الثاني منها ما ينضم إليه إلا أن قولهم الحقيقية والمجاز أولى من الاشتراك يرجح ما في المغرب وإن إطلاقه يعم المعنوي أيضاً ا هـ. أي إطلاق قولهم المجاز أولى من الاشتراك يعم المشترك المعنوي قوله : من باب تسمية المسبب باسم السبب) أي إطلاق النكاح الذي هو حقيقة في الوطء على الضم مجاز علاقته السببية والمسببة فإن الوطء سبب للضم فصح إطلاق النكاح عليه لكونه مسبباً عنه، وإطلاقه على العقد مجاز أيضاً فإنه سبب للوطء قوله : (وعلى القول الثالث) أي القول بأن النكاح حقيقة في الوطء يكون مجازاً في العقد قوله: (ورجح في غاية البيان الأول أي أنه مشترك بين الوطء والعقد لأن المشترك حقيقة في معنييه وهي الأصل بخلاف ما إذا كان حقيقة في أحدهما مجازاً في الآخر قوله : (من أنه اسم للعقد الخاص) أي ما يأتي في قول المصنف هو عقد يرد على ملك المتعة قوله : (في عرف الفقهاء وهم أهل الشرع) الذي في
The Book of Nikah: It (nikah) is intended for ownership of pleasure, and it is sunnah, and when there is strong desire it is obligatory, and it is concluded by offer and acceptance. ... (His saying: 'until occupying oneself with it is better' i.e., occupying oneself with nikah and what it includes of fulfilling interests, keeping oneself from unlawful acts, raising children, etc. ... And it is rejected (the view that it is a verbal homonym) because intercourse is different from union... The correct view in usul is that if a word is ambiguous between commonality (ishtirak) and metaphor, metaphor is preferable... As for its meaning in Shari'ah: in the Book and Sunnah when absolute from context, it refers to intercourse... The cause of its legislation is to preserve the world as decreed in eternal knowledge...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the Book of Nikah by discussing the linguistic and legal meanings of 'nikah' and its ruling. The author begins by stating the ruling: nikah (marriage) is sunnah (recommended) in general, but becomes wajib (obligatory) when one fears falling into sin due to strong sexual desire. It is concluded by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). Then, he delves into the linguistic debate over the word 'nikah'. Some scholars say it is a homonym (mushtarak) between intercourse and contract; others say it is literal for contract and metaphorical for intercourse; others hold the opposite (the Hanafi view). The author supports the Hanafi position that 'nikah' in its literal sense (haqiqah) means intercourse, and its use for contract is metaphorical. The practical consequence of this difference appears in the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'Do not marry women whom your fathers married' (4:22). According to the Hanafis, the prohibition includes women with whom the father had intercourse even through zina (fornication), because 'nikah' is literal for intercourse, whether halal or haram. According to the Shafi'is, who take 'nikah' as literal for contract, only women married by a valid contract are prohibited. The author then discusses the preferred view among Hanafi scholars, concluding that the reliable position (mufta bihi) is that 'nikah' is literal for intercourse and metaphorical for contract, as stated by Qadi Khan and in Fath al-Qadir. The passage also touches on the wisdom behind the legislation of marriage: to preserve the world in the best manner, as decreed in Allah's eternal knowledge.

Key Terms
النكاح (al-nikah): Linguistically debated: either literal for intercourse or contract. In Hanafi fiqh, literal for intercourse, metaphorical for contract.
الوطء (al-wat'): Sexual intercourse, including both halal and haram.
العقد (al-'aqd): The marriage contract (offer and acceptance).
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning of a word, used without metaphor.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the practical consequence of taking 'nikah' as literal for intercourse?
A) It allows marrying women who were previously married contractually to one's father.
B) It prohibits marrying women with whom one's father had intercourse, even through zina.
C) It makes nikah wajib in all circumstances.
D) It makes the marriage contract invalid without consummation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'nikah' literally means sexual intercourse, so the Quran's prohibition of marrying women 'married' by one's father extends to any illicit relationship, making the scope of prohibition broader than in the Shafi'i school.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

General Conditions for Marriage and the Mahram Dispute
Explains general conditions for marriage and an intra-Hanafi dispute on mahram marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three general conditions for a valid marriage contract according to the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a mahram marriage creates a shubha that drops the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
A valid marriage requires a legally permissible woman—but when a mahram is involved, Abu Hanifa sees a shubha; the disciples do not.

The Hanafi school meticulously defines the general conditions for a marriage contract. Among these, the legal permissibility (mahalliyya) of the woman is crucial. This lesson examines all three general conditions—capacity, freedom, and mahalliyya—and then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute: when a person marries his mahram, does the contract create a semblance (shubha) that prevents the fixed penalty for fornication? Abu Hanifa says yes; his two foremost disciples say no. The relied-upon position is that of the disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general conditions for marriage
The text lists three general conditions: capacity (ahliyya: reason and puberty), freedom (hurriya), and legal permissibility of the woman (mahalliyya). Understand how each condition applies to different parties (guardian, husband, wife, contracting agent).
Conditions are classified as general (apply to all transactions) and specific.
2
Analyze capacity and freedom
Capacity is required for the contracting party to form the contract. For a guardian, it is stricter; for a husband/wife, a minor’s marriage is valid if contracted by the guardian. Freedom is a condition for execution without permission; a slave’s marriage is suspended on the master’s consent.
Al-Zayla'i adds freedom to the general conditions.
3
Explore mahalliyya (legal permissibility of the woman)
The woman must be a human female not prohibited by a permanent cause: excludes males, hermaphrodites, female jinn, and mahrams (eternally prohibited relatives). The contract is void if the object is missing.
The passage discusses hermaphrodite and jinn cases to illustrate mahalliyya.
4
Focus on the mahram dispute
The text presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa holds that a mahram woman is still legally capable (mahall) for marriage in general, so a marriage contract creates shubha and drops hadd. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue she is not mahall relative to that specific man, so hadd applies.
Abu Hanifa’s evidence: she can marry someone else, so she has mahalliyya. The disciples: she is eternally prohibited to that man.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
According to al-Khulasa, the fatwa is on the statement of the two disciples. The practical consequence: if a person marries his mahram and consummates, hadd is applied. The contract is void.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح
النوع بالوطء على غير الوجه المشروع لكنه مستلزم للتظالم والسفك وضياع الأنساب بخلافه على الوجه المشروع.
،
وشرطه نوعان: عام في تنفيذ كل تصرف دائر بين النفع والضرر، وخاص. فالأول الأهلية بالعقل والبلوغ. قال في فتح القدير : وينبغي أن يزاد في الولي لا في الزوج والزوجة ولا في متولي العقد فإن تزويج الصغير والصغيرة جائز، وتوكيل الصبي الذي يعقل العقد ويقصده جائز في البيع عندنا فصحته هنا أولى لأنه محض سفير وأما الحرية فشرط النفاذ بلا إذن أحد ا هـ. وضم الزيلعي الحرية إلى العقل والبلوغ في الشرط العالم، والتحقيق أن التمييز شرط في متولي العقد للانعقاد أصيلاً كان أو لم يكن، فلم ينعقد النكاح بمباشرة المجنون والصبي الذي لا يقعل وأما البلوغ والحرية فشرط النفاذ في متولي العقد لنفسه لا لغيره فتوقف عقد الصبي العاقل والعبد على إجازة الولي والمولى. وأما المحلية فقال في فتح القدير : إنها من الشروط العامة وتختلف بحسب الأشياء والأحكام كمحلية المبيع للبيع والأنثى للنكاح ا هـ . والأولى أن يقال: إن محلية الأنثى المحققة من بنات آدم ليست من المحرمات . وفي العناية : محله امرأة لم يمنع من نكاحها مانع شرعي فخرج الذكر للذكر والخنثى مطلقاً والجنية للأنسى وما كان من النساء محرماً على التأبيد كالمحارم ولذا قال في التبيين من كتاب الخنثى : لو زوجه أبوه أو مولاه امرأة أو رجلاً لا يحكم بصحته حتى يتبين حاله أنه رجل أو امرأة، فإذا ظهر أنه خلاف ما زوج به تبين أن العقد كان صحيحاً وإلا فباطل لعدم مصادفة المحل، وكذا إذا زوج الخنثى من خنثى آخر لا يحكم بصحة النكاح حتى يظهر أن أحدهما ذكر والآخر أنثى ا هـ . وفي القنية : لا يجوز التزويج بجنية، وأجازه الحسن البصري بشهود، وذكر أهل الأصول أن النهي عن نكاح المحارم مجاز عن النفي فكان نسخاً لعدم محله . كثير من الفقهاء بعدم محلية المحارم للنكاح. وجزم به في غاية البيان لكن يشكل عليه إسقاط أبي حنيفة الحد عمن وطئ محرمه بعد العقد عليها فإنها إذا لم تكن محلاً لمن تبق شبهة بالعقد. والجواب أنها لم تخرج عن المحلية للنكاح أصلاً بدليل حل تزوّجها لمن لم يكن محرماً لها، فأبو حنيفة نظر إلى هذا وهما نظرا إلى خروجها عن المحلية بالنسبة إلى الواطئ وهو الظاهر فلذا في الخلاصة : إن الفتوى على قولهما وسيأتي تمامه في محله إن شاء الله تعالى.
غير هذه النسخة في عرف أهل الشرع وهم الفقهاء قوله : (فإن تزويج الصغير والصغيرة) مفرع على قوله لا في الزوج والزوجة وقوله وتوكيل الصبي الخ ) مفرع على قوله ولا في متولي العقد وكل من تزويج وتوكيل مصدر مضاف لمفعوله قوله : ( والأولى أن يقال إن محلية الأنثى) كذا فيما رأيته من النسخ بالإضافة، والظاهر أنها محرفة والأصل محليته» أو «محله» بالضمير مع التاء أو بدونها فالأنثى
خبر «أن» .
Book of Nikah: The type [of marriage] is by intercourse in an unlawful manner, but it entails oppression, bloodshed, and loss of lineage, unlike marriage in the lawful manner. Its conditions are of two types: general in the execution of every action that revolves between benefit and harm, and specific. The first [general] is capacity (ahliyya) by reason and puberty. It is stated in Fath al-Qadir: 'It should be added for the guardian, not for the husband and wife, nor for the one undertaking the contract, for marrying a minor boy and minor girl is permissible, and the agency of a boy who understands the contract and intends it is permissible in sale with us, so its validity here is even more appropriate because he is merely a messenger. As for freedom, it is a condition for execution without anyone's permission.' end quote. Al-Zayla'i added freedom to reason and puberty as a general condition. The verification is that discernment is a condition in the one undertaking the contract for its formation, whether as principal or not; thus marriage is not contracted by the direct action of the insane or a boy who does not understand. As for puberty and freedom, they are conditions for execution when the one undertaking the contract acts for himself, not for others; thus the contract of a discerning boy and a slave is suspended upon the permission of the guardian and master. As for legality (mahalliyya), it is stated in Fath al-Qadir: 'It is among the general conditions and varies according to things and rulings, like the legality of the sold item for sale and the female for marriage.' end quote. It is better to say: 'The legality of an actual female from the daughters of Adam is not from the prohibited things.' In al-Inayah: 'Its [the contract's] object is a woman not prevented from marrying her by a legal preventer.' So excluded is a male for a male, a hermaphrodite absolutely, a female jinn for a human, and women who are eternally prohibited like mahrams. Hence it is stated in al-Tabyin from the book on hermaphrodites: 'If his father or master marries him to a woman or a man, its validity is not judged until his condition becomes clear, whether he is a man or a woman. If it appears that he is contrary to what he was married as, it becomes clear that the contract was valid; otherwise it is void due to not coinciding with the object.' Similarly, if a hermaphrodite is married to another hermaphrodite, the validity of the marriage is not judged until it appears that one is a male and the other a female. end quote. In al-Qunyah: 'Marrying a female jinn is not permissible; al-Hasan al-Basri permitted it with witnesses.' The scholars of usul mentioned that the prohibition of marrying mahrams is a metaphor for negation, so it is an abrogation due to lack of its object. Many jurists hold the lack of legality of mahrams for marriage. It is asserted in Ghayat al-Bayan, but it is problematic because Abu Hanifa drops the hadd punishment from one who fornicates with his mahram after contracting marriage with her, for if she is not a valid object, how can the semblance of a contract remain? The answer is that she has not left legality for marriage absolutely, evidenced by the permissibility of marrying her for someone who is not her mahram. Abu Hanifa looked at this, while the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] looked at her being removed from legality relative to the fornicator, which is apparent. Hence in al-Khulasa: 'The fatwa is upon their statement.' Its complete discussion will come in its place, Allah willing. [Note: In another copy: 'In the custom of the people of Shariah, they are the jurists.' His saying: 'For marrying a minor boy and minor girl' is a branch of his saying 'not in the husband and wife', and his saying 'the agency of a boy' is a branch of his saying 'nor in the one undertaking the contract'. Both 'tazwij' and 'tawkil' are masdars with their objects. His saying: 'It is better to say: the legality of the female' is as I saw in copies in the genitive case; apparently it is a corruption, the original being 'mahalliyyatuhu' or 'mahalluhu' with a pronoun and ta or without, so 'al-untha' is the predicate of 'anna'.]
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Nikah, explaining the general conditions (shurut) required for a valid marriage contract according to the Hanafi school. The author classifies conditions into two categories: general and specific. The general conditions pertain to the capacity of the parties involved: they must be sane and have reached puberty. However, a distinction is made: for the guardian (wali), these conditions are stricter; for the husband and wife, the contracting party may be a minor but the contract is valid if concluded by the guardian. The passage then discusses the condition of freedom: it is a condition for execution of the contract without permission; a slave may marry but his contract is suspended upon the master's permission. The third general condition is mahalliyya—the woman must be legally permissible to marry. This excludes males, hermaphrodites, female jinn, and permanently prohibited relatives (mahrams). The text delves into the case of hermaphrodites: a marriage is not valid until their sex is determined. Regarding jinn, marrying a female jinn is not allowed. A key intra-Hanafi dispute arises concerning mahrams: Abu Hanifa holds that a mahram woman is still legally capable of marriage (mahall) to someone who is not her mahram, so if a man marries his mahram, he drops the hadd punishment due to semblance (shubha). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that she is not a valid object for the fornicator, so the marriage is void and hadd applies. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the disciples, as stated in al-Khulasa. The note at the end clarifies textual variants regarding the phrase 'mahalliyyat al-untha'.

Key Terms
محلية (mahalliyya): The quality of a woman being legally permissible for marriage to a specific man; excludes prohibited categories like mahrams, jinn, etc.
شبهة (shubha): A semblance of legality that arises from an invalid contract, which can prevent the application of fixed punishments (hadd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position regarding the legality of a mahram woman for marriage after being contracted by a mahram?
A) She is legally capable, so hadd is dropped.
B) She is not legally capable relative to that man, so hadd applies.
C) The marriage is valid if witnesses are present.
D) The marriage is valid but hadd is still applied.

Key Takeaway
The general conditions for a valid marriage are capacity, freedom, and a legally permissible woman; the mufta bihi regarding mahram marriage is that the woman is not a valid object for that man, so hadd applies.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
8 min
The Specific Condition and Pillar of Marriage
Defines the specific condition, pillar, and general ruling of marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific condition and pillar of a valid marriage and identify the general ruling of the contract.

Core Idea
Marriage is formed by proposal and acceptance witnessed by two qualified men.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the essential elements that constitute a valid marriage contract. Unlike other contracts, marriage has a special condition (shart khas) on which its validity depends. Understanding these foundational components is critical before exploring the legal effects that flow from a valid marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific condition (shart khas)
Look at the first sentence: the specific condition for formation is the hearing of two witnesses with a specific description (free, adult, Muslim, male) for the proposal and acceptance. This is external to the contract itself.
2
Note the additional condition from al-Muhit
The text adds that the woman must be legally permissible (not a mahram etc.). This is a known condition.
This condition is also a shart but not specific to the formation; it relates to the subject matter.
3
Identify the pillar (rukn)
The pillar is the proposal (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), either explicitly (past tense) or effectively (by words that stand in their place). The contracting parties are the two spouses (or their representatives).
4
Derive the general ruling (hukm)
The immediate ruling of a valid marriage is mutual permissibility of enjoyment, prohibition of affinity, and mutual rights. The text says 'this will come to you' indicating detail follows.
This general hukm is the foundation for the detailed effects.
5
Prepare for the detailed effects
Recognize that the passage then transitions to detailed rulings from al-Bada'i, which will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح
۱۳۹
أعني الشرط الخاص للانعقاد - سماع اثنين بوصف خاص للإيجاب والقبول. زاد في المحيط وكون المرأة من المحللات وقد علمت ما فيه وركنه الإيجاب والقبول حقيقة أو حكماً كاللفظ القائم مقامهما من متولي الطرفين شرعاً. وحكمه حل استمتاع كل منهما بالآخر على الوجه المأذون فيه شرعاً، وحرمة المصاهرة وملك كل واحد منهما بعض الأشياء على الآخر سيرد عليك. كذا في فتح القدير .
مما
وقد ذكر أحكامه في البدائع في فصل على حدة فقال : منها حل الوطء لا في الحيض والنفاس والإحرام وفي الظهار قبل التكفير. ووجوبه قضاء مرة واحدة وديانة فيما زاد عليها، وقيل يجب قضاء أيضاً. ومنها حل النظر والمس من رأسها إلى قدمها إلا لمانع، ومنها ملك المنفعة وهو اختصاص الزوج بمنافع بضعها وسائر أعضائها استمتاعاً، ومنها ملك الحبس والقيد وهو صيرورتها ممنوعة عن الخروج والبروز ، ومنها وجوب المهر عليه، ومنها النفقة والكسوة ومنها حرمة المصاهرة، ومنها الإرث من الجانبين، ومنها وجوب وجوب النساء في حقوقهن، ومنها وجوب طاعته عليها إذا دعاها إلى الفراش، ومنها بين
العدل
ولاية تأديبها إذا لم تطعه بأن نشزت ، ومنها استحباب معاشرتها بالمعروف وعليه حمل الأمر في قوله تعالى وعاشروهن بالمعروف [النساء : ١٩] وهو مستحب لها أيضاً والمعاشرة بالمعروف الإحسان قولاً وفعلاً وخلقاً إلى آخر ما في البدائع.
Book of Nikah 139: I mean the specific condition for formation: the hearing of two [witnesses] with a specific description for the proposal and acceptance. It is added in al-Muhit: 'And the woman being among the permissible ones; you have already known what is in it.' Its pillar is the proposal and acceptance, literally or effectively, like the words that stand in their place from the one undertaking the contract from both sides according to the Shariah. Its ruling is the permissibility of each one's enjoyment of the other in the legally permitted manner, the prohibition of affinity, and each one's ownership of some things over the other; this will come to you. So in Fath al-Qadir. 
And he has mentioned its rulings in al-Bada'i in a separate chapter: Among them: the permissibility of intercourse except during menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and ihram, and during zihar before expiation. Its obligation is judicially once, and in conscience for what is more than that; and it is said it is also judicially obligatory. Among them: the permissibility of looking and touching from her head to her feet except for a preventer. Among them: ownership of the benefit, which is the husband's exclusive right to the benefits of her vagina and all her body parts for enjoyment. Among them: ownership of confinement and restriction, which is her being prevented from going out and appearing. Among them: the obligation of the dower upon him. Among them: maintenance and clothing. Among them: the prohibition of affinity. Among them: inheritance from both sides. Among them: obligatory matters for women regarding their rights. Among them: the obligation of her obedience to him when he calls her to bed. Among them: the authority to discipline her if she does not obey him by being nushuz. Among them: recommendation to treat her kindly, and upon this is understood the command in His saying: 'and live with them honourably' [4:19]; and it is also recommended for her. And living honourably is kindness in word, deed, and character up to the end of what is in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Nikah in al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the specific condition (shart khas) for the formation of marriage: the presence of two witnesses who hear the proposal and acceptance with specific attributes (free, adult, Muslim, etc.). The pillar (rukn) of marriage is the proposal and acceptance, whether explicit or implied by words that substitute for them, from the contracting parties legally. The overall ruling (hukm) is mutual permissibility of enjoyment within legal bounds, prohibition of affinity (i.e., marriage to relatives of the spouse), and certain rights like ownership of benefits and dower. The passage then quotes from al-Bada'i a comprehensive list of the rulings (ahkam) of marriage: permissibility of intercourse (except during menstruation, postpartum, ihram, and during zihar before expiation); obligation of intercourse at least once (judicially) and more as a religious duty; permissibility of seeing and touching the spouse; husband's ownership of the benefit of the wife's private parts and body; wife's restriction from leaving home; obligation of dower and maintenance; prohibition of affinity; inheritance between spouses; wife's obedience; husband's right to discipline for nushuz; and recommendation of kind treatment based on Quran 4:19. This list encapsulates the legal effects of a valid marriage contract.

Key Terms
صحيح (sahih): A marriage that fulfills all conditions and pillars, producing full legal effects
شرط خاص (shart khas): A condition specific to the formation of a particular contract, here the requirement of two witnesses for marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the specific condition (shart khas) for the formation of marriage?
A) The presence of the woman's guardian
B) Two witnesses hearing the proposal and acceptance
C) The payment of dower
D) The husband's ability to provide maintenance

Key Takeaway
A valid marriage requires two witnesses to hear the proposal and acceptance; these are external conditions for formation, while the pillar is the spoken exchange itself.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
The Legal Effects (Ahkam) of a Valid Marriage
Enumerates the detailed rulings and effects of a valid marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the key legal effects of a valid Hanafi marriage and distinguish between obligations, prohibitions, and recommendations.

Core Idea
A valid marriage creates a network of rights and duties: mutual enjoyment, financial obligations, inheritance, and mutual respect.

The marriage contract is not just a religious act but a legal bond with concrete consequences. The great Hanafi jurist al-Kasani in al-Bada'i enumerates a comprehensive list of these effects. This lesson unpacks that list, showing how each ruling derives from the nature of marriage as a contract of mutual rights.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the first set: permissibility and obligation of intercourse
The passage lists: intercourse is permissible except in prohibited times (menstruation, postpartum, ihram, zihar before expiation). Its obligation is judicial once; more is recommended (religiously encouraged).
This distinguishes legal obligation (judicial) from religious duty (qada'an/diyanatan).
2
Identify rights related to body and movement
Husband has the right to look and touch the wife's entire body (except for a Shar'i preventer). This is called milk al-manfa'ah (ownership of benefit). Also, the wife is restricted from leaving home (milk al-habs wa al-qayd) – she requires her husband's permission.
Milk al-manfa'ah is a hallmark of the Hanafi conception of marriage.
3
List financial obligations: dower and maintenance
The husband must pay the dower (mahr) and provide maintenance (nafaqah) including food, clothing, and shelter.
These are obligations upon the husband, not the wife.
4
Note legal and relational consequences: marriage prohibitions and inheritance
The marriage creates prohibition of affinity (hurmat al-musaharah) – relatives of the spouse become mahram. Also, mutual inheritance rights are established.
5
Understand obligations of obedience and discipline
The wife must obey the husband when called to bed (she cannot refuse without valid excuse). If she is nushuz (disobedient), the husband has authority to discipline (within limits). Finally, kind treatment (mu'ashara bil ma'ruf) is recommended for both, based on Quran 4:19.
Discipline is not for trivial matters; it is a graded process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
ملك المنفعة (milk al-manfa'ah): The husband's exclusive right to enjoy the wife's body including intercourse and other forms of intimacy
ملك الحبس والقيد (milk al-habs wa al-qayd): The husband's right to confine his wife to the home and restrict her movement.
نشوز (nushuz): The wife's disobedience to her husband's legitimate commands, particularly regarding marital duties.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on looking and touching the wife's entire body?
A) It is prohibited except for necessity
B) It is permissible except for a preventer (la'i mani)
C) It is permissible only in private
D) It is obligatory for intimacy

Key Takeaway
A valid marriage yields a comprehensive set of legal consequences including physical intimacy rights, financial duties, kinship prohibitions, inheritance, and mutual obligations of obedience and kind treatment.

Nikah — Marriage

Definition, Condition, and Pillar of Marriage in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines marriage contract and its essential components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the condition (shart) and pillar (rukn) of the marriage contract and state the legal effects (hukm) of a valid marriage according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The pillar of marriage is offer and acceptance; witnesses are a condition, not a pillar.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author defines the marriage contract by specifying its essential components: the condition for validity (shart al-in‘iqad), the pillar (rukn), and the legal effects (hukm). Understanding this tripartite structure is foundational for all subsequent rulings on marriage. We will break down each element as per the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shart
The condition for validity is the hearing of two witnesses (or one man and two women) for the offer and acceptance.
The passage specifies 'hearing of two' with a specific description for the offer and acceptance.
2
Identify the rukn
The pillar is the offer and acceptance itself, whether explicitly or effectively, from the legally authorized parties.
The passage states: 'Its pillar is the offer and acceptance, literally or effectively, like the phrase that stands in their place.'
3
Identify the hukm
The legal effects include permissibility of enjoyment, prohibition of affinity, and mutual rights.
The passage lists three effects: permissibility of enjoyment, prohibition of affinity, and ownership of certain rights over the other.
4
Distinguish shart from rukn
A missing shart makes the contract invalid (batil); a missing rukn means the contract never existed. The shart is an external requirement, while the rukn is the core of the contract.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh; other schools may classify witnesses differently.
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
Shafi'is consider witnesses a pillar (rukn), while Hanafis consider them a condition (shart). This affects rulings on witness substitution.
Hanafis allow one male and two female witnesses, as implied by the passage 'hearing of two' which can include women.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح
أعني الشرط الخاص للانعقاد - سماع اثنين بوصف خاص للإيجاب والقبول. زاد في المحيط وكون المرأة من المحللات وقد علمت ما فيه وركنه الإيجاب والقبول حقيقة أو حكماً كاللفظ القائم مقامهما من متولي الطرفين شرعاً. وحكمه حل استمتاع كل منهما بالآخر على الوجه المأذون فيه شرعاً، وحرمة المصاهرة وملك كل واحد منهما بعض الأشياء على الآخر سيرد عليك. كذا في فتح القدير .
Book of Marriage. I mean the specific condition for validity: hearing of two [witnesses] with a specific description for the offer and acceptance. Al-Muhit added: and that the woman be from those who are permissible [for marriage], and you have known what is in it. Its pillar is the offer and acceptance, literally or effectively, like the phrase that stands in their place from the legal administrator of both parties. Its ruling is the permissibility of enjoyment of each other in the manner permitted legally, and the prohibition of affinity, and the ownership of each one of some things over the other, as will come to you. Likewise in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a definition and essential components of the marriage contract from the commentary. The author specifies the condition for validity (shart) as the hearing of two male witnesses (or one male and two females) with the specific description required for offer and acceptance, as noted in al-Muhit. The pillar (rukn) is the offer and acceptance itself, either explicitly or effectively through a phrase that stands in their place from the person legally authorized to contract. The ruling (hukm) of a valid marriage is the permissibility of sexual enjoyment for each spouse, the establishment of affinity prohibitions, and mutual ownership of certain rights as detailed later.

Key Terms
شرط الانعقاد (shart al-in‘iqad): Condition for the validity of the marriage contract; without it the contract does not take effect.
ركن النكاح (rukn al-nikah): The essential element that constitutes the marriage contract: offer and acceptance.
حكم النكاح (hukm al-nikah): The legal effect of a valid marriage: permissibility of enjoyment, affinity prohibition, mutual rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the pillar (rukn) of marriage according to the Hanafi school?
A) Witnesses
B) Offer and acceptance
C) Wali
D) Mahr

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the marriage contract's pillar is the offer and acceptance; witnesses are a condition for validity, and the contract's effects include permissibility of enjoyment, affinity prohibition, and mutual rights.

The Ten Rulings of Marriage in the Hanafi School
List of rulings from Bada'i' al-Sana'i'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten major effects of a valid Hanafi marriage and explain the concepts of milk al-manfa‘ah and milk al-habs.

Core Idea
A valid marriage establishes mutual rights: intimacy, financial support, confinement, and kind companionship.

This lesson presents a compact list of the legal consequences (ahkam) of a valid marriage from Imam al-Kasani's Bada'i‘ al-Sana'i‘. These are not conditions or pillars but the rights and obligations that arise once the marriage is properly concluded. Understanding this list helps spouses know their duties and avoids common marital disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the physical intimacy rulings
Locate the rulings on intercourse (halal with exceptions, obligation once), looking and touching (halal except preventer), and the concepts of milk al-manfa‘ah and milk al-habs.
These rulings establish the husband's exclusive right and the wife's confinement.
2
List the financial obligations
Find the obligations of dower (mahr), maintenance (nafaqah), and clothing (kiswah) placed on the husband.
These are rights of the wife, not gifts.
3
Note the social and legal effects
Identify inheritance from both sides, prohibition of affinity (musaharah), and the wife's right to be treated kindly (mu‘asharah bi al-ma‘ruf).
These effects extend beyond the couple.
4
Understand obedience and discipline
Recognize the wife's obligation to answer when called to bed and the husband's right to discipline for nushuz (rebellion), but only lightly.
Obedience is limited to marital rights; discipline is not arbitrary.
5
Distinguish obligation from recommendation
Note that companionship with kindness is recommended (mustahab) for both spouses, not wajib, though the verse is amr (imperative) indicating recommendation here.
This is a Hanafi interpretation based on usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما وقد ذكر أحكامه في البدائع في فصل على حدة فقال : منها حل الوطء لا في الحيض والنفاس والإحرام وفي الظهار قبل التكفير. ووجوبه قضاء مرة واحدة وديانة فيما زاد عليها، وقيل يجب قضاء أيضاً. ومنها حل النظر والمس من رأسها إلى قدمها إلا لمانع، ومنها ملك المنفعة وهو اختصاص الزوج بمنافع بضعها وسائر أعضائها استمتاعاً، ومنها ملك الحبس والقيد وهو صيرورتها ممنوعة عن الخروج والبروز ، ومنها وجوب المهر عليه، ومنها النفقة والكسوة ومنها حرمة المصاهرة، ومنها الإرث من الجانبين، ومنها وجوب وجوب النساء في حقوقهن، ومنها وجوب طاعته عليها إذا دعاها إلى الفراش، ومنها ولاية تأديبها إذا لم تطعه بأن نشزت ، ومنها استحباب معاشرتها بالمعروف وعليه حمل الأمر في قوله تعالى وعاشروهن بالمعروف [النساء : ١٩] وهو مستحب لها أيضاً والمعاشرة بالمعروف الإحسان قولاً وفعلاً وخلقاً إلى آخر ما في البدائع.
From among [the rulings]: And the author of Bada'i' mentioned its rulings in a separate section and said: Among them is the permissibility of intercourse except during menstruation, postpartum, ihram, and in dhihar before expiation. And its obligation [of intercourse] once as a qada (judicial order) and in terms of religious duty (diyanatan) regarding more than that, and it is said it is obligatory as qada also. Among them is the permissibility of looking and touching from her head to her foot except for a preventer. Among them is the ownership of usufruct (manfa‘ah), which is the husband's exclusive right to the benefit of her vagina and all her limbs for enjoyment. Among them is the ownership of confinement (habs) and restraint (qayd), which is her becoming prohibited from going out and appearing. Among them is the obligation of dower upon him. Among them is maintenance and clothing. Among them is the prohibition of affinity. Among them is inheritance from both sides. Among them is the obligation of the rights of women. Among them is the obligation of her obedience when he calls her to bed. Among them is his authority to discipline her if she does not obey by being rebellious (nushuz). Among them is the recommendation of companionship with kindness, and upon this is based the command in His statement: 'And live with them with kindness' [4:19], and it is recommended for her as well. And companionship with kindness is beneficence in speech, action, and character – to the end of what is in Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the various legal rulings (ahkam) of marriage as collected from Imam al-Kasani's Bada'i‘ al-Sana'i‘, listed in a separate chapter. The rulings cover physical intimacy, rights, and obligations. Key points: Intercourse is permissible but prohibited during menstruation, postpartum bleeding, ihram, and before expiation of dhihar. It is considered obligatory once as a judicial matter and in religious duty for additional times. The husband gains exclusive right to enjoy his wife's body, and she is confined to prevent her from going out without permission. Dower (mahr), maintenance (nafaqah), inheritance, and the wife's obedience are all established. The husband has authority to discipline a rebellious wife lightly. Companionship with kindness is recommended (mustahab) based on Quran 4:19.

Key Terms
ملك المنفعة (milk al-manfa‘ah): The husband's exclusive right to the benefit of his wife's body for sexual enjoyment.
ملك الحبس (milk al-habs): The wife's obligation to remain in the marital home and not go out without husband's permission.
حل النظر والمس (hall al-nazar wa al-mass): Permissibility for the husband to see and touch his wife's entire body except for a valid preventer (e.g., menses).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ownership of usufruct
B) Prohibition of affinity
C) Obligation of zakat on the wife
D) Husband's right to discipline

Key Takeaway
A valid Hanafi marriage creates specific, enumerated rights and obligations between spouses, centering on intimacy, financial support, and kind treatment.

The Ruling on Suspending Marriage with Conditions (Ta'liq)
Expansion of Hanafi rule on suspensive conditions in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'liq in marriage, identify exceptions based on istihsan and qiyas, and apply the distinction between a binding offer and a conditional gamble.

Core Idea
Marriage contracts cannot be suspended; conditions attached are void but the marriage remains valid.

The Hanafi school holds that marriage, being a contract of immediate effect, cannot be made contingent on a future uncertain event (ta'liq). However, if the condition relates to a present state that can be verified instantly, the marriage may be valid by istihsan. This lesson unpacks the general rule, examines the analogy with salam contracts, and explores various scenarios where conditional consent is given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize that the passage begins with the principle: 'And from its rulings is that its suspension (ta'liq) by condition is not valid.' This is the core Hanafi rule.
Marriage requires immediate unconditional consent.
2
Examine the exceptions using istihsan
The passage presents cases where the condition is about a present state (e.g., 'if my father is pleased' while father is present) and rules it valid by istihsan. Note the condition 'today' and presence of the person.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in these cases.
3
Analyze the qiyas with salam
The text draws an analogy: invalidating a will (mashi'a) in the session is like invalidating an option (khiyar) in a salam contract. Both removal of the contingency makes the contract valid.
Qiyas jaliy connects marriage to salam.
4
Distinguish between binding and non-binding conditions
The passage contrasts a conditional offer that is a gamble (mukhatara) with an option imposed after an already binding contract. The former invalidates the marriage; the latter leaves the contract valid with a void condition.
A gamble condition makes the marriage invalid.
5
Apply to time-based conditions
Cases like 'after the expiry of my iddah' or adding a time limit are also invalid because they suspend the contract on an uncertain future event.
Time-based ta'liq is generally void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحكامه أن لا تعليقه يصح بالشرط لكن قال في التتمة : تزوج امرأة إن شاءت أو قال إن شاء زيد فأبطل صاحب المشيئة مشيئته في المجلس فالنكاح جائز لأن المشيئة، إذا بطلت في المجلس صار نكاحاً بغير مشيئة كما قالوا في السلم إذا أبطل الخيار في المجلس جاز السلم، ولو بدأ الزوج فقال تزوجتك إن شئت ثم قبلت المرأة من غير شرط تم النكاح ولا يحتاج إلى إبطال المشيئة بعد ذلك، ولو قال تزوجتك بألف درهم إن رضي فلان اليوم ؛ فإن كان فلان حاضراً فقال قد رضيت جاز النكاح استحساناً، وإن كان غير حاضر لم يجز، وليس هذا كقوله قد تزوجتك ولفلان الرضا لأن هذا قول قد وجب وشرط خيار، والأول لم يوجب وجعل الإيجاب مخاطرة. ولو قال تزوجتك اليوم على أن لك المشيئة اليوم إلى الليل فالنكاح جائز والشرط باطل كشرط الخيار ا هـ. هكذا في البزازية لكن قال قبله : لو قالت زوجت نفسي منك إن رضي أبي لا يصح لأنه علقه بالخطر ا.هـ. وقياس ما تقدم أن الأب إن كان حاضراً في المجلس ورضي الجواز . ثم رأيته في الظهيرية وفي البزازية خطب بنت رجل لابنه فقال أبوها زوجتها قبلك من فلان فكذبه أبو الابن فقال إن لم أكن زوجتها من فلان فقد زوجتها من ابنك وقبل أبو الابن ثم علم كذبه انعقد لأن التعليق بالوجود تحقيق ا هـ . وفي المجتبى : زوجت نفسي منك بعد انقضاء عدتي فقبل لا يصح كالتعليق وإضافته إلى وقت لا يصح
And from its rulings is that its suspension (ta'liq) by condition is not valid. But in al-Tatimma he said: If a man marries a woman saying 'if she wills' or said 'if Zaid wills', then the one with the will invalidates his will in the session, the marriage is valid because when the will is invalidated in the session it becomes a marriage without will, as they said in salam (forward sale): if the option is invalidated in the session, the salam is valid. If the husband begins and says 'I marry you if you will', then the woman accepts without condition, the marriage is complete and there is no need to invalidate the will afterwards. If he says 'I marry you for one thousand dirhams if so-and-so is pleased today', and so-and-so is present and says 'I am pleased', the marriage is valid by istihsan, and if he is absent it is not valid. This is not like saying 'I have married you and so-and-so has the right of approval' because this is a statement that is already binding and stipulates an option, while the first is not binding and makes the offer a gamble. If he says 'I marry you today on condition that you have the will today until night', the marriage is valid and the condition is void, like the condition of option. End quote. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya, but before it he said: If a woman says 'I have married myself to you if my father is pleased', it is not valid because she suspended it on a risk. End quote. By analogy to what preceded, if the father is present in the session and pleased, it is permissible. Then I saw in al-Zahiriya and in al-Bazzaziyya: A man proposed to the daughter of a man for his son, and her father said 'I married her to you before from so-and-so', the son's father denied it, so he said 'If I have not married her to so-and-so, then I have married her to your son', and the son's father accepted, then he knew he lied, it is valid because suspension on existence is a realization. End quote. And in al-Mujtaba: 'I married myself to you after the expiry of my iddah', and he accepted, it is not valid like suspension, and adding it to a time is not valid.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling that marriage cannot be suspended on a condition (ta'liq). However, multiple cases are discussed. The general rule: a marriage contract with a suspensive condition is invalid because marriage requires immediate and unconditional consent. However, there are exceptions: if the condition is about a present state (e.g., 'if my father is pleased' and he is present and agrees), it can be valid by istihsan. The text distinguishes between a condition that makes the offer a gamble (mukhatara) and a condition that is an additional option after the contract is already binding. A condition of option (khiyar) in marriage is void because marriage is a contract that does not allow option, but the marriage itself remains valid. The analogy to salam (salam) is used: invalidating an option in the session preserves the contract. The cases cover various scenarios: conditional on will (mashi'a), conditional on a third party's pleasure, and conditional on a future event like iddah expiry.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the effect of a contract on a future uncertain event.
مشيئة (mashi'a): Will or option; here a condition that the marriage depends on someone's will.
مخاطرة (mukhatara): A gamble or risk; used here to describe an offer that is not binding because it is conditional on an uncertain event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a woman says 'I marry myself to you if my father is pleased' and the father is absent, what is the ruling?
A) Valid if father later agrees
B) Invalid because it is suspended on a risk
C) Valid because the condition is about a present state
D) The marriage is suspended until father agrees

Key Takeaway
Marriage in the Hanafi school requires immediate unconditional consent; any suspensive condition is void while the marriage remains valid, except when the condition is about a present state verifiable in the session.

Nikah — Marriage

6 min
Legal Status of Marriage: Fard, Wajib, Sunnah, Haram, Makruh, Mubah
Classifies marriage's legal obligation based on individual circumstances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the legal status of marriage for a given individual based on their circumstances and apply the two conditions of ability and fear of injustice.

Core Idea
Marriage is not a fixed ruling—its legal status depends on whether it prevents sin or causes injustice.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the Hanafi classification of marriage. Unlike other schools that often consider marriage simply recommended, the Hanafi school determines its ruling based on the individual’s ability, desire, and risk of sin. Understanding this classification allows you to apply fiqh to real-life situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
Recognize that marriage can be fard, wajib, sunnah, haram, makruh, or mubah. Each corresponds to a specific situation.
The text lists these six categories: الفرض, الوجوب, السنة, الحرمة, الكراهة, الإباحة.
2
Understand the trigger for fard
Marriage becomes fard when a person fears falling into zina if they do not marry, and there is no other lawful way to avoid it. The principle: 'ما لا يتوصل إلى ترك الحرام إلا به يكون فرضاً'.
This is the most stringent level of obligation.
3
Distinguish fard from wajib
If the fear of zina is present but not to the extreme that marriage is the only means of avoidance, then marriage is wajib. The text reconciles different scholarly expressions by noting this nuance.
This reflects the Hanafi distinction between fard (qat'i) and wajib (zanni).
4
Apply the two conditions for fard/wajib
For marriage to be fard or wajib, two conditions must be met: (1) ownership of mahr and nafaqa (ability to provide), and (2) absence of fear of injustice (jawr). If either is missing, the ruling changes.
Citing Bada'i': 'لَيْسَ مَنْ خَافَهُ إِذَا كَانَ عَاجِزًا عَنْهُمَا آثِمًا'.
5
Resolve conflict of fears
If the fear of falling into zina conflicts with the fear of injustice in marriage, the fear of injustice is given precedence. This is because injustice violates a servant's right, while zina is a right of Allah, and the servant's right takes priority.
Quote: 'فإن تعارض خوف الوقوع في الزنا وخوف الجور قدم الثاني'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفته فرض وواجب وسنة وحرام ومكروه ومباح. أما الأول فبأن يخاف الوقوع في الزنا لو لم يتزوج بحيث لا يمكنه الاحتراز عنه إلا به لأن ما لا يتوصل إلى ترك الحرام إلا به يكون فرضاً . وأما الثاني فبأن يخافه لا بالحيثية المذكورة إذ ليس الخوف مطلقاً مستلزما بلوغه إلى عدم التمكن. وبه يحصل التوفيق بين قول من عبر بالافتراض وبين من عبر بالوجوب، وكل من هذين القسمين مشروط بشرطين : الأول ملك المهر والنفقة فليس من خافه إذا كان عاجزاً عنهما آثماً . كما في البدائع الثاني عدم خوف الجور فإن تعارض خوف الوقوع في الزنا لو لم يتزوج وخوف الجور لو تزوج قدم الثاني فلا افتراض بل مكروه كما أفاده في فتح القدير. ولعله لأن الجور معصية متعلقة بالعباد والمنع من الزنا من حقوق الله تعالى، وحق العبد مقدم عند التعارض لاحتياجه وغنى المولى تعالى. وأما الثالث فعند الاعتدال وسيأتي بيانه. وأما الرابع فبأن يخاف الجور بحيث لا يمكنه الاحتراز عنه لأنه إنما شرع لمصلحة من تحصين النفس وتحصيل الثواب وبالجور يأثم ويرتكب المحرمات فتنعدم المصالح لرجحان هذه المفاسد. وأما الخامس فبأن يخافه لا بالحيثية المذكورة وهي كراهة تحريم ومن أطلق الكراهة عند خوف الجور فراده القسم الثاني من القسمين وأما السادس فبأن يخاف العجز عن الإيفاء بمواجبه . في المجتبى يعني في المستقبل. وأما محاسنه فكثيرة ودلائله شهيرة.
And its description: fard, wajib, sunnah, haram, makruh, mubah. As for the first: it is that he fears falling into zina if he does not marry, such that he cannot avoid it except by marrying, because that by which avoidance of the forbidden is reached becomes fard. As for the second: he fears it [zina] but not to the extent mentioned, because fear absolutely does not necessitate reaching the point of inability [to avoid]. And by this is achieved reconciliation between the one who called it fard and the one who called it wajib. And each of these two categories is conditioned by two conditions: first, ownership of dower and maintenance, so one who fears [zina] but is unable to pay them is not sinful; as in Bada'i‘. Second, absence of fear of injustice (jawr). If the fear of falling into zina if he does not marry and the fear of injustice if he marries conflict, the second is given precedence, so it is not fard but rather makruh, as stated in Fath al-Qadir. Perhaps because injustice is a sin related to the rights of the servants, while prevention of zina is from the rights of Allah, and the right of the servant takes precedence in case of conflict due to his need and the self-sufficiency of Allah. As for the third [sunnah]: it is when there is moderation, and its explanation will come. As for the fourth [haram]: it is when he fears injustice such that he cannot avoid it, because marriage was legislated for the benefit of protecting oneself and attaining reward, and with injustice he sins and commits prohibitions, so the benefits cease due to the preponderance of these harms. As for the fifth [makruh]: it is when he fears it [injustice] not to the mentioned extent, and it is makruh tahrimi, and whoever uses absolute karahah when fearing injustice means the second of the two categories. As for the sixth [mubah]: it is when he fears inability to fulfill its obligations; in al-Mujtaba: meaning in the future. And its merits are many and its evidences are well-known.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) that categorizes the legal status (hukm taklifi) of marriage according to individual circumstances. The Hanafi school classifies marriage into five categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, haram, makruh, and mubah. Each depends on the person's ability and fear of sin. Marriage becomes fard (obligatory) for someone who fears falling into zina and can only avoid it by marrying, and has the means (mahr and nafaqa) and does not fear injustice (jawr) such as oppression. If the fear of zina is not extreme (only possible), it is wajib (necessary). If one fears injustice, marriage becomes haram (because injustice harms others and is a greater sin). Makruh applies when there is some fear of injustice but not extreme. Mubah when there is no strong reason. Sunnah is the default for those with moderate desire. The text reconciles different expressions (fard vs wajib) by noting that fard is when avoidance of haram is only possible through marriage, while wajib is when it is not the only way. The priority of servant's rights over Allah's rights in case of conflict is a key Hanafi principle.

Key Terms
الجور (jawr): Injustice or oppression; here specifically the husband's fear that he will treat his wife unjustly.
حصول التوفيق بين القولين (husul al-tawfiq bayna al-qawlayn): Reconciliation between those who say fard and those who say wajib; both are valid for different levels.
ملك المهر والنفقة (milk al-mahr wa al-nafaqa): Ownership/ability to provide dower and maintenance; a condition for marriage to be fard/wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which takes precedence if there is a conflict between fear of zina and fear of injustice (jawr) in marriage?
A) Fear of zina
B) Fear of injustice
C) They are equal
D) It depends on the individual

Key Takeaway
Marriage’s ruling is not fixed; it depends on whether it prevents greater evil or causes harm, with the right of the servant prioritized over the right of Allah.

The Hanafi Refinement of 'Aqd in the Definition of Marriage
Refines the definition of marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi jurists define marriage using the verbal noun 'aqd (masdar) and distinguish it from other meanings.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage is the act of contracting for ownership of enjoyment, not the contract document.

The definition of marriage in the Hanafi school is precise. In Kanz al-Daqā'iq, it is stated: 'It is a contract that pertains to the ownership of enjoyment intentionally.' This commentary clarifies that the word 'contract' (al-‘aqd) refers to the verbal noun, meaning the act of contracting, to avoid confusion. Understanding this refinement is essential for grasping the Hanafi legal concept of nikah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition in the matn
Locate the core definition: 'It is a contract that pertains to the ownership of enjoyment intentionally.' Note the term 'aqd.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqā'iq.
2
Recognize the potential ambiguity in 'aqd
The word 'aqd can mean the act of contracting (masdar), the contract document, or the legal consequence. The commentator warns against confusion.
Arabic has multiple meanings for 'aqd.
3
Understand the commentator's refinement
The commentator explicitly states: 'What is meant by contract is the verbal noun (masdar) derived from contract.' This disambiguates the definition.
This is a critical refinement for precision.
4
Apply the refined definition
Now you know that 'nikah is aqd' means the act of offer and acceptance, not the written document. This affects rulings on validity.
For example, a written document alone is insufficient without oral exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو عقد يرد على ملك المتعة قصداً أي النكاح عند الفقهاء. والمراد بالعقد الحاصل بالمصدر احترازاً عن المعنى
His statement: 'It is a contract that pertains to the ownership of enjoyment intentionally.' This is marriage according to the jurists. And what is meant by 'contract' (al-aqd) is the verbal noun (masdar) derived from 'contract', as a precaution against the other meaning.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) that refines the definition of marriage from the matn of Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The definition states: 'It is a contract that pertains to the ownership of enjoyment intentionally.' The commentator notes that the word 'aqd (contract) here refers to the masdar (verbal noun) meaning the action of contracting, not the actual contract itself. This is to avoid confusion with the other meaning of 'aqd as the object or the agreement. The definition is specifically for marriage (nikāh) according to the Hanafi jurists.

Key Terms
العقد الحاصل بالمصدر (al-‘aqd al-hāsil bi al-masdar): The contract as a verbal noun (masdar) – the act of contracting.
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut‘ah): Ownership of sexual enjoyment; the object of the marriage contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of marriage distinguishes the act of contracting from the written document, emphasizing the linguistic precision of 'aqd as masdar.

Nikah — Marriage

Definition of Nikah and Nature of Ownership of Pleasure
Defines nikah and explains nature of ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of nikah as a contract aiming at ownership of pleasure and distinguish the Hanafi position on the nature of that ownership from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Nikah is a contract that establishes ownership of pleasure (milk al-mut'a), akin to ownership of substance in legal effect.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of the marriage contract (nikah) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We will explore what 'ownership of pleasure' means and why Hanafi jurists consider it like ownership of substance (milk al-ayn) for the purpose of permitting sexual relations, while the Shafi'is consider it only ownership of usufruct (milk al-manfa'ah). The difference is subtle but reveals deeper principles about the nature of marital rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of nikah
Locate the matn's definition: 'it is a contract that aims at ownership of pleasure.' Understand each component: 'contract' (offer and acceptance), 'aims at' (establishes legally), 'ownership of pleasure' (right to sexual enjoyment).
2
Analyze the term 'ownership' (milk)
The passage clarifies that 'ownership' here does not mean real ownership like property but rather permissibility (halal) to have intercourse. However, the Hanafi school argues that this ownership is legally treated like ownership of substance for the purpose of permitting all parts and benefits.
Note the distinction: it's not real ownership but has similar effect in one area.
3
Follow the qiyas proof
The Hanafis use qiyas on the validity of marrying a young girl (mawdi'a) who cannot yet enjoy intercourse. If marriage only gave usufruct rights, such a marriage would be invalid. Since it is valid, the contract must give something more akin to ownership of substance.
The illa: validity does not depend on actual ability to enjoy.
4
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute
Compare the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i view that it is only milk al-manfa'ah. Note the practical difference (thamarah): if the wife is violated by a third party, compensation goes to her, not the husband, because ownership is not real.
The difference is largely verbal; both agree the husband has exclusive right to sexual enjoyment.

This is the meaning of al-Bahr's speech, even though al-Dabusi's speech contradicts the statement of the text 'aims at ownership of pleasure', because its implication is that there is no difference between us and al-Shafi'i in that. So he owns the dower of his slave-girl due to his ownership of her vagina's substance, and he does not own the dower ... end. Has been mentioned, and in it.

Commentary
This passage defines the marriage contract (nikah) according to the Hanafi school. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that marriage is 'a contract that aims at ownership of pleasure (mamlakat al-mut'a).' The commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains each term. 'Contract' (a'qd) is the offer and acceptance, using the famous words 'zawwajtu' and 'tazawwajtu' or other valid formulations. The author distinguishes this definition from that of al-Warshaki, who defined the contract as the meaning that changes the situation, rather than the words. 'Ownership of pleasure' (milk al-mut'a) means ownership of benefit and sexual intercourse. The contract's aim is to establish this ownership legally. The word 'ownership' here means permissibility (halal), not actual ownership like owning property. The passage then delves into a dispute among the early scholars: Is this ownership like ownership of the substance (milk al-ayn) or of usufruct (milk al-manfa'ah)? The Hanafis, following Abu Hanifa, say it is like ownership of the substance in legal effect because it permits sexual relations and includes all parts and benefits of the wife. They prove this by the validity of marrying a young girl who cannot yet be enjoyed. The Shafi'is hold it is only ownership of usufruct. The author notes that the difference is largely verbal because both schools agree on the practical effects. The passage ends by criticizing the view that ownership here is merely permissibility, arguing that the husband owns the benefit in reality, and the compensation for violation (dower) would be to the husband if he owned the substance, but in fact it goes to the wife because ownership is not real.

Key Terms
ملك المتعة (mamlakat al-mut'a): Ownership of pleasure; the right to enjoy sexual relations with the wife, which is the aim of the marriage contract.
العقد (al-‘aqd): The contract of marriage, consisting of offer and acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ownership of the wife's substance (milk al-ayn)
B) Ownership of her usufruct only (milk al-manfa'ah)
C) Ownership of pleasure (milk al-mut'a) which is like ownership of substance in effect
D) No ownership, only permissibility

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of nikah as a contract aiming at ownership of pleasure establishes a unique form of ownership that is akin to ownership of substance in legal effect for marital rights, but not real ownership, protecting the wife's independent legal personality.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
Definition of Nikah and Milk al-Mut'ah
Defines the marriage contract and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the marriage contract and explain the meaning and legal effect of milk al-mut'ah.

Core Idea
Nikah is a combination of offer and acceptance, granting ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), not ownership of the wife's person.

Before we can derive rulings on marriage, we must first understand what the contract is. This passage from Bahar al-Raiq defines nikah and its central legal effect, milk al-mut'ah. The definition is not merely academic—it shapes every legal consequence that follows, from spousal rights to the permissibility of intimacy. Let's unpack the terms carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Find the core definition: nikah is 'the combination of the offer of one speaker and the acceptance of the other'. Note that the offer and acceptance can be stated using the well-known words 'zawwajtu' and 'tazawwajtu' or other expressions.
The definition emphasizes the contractual nature of marriage in Hanafi law.
2
Understand Legal Effect – Milk al-Mut'ah
The passage explains that the contract's legal effect is milk al-mut'ah, which is 'ownership of benefit and intercourse'. This is the husband's right to enjoy his wife, including sexual relations.
Distinguish milk al-mut'ah from milk al-raqabah (ownership of substance). In marriage, only the right of enjoyment is conveyed.
3
Clarify 'Milk' as Permissibility
The author clarifies that by 'milk' here we mean legal permissibility (hill), not proprietary ownership. The husband does not own his wife like a slave; he has a right to lawful enjoyment.
This distinction prevents misunderstandings about the nature of marital rights.
4
Recognize Alternative Definitions
The passage critiques the definition of al-Warshaki, who defined nikah as 'a meaning that changes the place'. This is rejected as an uncommon terminology.
The text underscores the importance of sticking to the well-known Hanafi definition.
