Commentary
This passage defines the marriage contract (nikah) in Hanafi law. It begins by stating that nikah, whether the marriage contract proper or other similar contracts, consists of an offer (ijab) from one speaker and an acceptance (qabul) from the other. The well-known words are 'zawwajtu' (I married) and 'tazawwajtu' (I married) but other words are also acceptable. Alternatively, a single person acting for both parties can utter both. The author then critiques the definition of al-Warshaki, who defines nikah as 'a meaning that changes the place' and treats the words as merely tools, which is an uncommon terminology. The correct Hanafi position is that milk al-mut'ah (ownership of enjoyment) is the legal effect of the contract, meaning the husband is entitled to both general use (intifa') and intercourse (wat'). The passage clarifies that 'milk' here means permissibility (hill) rather than proprietary ownership. The commentary then includes a biographical note on al-Warshaki and a quote from al-Durar explaining that mut'ah is the man's right to enjoy his wife, which implies that the husband has a right to look at his wife's private parts while she may not look at his if he prohibits her. The passage ends with a discussion of the wording 'yu'id' versus 'yarid'.

Key Terms
مَلْكُ الْمُتْعَةِ (milk al-mut'ah): Ownership of enjoyment, specifically the husband's right to benefit from his wife including intercourse.
إِيجَابٌ (ījāb): The offer of one party (usually the guardian) to enter into the marriage contract.
قَبُولٌ (qabūl): The acceptance of the other party (usually the groom) to the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi definition of the marriage contract (nikah)?
A) It is a contract that transfers ownership of the wife's person to the husband.
B) It is the combination of an offer and acceptance, granting the husband ownership of enjoyment.
C) It is a contract that makes sexual relations permissible without any ownership.
D) It is the exchange of the bride's money.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi nikah is a contract of exchange of offer and acceptance that conveys milk al-mut'ah—the husband's right to enjoy his wife—understood as a legal permissibility, not proprietorship.

Nature of Ownership in Marriage: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on the nature of marital ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi position on the husband's ownership in marriage and EXPLAIN the terminological nature of the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
The husband's ownership in marriage is like ownership of substance in its ruling — but the dispute is largely terminological.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with a key point of ikhtilaf between the Hanafis and Shafi'is: what exactly does the husband own in marriage? Is it ownership of the substance (milk al-ayn) or merely ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah)? The Hanafis argue it is like ownership of substance in its ruling, but careful analysis suggests the difference may be more about language than law.

Guided Path
1
State the positions
Identify what each school explicitly says about ownership in marriage. Hanafis: hukm milk al-ayn. Shafi'is: milk al-mut'ah.
Both agree that all parts and benefits belong to the husband.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof includes: (1) permissibility of marrying a small girl where no enjoyment is possible, (2) compensation for doubtful intercourse goes to the wife, not the husband. Analyse how each supports the claim.
If the husband owned the substance, compensation would go to him.
3
Assess the terminological resolution
The author suggests the dispute is khulf lafzi (terminological). Later scholars like in al-Bada'i interpret the husband's right as ikhtisas (special entitlement), reconciling both views.
This avoids implying full proprietary ownership while preserving the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمراد بالملك الحل الخ قال في النهر : وفي سراج الدبوسي اختلفوا في أن هذا الملك في حكم ملك العين أو المتعة ؛ قال أصحابنا بالأول، والشافعي بالثاني، وأجمعوا على أن جميع أجزائها ومنافعها له واستدل أصحابنا بجواز نكاح الموضعة أي الصغيرة ولا متعة وطء فيها ولا يرد ما لو وطئت بشبهة فإن البدل لها، ولو ملك العين لكان له لأن هذا الملك ليس حقيقياً بل في حكمه في حق تحليل الوطء دون ما سواه من الأحكام التي لا تتصل بحق الزوجية ا هـ. والظاهر الخلف لفظي، وإذا عرف هذا فما في البحر من أن المراد بالملك الحل لا الملك الشرعي لأن المنكوحة الخ فيه نظر، بل يملك الانتفاع حقيقة ولا يلزمه ذلك ما نظر لأن مدار كلام الدبوسي على أن هذا الملك ليس حقيقياً وأن المراد منه حكمه وهو حل الوطء ونحوه وهو معنى كلام البحر على أن كلام الدبوسي مخالف لقول المتن يرد على ملك المتعة فإن مقتضاه أنه لا خلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي في ذلك نعم كلام البدائع الآتي صريح في الخلاف عندنا لكن قول المؤلف هنا ولو ملك الانتفاع ببعضها حقيقة لكان بدله له فيه نظر لأن ملكه للبدل إنما يترتب على ملكه لذات البضع لا على ملكه لمنفعته فيملك عقر أمته لملكه لذات بضعها ولا يملك عقر زوجته لعدم ملك الذات، بل هو مالك لمنفعته وملك كل شيء بحسبه، ولذا فسر في البدائع الملك هنا بالاختصاص
His statement: 'And by 'milk' is meant permissibility, not legal ownership...' He said in al-Nahr: And in Siraj al-Dabusi: They differed over whether this ownership is in the ruling of ownership of substance or ownership of enjoyment. Our companions said the first, and al-Shafi'i said the second. And they agreed that all parts and benefits of her belong to him. Our companions argued by the permissibility of marrying a small girl (al-mawda'a) where there is no enjoyment of intercourse, and it is not refuted by the case of one who is sexually approached under a doubtful contract (shubha), for then the dower (badal) belongs to her; if he owned the substance, it would belong to him. This is because this ownership is not real but rather in its ruling for the purpose of permitting intercourse, excluding other rulings not connected to the marital right. And apparently the dispute is terminological. Knowing this, what is in al-Bahr that by 'milk' is meant permissibility not legal ownership because the married woman... is subject to scrutiny. Rather, he owns the benefit truly, and that does not necessitate what he thought, because the basis of al-Dabusi's statement is that this ownership is not real and that what is meant is its ruling, which is permissibility of intercourse. And that is the meaning of al-Bahr's statement. However, al-Dabusi's statement contradicts the text's statement 'yaridu 'ala milk al-mut'ah' for it implies no difference between us and al-Shafi'i in that. Yes, the subsequent statement of al-Bada'i is explicit in the difference in our view. But the author's statement here: 'and if he owned the benefit of some part truly, its compensation would belong to him' is subject to consideration, because his ownership of compensation depends on his ownership of the substance of the vulva (dhat al-bud'), not on his ownership of its benefit. Thus he owns the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse with a slave-girl) because he owns the substance of her vulva, but he does not own the 'aqr of his wife because he does not own the substance; rather, he owns its benefit, and ownership of each thing is according to its nature. Therefore, in al-Bada'i, ownership here is interpreted as 'ikhtisas' (special entitlement).
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the nature of the husband's ownership in marriage. The Hanafis (our companions) hold that the husband's ownership of his wife is like ownership of substance (hukm milk al-ayn) in terms of its ruling, while the Shafi'is hold it is only ownership of benefit (milk al-mut'ah). However, both schools agree that the husband has all rights over his wife's parts and benefits. The Hanafi proof is the permissibility of marrying a very young girl where there is no actual enjoyment, which would not be possible if the ownership were solely of benefit. Also, if the wife is violated by a third party under a doubtful contract, the compensation (dower) goes to her, not to the husband, proving he does not own the substance. The author of al-Bahr argues that 'milk' means permissibility rather than legal ownership, but the commentator critiques this, suggesting the difference between the schools is terminological (khulf lafzi). Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that the husband's right is a special entitlement (ikhtisas) for the purpose of sexual intercourse and related matters.

Key Terms
مَلْكُ الْعَيْنِ (milk al-ayn): Ownership of the substance of a thing, e.g., owning a slave-girl outright.
مَلْكُ الْمُتْعَةِ (milk al-mut'ah): Ownership of enjoyment, i.e., the right to benefit from the wife.
اخْتِصَاصٌ (ikhtisas): Special entitlement, a term used by later Hanafi scholars to describe the husband's right without implying full proprietary ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, the husband's ownership in marriage is described as:
A) Full ownership of the substance (milk al-ayn).
B) Ownership of enjoyment only (milk al-mut'ah).
C) Ownership in the ruling of substance (hukm milk al-ayn) for the purpose of intercourse.
D) No ownership at all, only a right of custody.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on marital ownership is that it is like ownership of substance in its ruling, but the dispute with the Shafi'is is largely terminological, and later scholars refined it as ikhtisas.

Ruling on Marriage: Sunnah or Wajib
Explains that marriage is sunnah mu'akkadah in normal circumstances but becomes wajib when one fears falling into zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage in the moderate state and when tawaqan occurs, and identify the conditions that change the ruling.

Core Idea
Marriage is sunnah mu'akkadah normally, but becomes wajib when one fears falling into zina.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between the moderate state (hālat al-iʿtidāl) and the state of intense desire (tawaqān). In the moderate state, marriage is an emphasized sunnah; leaving it is sinful. When a person fears committing zina without marriage, the ruling rises to obligatory. This lesson unpacks the conditions and evidence for this differentiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two states
Recognize the moderate state (iʿtidāl) where marriage is sunnah mu'akkadah, and the state of tawaqān where marriage becomes wajib.
The moderate state is the default; tawaqān is the exception.
2
Define the conditions of iʿtidāl
The moderate state requires ability to have intercourse, pay mahr, provide nafaqah, and freedom from fear of zina, injustice, or neglecting duties.
If any condition is missing, the person is not in iʿtidāl and the ruling changes.
3
Examine the dalīl
The Prophet's hadith 'Whoever turns away from my sunnah is not from me' (Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim) proves that marriage is at least sunnah mu'akkadah.
The hadith is used to establish the sunniyyah, not wujūb, in the normal case.
4
Note the intra-Hanafī ikhtilāf
Some Hanafī scholars consider marriage fard kifāyah or wājib kifāyah, but the relied-upon view is sunnah mu'akkadah.
The difference may be terminological; in practice, the ruling is conditional.
5
Apply the ruling
When tawaqān leads to fear of zina, marriage becomes wājib (or even farḍ if fear is strong). The level of obligation depends on the intensity of fear.
Tawaqān is not mere desire but fear of falling into sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهو سنة وعند التوقان واجب) بيان لصفته، أما الأول السنة المؤكدة على الأصح وهو محمل من أطلق الاستحباب وكثيراً ما يتساهل في إطلاق المستحب على السنة كذا في فتح القدير. وصرح في المحيط أيضاً بأنها مؤكدة ومقتضاه الإثم لو لم يتزوج لأن الصحيح أن ترك المؤكدة مؤثم كما علم في الصلاة. وأفاد بذكر وجوبه حالة التوقان أن محل الأول حالة الاعتدال كما في المجمع والمراد بها حالة القدرة على الوطء والمهر والنفقة مع عدم الخوف من الزنا والجور وترك الفرائض والسنن، فلو لم يقدر على واحد من الثلاثة أو خاف واحداً من الثلاثة فليس معتدلاً فلا يكون سنة في حقه كما أفاده في البدائع ودليل السنية حالة الاعتدال الاقتداء بحاله الا في نفسه ورده على من التخلي للعبادة كما في الصحيحين رداً بليغاً بقوله «فمن رغب عن سنتي فليس مني كما أوضحه في فتح القدير والتوقان مصدر تاقت نفسه إلى كذا إذا اشتاقت من باب طلب. كذا في المغرب. والمراد به أن يخاف منه الوقوع في الزنا لو لم يتزوج إذ لا يلزم من الاشتياق إلى الجماع الخوف المذكور وأراد بالواجب اللازم فيشمل الفرض والواجب الاصطلاحي فإنا قدمنا أنه فرض وواجب. ولم يذكر أنه حرام أو مكروه كما في المجمع لأن الجور حرام بالنسبة إلى كل شخص وليس هو مختصاً بالنكاح حتى يجعل من أحكامه وصفته .أراد من أمته زوجته لعدم ملك الذات، بل هو مالك لمنفعته وملك كل شيء بحسبه، ولذا فسر في البدائع الملك هنا بالاختصاص قوله : (أما الأول فالمراد به السنة المؤكدة على الأصح قال في النهر وقال بعض مشايخنا إنه فرض كفاية، وقيل بل واجب على الكفاية، وقيل على التعيين وينبغي ترجيحه لثبوت المواظبة عليه والإنكار عل من رغب عنه ا هـ. وهو وجيه ولا يبعد أن يكون المراد بالسنية لما مر في باب الإمامة من تصريح صاحب البدائع وغيره في الجمع بين القول بوجوب الجماعة وسنيتها بأنه اختلاف في العبارة لأن السنة المؤكدة والواجب سواء ا.هـ. تأمل. ولا ينافي ذلك كون الوجوب عند التوقان لأن الواجب يختلف فإذا خاف الوقوع في الحرام وتركه يكون إثمه أشد من تركه عند عدم التوقان قوله : (والمراد به أن يخاف منه الوقوع في الزنا أي الخوف بمعنييه السابقين لحمله الواجب على ما يشمل الفرض .
His statement: 'And it is sunnah, and when there is intense desire (tawaqan), it is wajib' is an explanation of its description. As for the first, it is sunnah mu'akkadah according to the correct view, and it is the interpretation of those who use the term 'mustahabb' (recommended) loosely; they often take liberty in calling sunnah as mustahabb. As in Fath al-Qadir. And it is stated clearly in al-Muhit that it is mu'akkadah, and its consequence is sin if one does not marry, because the correct view is that leaving a sunnah mu'akkadah is sinful, as known from prayer. And by mentioning its obligation in the case of tawaqan, he indicates that the first case is the moderate state, as in al-Majma'. That state is the ability to have intercourse, pay the dowry, and provide maintenance, along with no fear of falling into zina, injustice, or abandoning obligations and sunnah. So if one is unable to do any of these three or fears any of these three, he is not in a moderate state, and thus marriage is not sunnah for him, as stated in al-Bada'i. The evidence for it being sunnah in the moderate state is following the Prophet's example in his own case, and his strong rejection of those who turn away from his sunnah, as in the two Sahih collections, with his words: 'Whoever turns away from my sunnah is not from me.' As elaborated in Fath al-Qadir. And 'tawaqan' is the masdar of 'taqat nafsuhu ila katha' when he yearns for it, from the bab of talab. As in al-Mughrib. And what is meant by it is that one fears falling into zina if he does not marry, because yearning for intercourse does not necessarily entail the mentioned fear. And by 'wajib' he means the obligatory (al-lazim), which includes both fard and the technical wajib, since we have stated that it is fard and wajib. He did not mention that it is haram or makruh, as in al-Majma', because injustice is haram for every person and is not specific to nikah so as to be among its rulings and attributes. ... (continues with further discussion)
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of marriage in Hanafi law. The matn states that marriage is 'sunnah' (recommended) under normal conditions and 'wajib' (obligatory) when one experiences intense desire (tawaqan). The commentator clarifies that the sunnah mentioned is the mu'akkadah (emphasized) sunnah, which is so close to wujub that some scholars even call it fard kifayah. The moderate state (i'tidal) is defined as having the physical ability to marry, the financial means for mahr and nafaqah, and no fear of committing zina, injustice, or neglecting religious duties. When a person loses moderation, the ruling changes. The proof for marriage being sunnah is the Prophet's sunnah and his warning against those who abandon it, as in the hadith 'Whoever turns away from my sunnah is not from me.' When desire becomes so intense that one fears falling into zina, marriage becomes wajib according to the level of fear: if it is a serious fear, it may be fard; if a lesser fear, it is wajib in the technical sense. The passage also notes that some scholars consider marriage to be fard kifayah, and there is a discussion that the difference between calling it sunnah mu'akkadah and wajib may be mere terminology.

Key Terms
التَّوَقَانُ (al-tawaqān): Intense desire or yearning for something, specifically sexual desire that leads to fear of committing zina.
السُّنَّةُ الْمُؤَكَّدَةُ (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah): Emphasized sunnah, which is close to wajib; neglecting it is sinful but not as severe as neglecting a fard.
حَالَةُ الِاعْتِدَالِ (hālat al-i'tidāl): The moderate state where a person has ability to marry and no fear of zina, injustice, or neglecting obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does marriage become wajib according to the Hanafi school?
A) When a person is wealthy and able to have children.
B) When a person fears falling into zina if they do not marry.
C) When parents insist on marriage.
D) When the mahr is affordable.

Key Takeaway
Marriage is sunnah mu'akkadah in the moderate state but becomes wajib when intense desire leads to fear of zina.

Definition of Nikah and Its Core Legal Effect: Milk al-Mut'ah
Explaining the definition of nikah and the ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define legal marriage (nikah) and identify its primary effect as milk al-mut'ah, distinguishing it from ownership of the person (milk al-raqabah).

Core Idea
Marriage grants ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), not ownership of the wife's person.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi definition of nikah and its most important effect: the husband's exclusive right to enjoy his wife's body. The author clarifies that this is not ownership of her person but of the benefit of her private part. Understanding this distinction is key to many marriage rulings, including dower and concubinage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of nikah
The passage implies that marriage is a contract that gives the husband milk al-mut'ah. This is the essential effect that distinguishes it from other contracts.
The definition is not explicitly given here but is the subject of the commentary.
2
Understand milk al-mut'ah as the primary effect
Milk al-mut'ah means the husband's exclusive right to enjoy his wife's private part and all her limbs. This is not ownership of her person but the benefit.
This is the core Hanafi position.
3
Contrast with incidental permissibility (shubha and concubinage)
When a married woman is approached under a shubha, the dower goes to her, proving the husband does not own her body. In concubinage, milk al-mut'ah is incidental to ownership of the slave body.
This clarifies that marriage is primarily for enjoyment, not ownership.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Some Hanafi scholars say milk al-mut'ah is ownership of the benefit; others say it is ownership of the person but only for enjoyment. Both agree on the effect, but differ on its nature.
This does not affect the fiqh rulings.
5
Apply the implications to marriage rulings
Because the husband does not own the wife's person, he cannot sell her or treat her as property. The dower is a substitute for the benefit, not for her person.
This has practical consequences for financial obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرعي فالمراد به كتاب النكاح لأن المنكوحة لو وطئت بشبهة فمهرها لها، ولو ملك الانتفاع ببضعها حقيقة لكان بدله له. وذكر في البدائع أن من أحكامه ملك المتعة وهو اختصاص الزوج بمنافع بضعها وسائر أعضائها استمتاعاً أو ملك الذات والنفس في حق التمتع على اختلاف مشايخنا في ذلك. واحترز بقوله «قصداً عما يفيد الحل ضمناً كما إذا ثبت في ضمن ملك الرقبة كشراء الجارية للتسري فإنه موضوع شرعاً لملك الرقبة وملك المتعة ثابت ضمناً وإن قصده المشتري، وإنما لم يكن ملك المتعة مقصوداً لملك الرقبة في الشراء أو نحوه لتخلفه عنه في شراء محرمه نسباً ورضاعاً والأمة المجوسية
The legal (marriage): what is meant by it is the book of nikah, because if a married woman is cohabited under a shubha (mistaken belief), her dower is for her; and if he owned the benefit of her private part in reality, its substitute would be for him. And it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' that among its legal effects is milk al-mut'ah (ownership of enjoyment), which is the husband's exclusive right to the benefits of her private part and all her other limbs for enjoyment, or ownership of the person and soul regarding enjoyment, according to the difference among our shaykhs about that. And he excluded by his saying '(intentionally)' that which effects permissibility incidentally, as when it is established within ownership of the slave body, like buying a slave girl for concubinage, for legally it is established for ownership of the slave body and milk al-mut'ah is established incidentally even if the buyer intended it. And milk al-mut'ah is not intended in ownership of the slave body in purchase or the like due to its absence in purchasing a mahram relative by lineage or suckling and a Magian slave girl.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines legal marriage (al-nikah al-shar'i) and its primary effect. The author first establishes that marriage grants the husband ownership of the benefit of his wife's private part (milk al-bud') such that if she is cohabited under a shubha, the dower goes to her, not him. This is evidence that he does not own the act itself but only the right to enjoyment. The author then cites al-Bada'i' which states that among the effects of marriage is milk al-mut'ah – the husband's exclusive right to enjoy his wife's body. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement whether this is ownership of the benefit or ownership of the person for the purpose of enjoyment. Then the author notes that the definition's phrase 'intentionally' excludes cases where permissibility is incidental, such as a master owning a slave girl for concubinage – there, milk al-mut'ah is subsidiary to milk al-raqabah (ownership of the slave's person), and it is not the primary purpose. Importantly, milk al-mut'ah is absent in cases where the slave is a mahram or a Magian, because such a woman cannot be lawfully used for enjoyment.

Key Terms
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut'ah): The husband's exclusive right to enjoy his wife's body and limbs within lawful limits.
بضع (bud'): The private part of a woman; the object of ownership in marriage.
شبهة (shubhah): A mistaken belief that intercourse is lawful, leading to a valid dower obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main effect of marriage according to the Hanafi school?
A) Ownership of the wife's person (milk al-raqabah)
B) Ownership of the enjoyment of her private part (milk al-mut'ah)
C) Partnership
D) Permissibility of intercourse only

Key Takeaway
Marriage in the Hanafi school grants the husband exclusive enjoyment rights (milk al-mut'ah), not ownership of the wife's person.

Recommended Practices for Marriage (Adab al-Nikah)
Recommended practices for marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended practices for marriage according to the Hanafi school, including the recommended location, timing, and spouse selection criteria.

Core Idea
Marriage is a recommended act of worship; its perfection lies in location, timing, and selection of a righteous spouse.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq outlines the recommended practices (adab) for marriage according to the Hanafi school. Following these sunnah practices brings barakah and helps avoid fitnah. The text covers where and when to perform the contract, how to announce it, the ruling on musical instruments, and extensive advice for choosing a spouse.

Guided Path
1
Locate the recommended venue and day
Find where the author states that the marriage contract should be performed. Identify which day is recommended and why.
The text explicitly says: 'استحباب مباشرة عقد النكاح في المسجد' and 'استحبابه يوم الجمعة'.
2
Examine the ruling on duff and zifaf
Read the part about beating the duff (tambourine) and the wedding procession (zifaf). Note the difference between duff with and without jingles, and whether zifaf is disliked.
The author mentions ikhtilaf on duff; the mufta bihi is that duff without jingles is permissible, with jingles is makruh. Zifaf is not disliked unless it involves sin.
3
Extract the criteria for a suitable spouse for men
List the qualities a man should seek in a wife according to the text: piety, good lineage, etc. Also list the types of women he should avoid.
The text gives both positive (e.g., righteous, virgin) and negative (e.g., beautiful from bad origin, ill-tempered) guidance.
4
Note the advice for women and guardians
Identify what qualities a woman should look for in a husband (good religion, generosity) and what guardians should consider when marrying off their daughters (compatibility, not delaying).
Key terms: kuf (compatible match), and the prohibition of marrying a daughter to an old or ugly man.
5
Summarize the adab
Compile a concise list of the sunnah practices and prohibitions mentioned, distinguishing between recommended, permissible, and disliked.

And the author indicated by it being sunnah or wajib the recommendation of directly performing the marriage contract in the mosque, because it is an act of worship. And they explicitly stated its recommendation on Friday, and they differed on the dislike of the wedding procession (zifaf); the chosen view is that it is not disliked unless it involves a religious harm. And al-Tirmidhi narrated from 'Aishah that she said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Announce this marriage, perform it in the mosques, and beat the duff (tambourine) for it.' Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Dhakhirah: Beating the duff at a wedding is differed upon; its place is when it has no jingles (jalajil); as for what has jingles, he said it is not disliked, so it is makruh. Similarly, they differed regarding singing at weddings and walimah. Some considered the duff... end quote. And in Fatawa al-'Allami: Whoever wants to marry, it is permissible for him to do so, for Allah will guarantee him sustenance, so he should not fear poverty if his intention is to protect himself and remain chaste. He should marry a righteous woman of known lineage, nobility, and religiosity, because lineage is a cause of discord. He should avoid a beautiful woman from a bad origin. He should not marry a woman for her lineage, honor, wealth, and beauty, for if he marries her for that, he will only increase in humiliation, poverty, and baseness. He should marry someone above him in intellect, etiquette, piety, and beauty, and below him in honor, profession, lineage, wealth, age, and stature, for that is easier to avoid contempt and fitnah. He should choose the woman who is easiest in engagement and expense. Marriage to a virgin is best due to the hadith: 'Upon you are virgins, for they have the sweetest mouths, the purest wombs, and are most content with little.' And he should not marry a tall, thin woman, nor a short, ugly one, nor a highly fertile one, nor ill-tempered, nor one with a child (i.e., already mother), nor an old woman, based on the hadith: 'A black fertile woman is better than a beautiful barren one.' And he should not marry a tall free woman, nor a free woman without her guardian's permission due to its being impermissible according to some, nor an adulteress. And a woman should choose a husband with good religion, character, generosity, and wealth, and she should not marry a sinful man. And he should not marry his young daughter to a very old man nor to an ugly man, and he should marry her to a compatible match (kuf). So when a compatible match proposes, he should not delay her. And a compatible match is every Muslim who is pious. And adorning the daughters... (partially cut off).

Commentary
This passage outlines recommended practices (adab) related to marriage. It begins by stating the recommendation to perform the marriage contract in a mosque and on Friday, based on a hadith from al-Tirmidhi. The author then discusses the ruling on beating the duff (a type of tambourine) during weddings, noting scholarly differences: it is permissible if without jingles, but if with jingles, some consider it disliked. Similarly, singing at weddings is controversial. The bulk of the passage provides extensive advice for men and women in choosing a spouse. For men, it recommends marrying a woman of good lineage and piety, avoiding beauty from a bad background, not marrying for worldly reasons, and choosing someone slightly above him in character but below in status to avoid fitnah. Marrying a virgin is preferred. It also lists types of women to avoid: tall and thin, short and ugly, very fertile, ill-tempered, those with children from previous marriage, old women, and adulteresses. For women, it advises choosing a religious and generous husband and avoiding a sinful man. For guardians, they should not marry their young daughter to an old or ugly man, and they should marry her to a compatible match (kuf) and not delay when a suitable match proposes.

Key Terms
الدف (al-duff): A tambourine-like instrument used in weddings; its permissibility varies by type.
الكفوء (al-kuf): A compatible match in marriage; a man who is a Muslim, pious, and suitable in terms of religious commitment, lineage, and profession.
الزفاف (al-zifaf): The wedding procession or the act of bringing the bride to the groom's house.

Marriage is Sunnah and Superior to Supererogatory Worship
Proof that marriage is a sunnah and better than supererogatory acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four proofs that marriage is a sunnah superior to supererogatory worship and explain the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Marriage is a sunnah superior to nawafil by consensus, threat, prophetic practice, and its benefits.

In this passage, Ibn Nujaym explains why marriage is not merely permissible but a confirmed sunnah that outranks supererogatory acts of worship. He draws on arguments from al-Bada'i' that highlight the legal and practical superiority of marriage, and addresses a potential objection from the story of Prophet Yahya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is nikah (marriage). The ruling is that it is a sunnah muakkadah, meaning a confirmed practice of the Prophet, not merely permissible.
The term 'sunnah' here implies a specific legal weight in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Locate the four proofs
The text enumerates four arguments: (1) consensus that sunnah acts take precedence over nawafil, (2) threat for leaving a sunnah but not for nawafil, (3) the Prophet's continuous practice, (4) marriage protects from sin and provides necessities.
These proofs are taken from al-Bada'i', a core Hanafi text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Each proof directly supports the claim that marriage is better than supererogatory worship. For example, the first proof uses ijma to establish a hierarchy: sunnah > nawafil. The third uses the Prophet's example as a binding model.
The dalalah is clear: if the Prophet never abandoned marriage despite being able to worship constantly, it must be superior.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and refutation
A potential objection arises from the praise of Prophet Yahya's celibacy. The author refutes this by stating that it belonged to the previous law, not ours.
This highlights the principle that rulings are specific to their shari'ah unless adopted by our law.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is that marriage is sunnah muakkadah. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq confirms this and considers it the relied-upon view.
Some later Hanafis considered it wajib under certain conditions, but the majority upholds the sunnah level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد بالسنية أن الاشتغال به أفضل من التخلي لنوافل العبادات ولذا قال في المجمع وتفضله على التخلي للنوافل واستدل له في البدائع بوجوه : الأول أن السنن مقدمة على النوافل بالإجماع الثاني أنه أوعد على ترك السنة ولا وعيد على ترك النوافل الثالث أنه فعله رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم و واظب عليه وثبت عليه بحيث لم يخل عنه بل كان يزيد عليه ولو كان التخلي للنوافل أفضل لفعله . وإذا ثبت أفضليته في حقه ثبتت في حق أمته لأن الأصل في الشرائع هو العموم والخصوص بدليل. والرابع أنه سبب موصل إلى ما هو مفضل على النوافل لأنه سبب لصيانة النفس عن الفاحشة ولصيانة نفسها عن الهلاك بالنفقة والسكنى واللباس والحصول الولد الموحد. وأما مدحه تعالى يحيى عليه السلام بكونه سيداً وحصوراً وهو من لا يأتي النساء مع القدرة فهو في شريعتهم لا في شريعتنا ا هـ .
And he indicated by [saying] 'sunnah' that engaging in it is better than devoting oneself to supererogatory acts of worship. Hence he said in al-Majma': and its preference over devoting to supererogatory acts is proven. And he adduced for it in al-Bada'i' with several points: First, that supererogatory acts are given precedence over nawafil by consensus. Second, that there is a threat for leaving a sunnah, and no threat for leaving nawafil. Third, that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did it, persevered in it, and was constant in it to the extent that he never abandoned it, rather he increased in it. If devoting oneself to supererogatory acts were better, he would have done it. And when its superiority in his regard is established, it is established for his community, because the principle in the laws is generality and specificity by evidence. Fourth, that it is a means leading to what is preferred over nawafil, because it is a cause for protecting oneself from immorality, for protecting oneself from destruction regarding expenditure, shelter, clothing, and for obtaining a monotheistic child. As for Allah's praise of Yahya (peace be upon him) for being a master and chaste, meaning one who does not approach women despite ability, that is in their law, not in our law.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling that marriage is a confirmed sunnah and superior to supererogatory worship. The author presents four proofs from al-Bada'i': first, consensus that sunnah acts are given precedence over nawafil; second, the existence of a threat for leaving a sunnah but not for nawafil; third, the Prophet's continuous practice; fourth, marriage leads to protection from sin and fulfills essential needs. The passage also refutes a possible objection from the story of Yahya (AS) by noting that his situation was specific to his law.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): A confirmed practice of the Prophet, superior to nawafil but not obligatory.
النوافل (al-nawafil): Supererogatory acts of worship beyond the obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Marriage is a confirmed sunnah with proofs from consensus, threat, prophetic example, and benefit, making it superior to supererogatory worship.

The Marriage Contract: Offer, Acceptance, and Verbal Requirement
Explains the terms of offer and acceptance in the marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the components of the marriage contract according to Hanafi fiqh, explain the dispute regarding acceptance by action, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Marriage requires explicit verbal acceptance; action does not suffice, unlike sale.

This lesson examines the technical definition of the marriage contract in Hanafi fiqh, focusing on the elements of offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the contract consists of three elements: offer, acceptance, and the legal connection (al-irtibat al-shar'i). He then addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute: Can acceptance be by action, such as the husband giving the dowry in the session? The preferred view is that verbal acceptance is mandatory, distinguishing marriage from sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the marriage contract
The passage states that the contract consists of offer, acceptance, and legal connection. Understand that offer and acceptance are parts, not the whole contract.
This differs from a view that considers offer and acceptance as the contract itself.
2
Define offer and acceptance technically
Offer is the first utterance by either party; acceptance is the second utterance. Both must be in the past tense for the contract to be concluded.
The passage rejects the possibility of acceptance preceding offer.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance by action
Sahib al-Bidayah holds that giving dowry in the session counts as acceptance by action. Others, including the author of al-Muhit, require verbal acceptance.
The dispute hinges on analogy to sale (qiyas) vs. the gravity of marriage requiring witnesses.
4
Evaluate the evidence and the role of qiyas
The opposing view analogizes marriage to sale, where mutual exchange (ta'ati) suffices. The Hanafi response distinguishes marriage due to its seriousness and need for witnesses.
The mufta bihi is that verbal acceptance is required, as per al-Muhit.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the first view, a marriage could be valid without a verbal 'qabiltu'; under the second, it would be invalid. The mufta bihi is the latter.
This affects the validity of many marriages if only action was used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وينعقد بإيجاب وقبول وضعاً للمضي أو أحدهما أي ينعقد النكاح أي ذلك العقد الخاص ينعقد بالإيجاب والقبول حتى يتم حقيقة في الوجود والانعقاد هو ارتباط أحد الكلامين بالآخر على وجه يسمى باعتباره عقداً شرعاً ويستعقب الأحكام بالشرائط الآتية . كذا قرره الكمال هنا وقرر في كتاب البيع ما يفيد أن المراد هنا من الانعقاد الثبوت وأن الضمير يعود إلى النكاح باعتبار حكمه، فالمعنى يثبت حكم النكاح بالإيجاب والقبول . ومقصوده في البابين تحقيق أن الإيجاب مع القبول عين العقد لا غيره كما يفهم من ظاهر العبارة. والحق أن العقد مجموع ثلاثة : الإيجاب والقبول والارتباط الشرعي، فلم يكن الإيجاب والقبول عين العقد لأن جزء الشيء ليس عينه وسيأتي تمامه في البيع إن شاء الله تعالى. والإيجاب لغة الإثبات، واصطلاحاً هنا اللفظ الصادر أولاً من أحد المتخاطبين صلاحية اللفظ لذلك، رجلاً كان وامرأة والقبول اللفظ الصادر ثانياً من أحدهما الصالح لذلك مطلقاً، فما وقع في المعراج وغيره من أنه لو قدم القبول على الإيجاب بأن قال تزوجت ابنتك فقال زوجتكها فإنه ينعقد غير صحيح إذ لا يتصور تقديمه، بل قوله تزوجت ابنتك إيجاب والثاني قبول وهل يكون القبول بالفعل كالقبول باللفظ كما في البيع؟ ؟ قال البزازية : أجاب صاحب البداية في امرأة زوجت نفسها بألف من رجل عند الشهود فلم يقل الزوج شيئاً لكن أعطاها المهر في المجلس أنه يكون قبولاً. وأنكره صاحب المحيط وقال : لا ما لم يقل بلسانه قبلت بخلاف البيع لأنه ينعقد بالتعاطي، والنكاح لخطره لا ينعقد حتى يتوقف على الشهود بخلاف إجازة نكاح الفضولي بالفعل لوجود القول ثمة ا هـ . وهل يكون القبول بالطلاق؟ قال في الخانية من تعليق الطلاق: امرأة قالت لأجنبي زوجت نفسي منك فقال الرجل فأنت طالق طلقت، ولو قال أنت طالق لا تطلق ولا يكون هذا الكلام قبولاً للنكاح لأن هذا الكلام إخبار أما في المسألة الأولى جعل طلاقها جزاء لنكاحها وطلاقها لا يكون جزاء لنكاحها إلا بالقبول فيكون كلامه قبولاً للنكاح ثم يقع الطلاق بعده ا هـ. فقد ساوى النكاح البيع فإنه لو قال بعتك هذا العبد بكذا فقال فهو حر عتق، ولو قال بدون الفاء لا وهذا بخلاف الإقرار قال في البزازية : قالت أنا امرأتك فقال لها أنت طالق يكون إقراراً قوله : (تقديمه) أي القبول قوله : (ولا) يكون هذا الكلام أي أنت بدون الفاء .
His statement: 'And it is concluded by offer and acceptance in the past tense or one of them' means the marriage contract is concluded by offer and acceptance so that it is actualized in existence. 'Conclusion' is the connection of one statement with the other in a manner that is considered a contract legally, and it entails the rulings with the conditions to follow. Thus Kamal Ibn al-Humam decided here and decided in the book of sale that what is meant here by 'conclusion' is affirmation (thubut), and the pronoun refers to marriage in terms of its ruling. So the meaning is: the ruling of marriage is established by offer and acceptance. And his intention in both chapters is to verify that offer and acceptance are identical to the contract, not something else, as understood from the apparent expression. But the truth is that the contract consists of three things: offer, acceptance, and the legal connection; so offer and acceptance are not the identical contract because a part of a thing is not the thing itself. Its completion will come in the book of sale, if Allah wills. 'Offer' linguistically means affirmation; technically here it is the first statement uttered by one of the two interlocutors, suitable for that, whether man or woman. 'Acceptance' is the second statement uttered by one of them, suitable for that absolutely. So what occurred in al-Mi'raj and elsewhere that if he presents the acceptance before the offer, such as saying 'I married your daughter' and he said 'I married her to you', it is concluded, is not correct, because presenting it is inconceivable. Rather, his saying 'I married your daughter' is the offer, and the second is acceptance. And is acceptance by action like acceptance by word, as in sale? Al-Bazzaziyyah said: The author of al-Bidayah answered regarding a woman who married herself to a man for a thousand dirhams in the presence of witnesses, and the husband did not say anything but gave her the dowry in the session, that it is acceptance. And the author of al-Muhit rejected that and said: No, unless he says with his tongue 'I accept', unlike sale, because sale is concluded by mutual exchange (ta'ati), and marriage, due to its seriousness, is not concluded until it is tied to witnesses, unlike the ratification of the marriage of a stranger by action because speech exists there. And is acceptance by divorce? He said in al-Khaniyyah from the chapter of suspended divorce: A woman said to a stranger: 'I married myself to you', and the man said: 'Then you are divorced', she is divorced. But if he said 'you are divorced', she is not divorced, and this statement is not acceptance of marriage because this statement is informative. In the first case, he made her divorce a consequence of her marriage, and her divorce cannot be a consequence of her marriage except with acceptance, so his statement is acceptance of the marriage, then divorce occurs after it. Thus, marriage is similar to sale; if he said 'I sold you this slave for such-and-such' and the other said 'he is free', he becomes free, but if he said without the 'fa' (then), it is not so. And this is unlike acknowledgment. He said in al-Bazzaziyyah: She said 'I am your wife', and he said 'you are divorced', it is an acknowledgment. His statement: 'its presentation' means the acceptance. His statement: 'and not' means this statement, i.e., 'you are divorced' without the 'fa'.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the technical definition of the marriage contract. The author argues that the contract consists of offer, acceptance, and legal connection, and responds to a view that offer and acceptance are the contract itself. He then discusses intra-Hanafi disputes: whether acceptance can be by action (like giving dowry) or by a conditional divorce statement. The preferred view is that acceptance must be verbal, unlike sale. The passage compares marriage to sale in terms of the particle 'fa' (then) and notes that without it, the statement is not valid acceptance.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The first utterance from either party indicating the offer of marriage.
القبول (al-qabul): The second utterance indicating acceptance of the offer.
الارتباط الشرعي (al-irtibat al-shar'i): The legal connection between offer and acceptance that constitutes the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi) in the passage, which of the following is a valid acceptance in a marriage contract?
A) The husband gives the dowry in the session without speaking.
B) The husband says 'I accept' in the past tense immediately after the offer.
C) The husband says 'You are divorced' without the particle 'fa'.
D) The husband remains silent but nods his head.

Key Takeaway
The marriage contract requires explicit verbal acceptance in the past tense; action alone does not constitute valid acceptance, distinguishing it from sale.

The Imperative in Marriage: Offer or Proxy? An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi debate on imperative verb in marriage contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether the imperative verb in a marriage contract constitutes an offer (ijab) or a proxy (tawkeel), and apply the contextual distinction to determine the operative ruling.

Core Idea
The imperative 'marry me' can be an offer or a proxy; context decides, impacting witness requirements.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi debate: when someone says 'zawwijni' (marry me) during a marriage contract, is that an immediate offer requiring acceptance and witnesses, or is it merely a proxy appointment? The difference shapes how the contract is formed and validated. We will follow the reasoning of leading Hanafi authorities and see how context resolves apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State that some Hanafis (Qadikhan, al-Khulasa) consider the imperative an offer (ijab), while others (al-Hidayah, al-Majma') consider it a proxy (tawkeel).
Both views are within the school.
2
Understand the proofs
Examine the reasoning: offer view relies on the imperative aiming at immediate realization; proxy view relies on one person managing both sides in nikah and the imperative limiting the agent to the session.
The proxy view is supported by analogy to sale differences.
3
Analyze the objection and resolution
Study the case from al-Khulasa where the proxy says 'give your daughter' – if the father responds 'I gave', the marriage does not conclude until the proxy says 'I accept'. This seems to favor the proxy view. The resolution is that this case is based on the assumption of proxy not offer.
Al-Kamal prefers the offer view, but the author notes contextual distinctions.
4
Trace the practical consequence
If the imperative is an offer, witnesses must hear it; if a proxy, witnesses need not hear it (though some still require it because the proxy is part of the contract). This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
Al-Marghinani in al-Mi'raj considers the proxy as a shatr al-aqd, so witnesses do hear it.
5
Apply the contextual distinction
Differentiate between a casual request (e.g., 'give your daughter') and a request after prior negotiations (e.g., 'zawwijni' after khutbah). The latter is more likely an offer because it indicates immediacy and establishment.
This is the resolution from al-Ramz and al-Asbijabi.

الخ ) غير صحيح وكذا قوله وحينئذ تظهر ثمرة الاختلاف» لأنه ظهر أنه لا تفريعه على كل من القولين إذ لو كان إيجاباً وتوكيلاً لما توقف على قوله ثانياً قبلت» بل لو كان إيجاباً كان قول الآخر وهبت قبولاً فيتم العقد وكذا لو كان توكيلاً كما علمته مما . ويمكن تصحيح كلامه على وجه بعيد وهو أن يجعل قوله «فلو كان الأمر إيجاباً» تفريعاً على قوله «لكن يرد عليه الخ» مما فلا يرد شيء مر فتدبر . هذا وقد أجاب في الرمز عن إشكال المؤلف بأنه إنما توقف الانعقاد على القبول فيما ذكر من الفروع لأنه لم يظهر إرادة الإيجاب فيها لأن الوكيل أو الأب إذا اجتمع فقال هب ابنتك لفلان أو لا بني أو اعطها مثلاً كان ظاهراً في الطلب وأنه مستقبل لم يرد به الحال والتحقق فلم يتم به عقد بخلاف زوجني ابنتك بعد الخطبة ونحوها فإنه ظاهر في التحقق والإثبات الذي هو معنى الإيجاب فلا يرد عليه أنه يصح توكيل الأب في تزويج ولده لأنا نقول: لفظه هذا يخرج مخرج الإيجاب والإثبات لكونه إنشاء للتزويج فلا بد أن يظهر منه معنى الإثبات كما يأتي عن الأسبيجابي، ويشهد له ما في البزازية : طلب منها الزنا فقالت وهبت نفسه منك، وقيل لا يكون نكاحاً بخلاف الهبة ابتداء على وجه النكاح.
With marriage and it is said because of its requirement... she and he is from Arabic of the words of marriage by coinage, and if he said you are not a wife to me and you are divorced it is not an acknowledgment due to the precedence of the presumption that he did not intend the reality of divorce etc. He mentioned absolutely in the two wordings so it includes the two wordings by ruling. The wording issued from the one who manages both sides legally, and includes what is not and what is not mentioned with them the two objects or one of them after the indication of the context and premises because omission for an evidence exists in every tongue. And the past tense was chosen because the language setter did not set a specific word for creation, rather creation was recognized by the Law, and choosing the past tense for its indication of establishment and certainty without the future. And his statement 'or one of them' is an explanation for its conclusion by two wordings: one past and the other future like his saying 'marry me your daughter' and he said 'I married you' which is explicit that the future is an offer, and Qadikhan stated it in his Fatawa where he said: his imperative word in marriage is an offer, and similarly divorce, khula', guarantee, gift etc., and similarly in al-Khulasa. The author of al-Hidayah and al-Majma' held that the imperative is not an offer but rather a proxy, and his saying 'I married you' stands in place of the two wordings unlike in sale because it is known that one person in marriage manages both sides unlike sale, and it is an implied proxy so it does not contradict its limitation to the session. So you have known the difference of the mashayikh regarding the imperative being an offer or proxy, so what is in al-Mukhtasar is according to one of the two opinions, so the objection of Mulla Khusraw is repelled... etc.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the issue of whether the imperative verb (like 'zawwijni' - marry me) in a marriage contract functions as an actual offer (ijab) or as a proxy appointment (tawkeel). It is an intra-Hanafi dispute, as both views are within the Hanafi school. The author first presents the view of Qadikhan from his Fatawa and al-Khulasa that the imperative is an offer, arguing that the wisdom behind choosing the past tense does not preclude the imperative from being a valid offer, especially when context indicates its immediate purpose. He then presents the contrary view from al-Hidayah and al-Majma' that the imperative is merely a proxy appointment because the marriage contract can be concluded by one person acting for both sides, and the imperative limits the agent to the session. The author then discusses the preferred view (raajih) according to al-Kamal, who says the imperative is indeed an offer because an offer is any statement that aims to achieve the meaning, which applies to the imperative. However, an objection arises from a case in al-Khulasa where if the proxy says 'give your daughter to so-and-so' and the father says 'I gave', the marriage is not valid until the proxy says 'I accept', suggesting the imperative is only a proxy. The author resolves this by noting that the ruling in al-Khulasa is based on the assumption that the imperative is a proxy, not an offer. Later, the passage discusses implications for witness requirements: if the imperative is a proxy, witnesses do not need to hear it (since proxy does not require witnesses), but if it is an offer, witnesses must hear it. Some scholars like al-Marghinani in al-Mi'raj consider that even if it is a proxy, due to its role in the contract, witnesses must hear it. The author concludes by noting a response in al-Ramz that distinguishes between contexts: in some cases like after a proposal, the imperative functions as an offer, while in casual requests it remains a mere proxy. This nuanced discussion shows the depth of Hanafi jurisprudence on contractual language.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijab): The offer in a contract; the statement that initiates the contract proposal. In this context, it refers to the imperative verb being considered a valid offer in marriage.
توكيل (tawkeel): Proxy appointment; the imperative is considered an instruction appointing the other party as an agent to conclude the marriage, not an actual offer.
لفظ الأمر (lafz al-amr): The imperative form of the verb, e.g., 'zawwijni' (marry me). Its legal status in marriage contracts is debated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical difference between considering the imperative 'zawwijni' an offer versus a proxy?
A) If it is an offer, witnesses must hear it; if a proxy, they do not.
B) If it is an offer, the contract is invalid without immediate acceptance; if a proxy, it can be delayed.
C) If it is an offer, only one party needs to speak; if a proxy, both must speak.
D) If it is an offer, the marriage is immediate; if a proxy, it is delayed.

Key Takeaway
The imperative in marriage can be an offer or a proxy depending on context; the preferred view is the offer view after prior negotiations, and witness requirements follow accordingly.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Nature of 'Zawwijni' in Marriage Contracts
Analyzing whether 'zawwijni' constitutes an offer or delegation in marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'zawwijni' as an offer (ijab) and as a delegation (tawkil) in Hanafi marriage contracts, and apply the contextual criteria to determine which applies.

Core Idea
In marriage, the imperative 'marry me' is an immediate offer due to prior preliminaries; in sale, it is a request for bargaining.

The word 'zawwijni' (marry me) appears frequently in marriage contracts. Hanafi jurists debate whether this imperative constitutes an immediate offer (ijab) or a delegation (tawkil) to the addressee. The context—whether the words are spoken after formal preliminaries or spontaneously—determines the legal classification. This lesson explores the arguments and the practical consequences for the validity and witness requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The passage centres on whether 'zawwijni' is ijab or tawkil within the Hanafi school.
Both views are based on different interpretations of the father's capacity to delegate.
2
Examine the examples from sale vs marriage
In sale, 'sell me' is never an offer; in marriage, it can be an offer due to context.
The difference arises because marriage usually follows formal preliminaries.
3
Analyse the witness requirement
On the tawkil view, witnesses need not hear the command; on the ijab view, they must hear it.
This is a key practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
4
Understand the role of context and custom
The passage emphasises that customary preliminaries in marriage make the imperative a definitive offer.
Custom (urf) is used to interpret the intent of the speaker.
5
Apply the criterion to a new scenario
Given a new case, determine if the words constitute ijab or tawkil based on indications like prior negotiations.
The analysis must consider both the wording and the surrounding circumstances.

This indicates that if someone says after the conversation between them 'I sold this slave for a thousand dirhams' and the other said 'I bought', it is valid even if the seller did not say 'I sold to you'. If the command were an offer (ijab), it would not depend on acceptance, unless it is said that it is built on the view that it is delegation, not offer. Then the practical consequence of the difference between the two views appears, but it depends on transmission. And it is stated in Fath al-Qadir that on the view that the command is delegation, the contract is completed by the respondent; on the view that the command is offer, the contract is completed by both of them. End. So on this, it is not a condition that the two witnesses hear the command on the first view, because witnessing is not required for delegation; but it is a condition on the second view, as is obvious. Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi legal discussion on the nature of the word 'zawwijni' (marry me) in marriage contracts. The author (al-Haskafi or Ibn Nujaym? Actually it's al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) is commenting on the issue of whether this imperative constitutes an immediate offer (ijab) or a delegation (tawkil) to the addressee to perform the marriage. The passage begins by citing a case from al-Tatimma: if someone says 'give your daughter to so-and-so' and the father says 'I gave', the contract is not valid unless the agent (the addressee) says 'I accepted'. This indicates that the father's statement is not an offer but a request. However, the author notes a problem from al-Zahiriyya: since a father can delegate in marrying his child, if his command were an offer, it would not require acceptance. The resolution depends on which view one takes: if it is delegation, the contract is completed by the respondent (the person being addressed) when he marries; if it is an offer, both parties complete it. The practical consequence is whether witnesses must hear the command: on the delegation view, no witness required for the command; on the offer view, yes. Later, the author compares with sale, where the word 'sell me' is never an offer but a request for bargaining. The reason is that marriage is typically preceded by formal preliminaries, so the imperative is understood as a definitive offer, while sale is usually a bargaining process. Then the passage discusses other verbal forms like the imperfect tense (atakawwajuka) and whether they constitute an offer. The correct Hanafi position is that the context determines the intent. The passage ends with more details from various authorities. Overall, the key point is that the legal classification of words in contracts depends on custom and context.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijāb): An offer that immediately creates the contract upon acceptance, without needing further action from the offeror.
توكيل (tawkīl): Delegation of authority to another to perform an act, here to perform the marriage contract.
قبول (qabūl): Acceptance that completes the contract when joined with an offer.
شاهد (shāhid): Witness present at the time of the contract; in marriage, two witnesses are required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the word 'zawwijni' treated as an offer (ijab) in marriage but not in sale?
A) Because marriage is a private matter while sale is public.
B) Because marriage is typically preceded by formal preliminaries, so the command indicates determination, whereas sale involves bargaining.
C) Because sale requires explicit wording.
D) Because marriage is a religious contract.

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of the imperative 'zawwijni' in marriage depends on context: after preliminaries it is an immediate offer; without them, it is a delegation.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Imperative and Present Tense as Valid Forms of Ijab in Nikah
Explains the validity of imperative and present tense as ijab in nikah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates imperative and present tense as ijab in marriage contracts and distinguish this from the ruling in sale.

Core Idea
In marriage, context refutes bargaining; imperative and present tense indicate definitive intent.

The form of words used for the marriage offer (ijab) can affect its validity. While past tense is universally accepted, the Hanafi school also validates the imperative and present tense under certain contextual conditions. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the reasoning, focusing on the absence of bargaining in marriage and the analogy to the testimony of faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is using the imperative (e.g., 'zawwiji nafsaki') or present tense (e.g., 'atazawwajuki') as the offer in a marriage contract. The ruling is that the contract is valid (sahih) when such forms are used, provided the context indicates definitive intent.
The ruling is from one of two opinions within the Hanafi school, but it is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is not a direct text but a rational analogy (qiyas) and analysis of custom (urf). The author cites the absence of bargaining (musawamah) in marriage, unlike in sale, and analogizes the present tense to the word of testimony (shahadah).
The dalil is zanni in transmission and meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof establish the ruling? In marriage, the imperative is not taken as a bargaining request because marriage is typically preceded by introductions and negotiations, so it indicates a definitive offer (tahqiq). The present tense, though literally future, can indicate the present state as in shahadah, especially when context (khutbah, introductions) points to present intent.
This contrasts with sale, where imperative is assumed for bargaining.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is intra-Hanafi difference: some scholars consider the imperative invalid as ijab. Also, for present tense, some require explicit intention of the present, but the correct view is that it is for the present without need for intention. Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is require past tense for ijab.
The text resolves by affirming validity based on context and analogy.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that both imperative and present tense are valid as ijab, considering context and intent. This is supported by later authorities like Ibn Nujaym (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) and references to al-Muhit and Fath al-Qadir.
Thus, a marriage contract using these forms is sahih in Hanafi fiqh.

ثم رأيت في الفتاوى الظهيرية ما يدل على أنه لا يشترط سماع الشهود للفظ الأمر قال في النكاح بالكتابة سواء قال زوجي نفسك مني فبلغها الكتاب فقالت زوجت أو كتب تزوجتك وبلغها الكتاب فقالت زوجت نفسي منك لكن في الوجه الأول لا يشترط إعلامها الشهود، وفي الوجه الثاني يشترط ا هـ . وإنما جعل الأمر إيجاباً في النكاح على أحد القولين ولم يجعل في البيع إيجاباً اتفاقاً لأنه لا مساومة في النكاح لأنه لا يكون إلا بعد مقدمات ومراجعات غالباً فكان للتحقيق بخلاف البيع لا يتقدمه ما ذكر فكان الأمر فيه للمساومة كما ذكره الكمال في البيوع، وبه اندفع ما ذكره في النكاح كما لا يخفى. هذا مع أن المصنف لم يصرح بالمستقبل وإنما ذكر أنه ينعقد بلفظين أحدهما ماض وسكت عن الآخر لشموله الحال والمستقبل ومنه الأمر وقد علمته وأما المضارع فإن كان مبدوءاً بالهمزة نحو أتزوجك فتقول زوجته نفسي فإنه ينعقد علله في المحيط بأنه وإن كان حقيقة في الاستقبال إلا أنه يحتمل الحال كما في كلمة الشهادة وقد أراد به التحقيق والحال لا المساومة بدلالة الخطبة والمقدمات بخلاف البيع اهـ . ولا حاجة إليه لأن الأصح أن المضارع موضوع للحال وعليه تتفرع الأحكام كما في قوله كل مملوك أملكه فهو حر فإنه يعتق ما في ملكه في الحال لا ما يملكه بعد إلا بالنية لما ذكرنا وإن كان مبدوءاً بالتاء نحو تزوجني بنتك فقال فعلت ينعقد به إن لم يقصد به الاستيعاد لأنه يتحقق فيه هذا الاحتمال بخلاف الأول لأنه لا يستخبر نفسه عن الوعد، وإذا كان المقصود هو المعنى لا اللفظ لو صرح بالاستفهام اعتبر فهم الحال كما ذكره الأسبيجابي لو قال هل أعطيتنها فقال أعطيتك إن كان المجلس للوعد فوعد وإن كان للعقد فنكاح. وفي فتح القدير والانعقاد بقوله أنا متزوجك ينبغي أن يكون كالمضارع المبدوء بالهمزة سواء. وشمل كلام المصنف ما في النوازل لو قال زوجيني نفسك فقالت بالسمع والطاعة، وما إذا قال كوني امرأتي فقبلت كما في فتح القدير وفي الظهيرية : لو قال أبو الصغيرة لأبي الصغير زوجت ابنتي ولم يزد عليه شيئاً فقال أبو الصغير قبلت يقع النكاح للأب من قال هو الصحيح. ويجب أن يحتاط فيه فيقول قبلت لابني. وهذه المسألة تدل على أن جرى بينهما مقدمات البيع بعت هذا العبد وقال الآخر اشتريت يصح وإن لم يقل بعت منك والخلع على هذا ا هـ .

He said concerning marriage by writing: whether he said 'Marry yourself to me' and the letter reached her and she said 'I have married' or he wrote 'I have married you' and the letter reached her and she said 'I have married myself to you' – but in the first case, informing the witnesses is not a condition, and in the second case it is a condition, end. And the author's words include what is in al-Nawazil: if he says 'Marry me to yourself' and she says with obedience, and the case when he says 'Be my wife' and she accepts, as in Fath al-Qadir. And he should be cautious and say 'I accept for my son.' This issue indicates that if there took place between them introductions of sale: he said 'I sold this slave' and the other said 'I bought,' it is valid even if he did not say 'from you,' and khul' is upon this, end.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the validity of using the imperative and present tense as the offer (ijab) in a marriage contract. The author first cites al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya regarding marriage by writing, distinguishing between two scenarios of wording and whether the woman must inform witnesses of the content. The core ruling is that the imperative (e.g., 'marry yourself to me') can serve as ijab in marriage according to one opinion because marriage is typically preceded by negotiations, so it indicates definitive intent, unlike in sale where the imperative suggests bargaining. The reasoning is that there is no bargaining in marriage, so the imperative is taken as an actual offer. The passage then addresses present tense verbs: if it begins with hamzah (e.g., 'I marry you'), it is concluded because although literally future, it can indicate the present, analogous to the shahadah. If it begins with ta' (e.g., 'marry me'), it is valid if not intended as a promise. Even if the wording is interrogative, the context determines whether it is a promise or a contract. The author also includes rulings from Fath al-Qadir and al-Nawazil about expressions like 'I am married to you' and 'marry me yourself,' and a case where a father says 'I have married my daughter' without specifying the son, where the marriage is valid but caution is advised. This discussion emphasizes that in Hanafi fiqh, the meaning and context of the utterance are considered, not just the literal verb form.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative verb form used in marriage offer, e.g., 'zawwiji nafsaki minni' (marry yourself to me).
المضارع (al-mudari'): The present tense verb form, e.g., 'atazawwajuki' (I marry you).
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer made by one party in a marriage contract.
القبول (al-qabul): The acceptance of the offer in a marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the imperative considered valid as ijab in marriage but not in sale according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because marriage requires bargaining, unlike sale.
B) Because marriage is typically preceded by negotiations and indicates definitive intent, unlike sale which implies bargaining.
C) Because the imperative is always used for contracts.
D) Because sale does not require witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the imperative and present tense can serve as valid ijab in marriage if the context indicates definitive intent, unlike in sale where such forms suggest bargaining.

Unity of the Session in the Marriage Contract
Expands on the condition of unity of session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of unity of session and apply the ruling that walking nullifies the offer while a moving ship does not.

Core Idea
Unity of session is required; walking invalidates but a ship counts as a single session.

The marriage contract requires a direct connection between offer and acceptance. The Hanafi school considers the session as a unifier for convenience, but what counts as a single session? This lesson examines the rule, including the curious distinction between walking and riding a ship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The first condition is that the offer and acceptance must occur in the same gathering (ittihad al-majlis) when both parties are present.
If the session differs, the contract is not concluded.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason is to ensure temporal connection of the offer and acceptance. The session is made a unifier for facilitation.
Immediacy (fawr) is not required.
3
Distinguish walking from ship
If the parties contract while walking or riding an animal, the contract is not valid. However, if they are on a moving ship, it is valid. This is because walking involves continuous movement breaking the unity of the session, while a ship provides a stable setting.
The ship is considered a single session despite movement.
4
Note the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the contract is valid only if the session is unified. Walking or riding an animal invalidates, but a moving ship is an exception.
Later scholars analogized modern vehicles like cars to ships if they provide a stable session.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف شرائط الإيجاب والقبول فمنها اتحاد المجلس إذا كان الشخصان حاضرين فلو اختلف المجلس لم ينعقد، فلو أوجب أحدهما فقام الآخر أو اشتغل بعمل آخر بطل الإيجاب لأن شرط الارتباط اتحاد الزمان فجعل المجلس جامعاً تيسيراً. وأما الفور فليس من شرطه فلو عقدا وهما يمشيان ويسيران على الدابة لا يجوز، وإن كانا على سفينة سائرة جاز وسيأتي تمامه في البيع إن شاء الله تعالى. ومنها أن لا يخالف القبول الإيجاب فلو أوجب بكذا فقال قبلت النكاح ولا أقبل المهر لا يصح وإن كان المال فيه تبعاً كما في الظهيرية بخلاف ما لو قالت زوجت نفسي منك بألف فقال قبلت بألفين فإن يصح والمهر ألف إلا إن قبلت الزيادة في المجلس فهو ألفان على المفتي به كما في التجنيس، وبخلاف ما لو قال تزوجتك بألف فقالت قبلت بخمسمائة فإنه صحيح ويجعل كأنها قبلت الألف وحطت عنه خمسمائة كما في الذخيرة. وفي الظهيرية : لو قالت لرجل زوجت نفسي منك بألف فقال الرجل قبلت قبل أن تنطق المرأة بالتسمية لا ينعقد النكاح ما لم يقل الزوج قبلت بعد التسمية. ومنها سماع كل منهما كلام صاحبه لأن عدم سماع أحدهما كلام صاحبه بمنزلة غيبته كما في الوقاية. وقيد المصنف انعقاده باللفظ لأنه لا ينعقد بالكتابة من الحاضرين فلو كتب تزوجتك فكتبت قبلت لم ينعقد، وأما من الغائب فكالخطاب، وكذا الرسول فيشترك سماع الشهود قراءة الكتاب وكلام الرسول. وفي المحيط : الفرق بين الكتاب والخطاب أن في الخطاب لو قال قبلت في مجلس آخر لم يجز، وفي الكتاب يجوز لأن الكلام كما وجد تلاشى فلم يتصل الإيجاب بالقبول في مجلس آخر، فأما الكتاب فقائم في مجلس آخر وقراءته بمنزلة خطاب الحاضر فاتصل الإيجاب بالقبول فصح ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن الشرط سماع الشهود قراءة الكتاب مع قبولها أو حكايتها ما في الكتاب لهم، فلو قالت إن فلاناً كتب إلي يخطبني فاشهدوا أني قد زوجت نفسي منه صح النكاح، وتمامه في الفصل السابع عشر في النكاح بالكتابة من الخلاصة، وقيد بالإيجاب النهر : ولم يذكر والمضارع المبدوء بالنون كنتزوجك أو نزوجك من ابني وينبغي أن يكون كالمبدوء بالهمزة .
And the author did not mention the conditions of offer and acceptance. Among them: unity of the session when both persons are present; if the session differs, it is not concluded. If one makes the offer then the other stands up or busies himself with another action, the offer is nullified, because the condition of connection is unity of time, so the session is made a unifier for facilitation. As for immediacy, it is not a condition, so if they contract while walking or riding an animal, it is not permissible; but if they are on a moving ship, it is permissible, and its completion will come in sale if Allah wills. And among them: that the acceptance not contradict the offer. So if he offers with such-and-such and he says 'I accept the marriage but I do not accept the mahr,' it is not valid, even though the wealth is subsidiary, as in al-Zahiriyya. Contrary to the case where she says 'I married myself to you for a thousand' and he says 'I accept for two thousand,' it is valid and the mahr is one thousand, unless he accepts the increase in the session, then it is two thousand according to the mufta bihi as in al-Tajnis. And contrary to the case where he says 'I married you for a thousand' and she says 'I accept for five hundred,' it is valid and it is as if she accepted the thousand and remitted five hundred from him, as in al-Dhakhira. In al-Zahiriyya: if a woman says to a man 'I married myself to you for a thousand' and the man says 'I accept' before the woman mentions the amount, the marriage is not concluded unless the husband says 'I accept' after the mention. And among them: each hearing the speech of the other, because failure of one to hear the other's speech is tantamount to his absence, as in al-Wiqaya. And the author restricted its conclusion to verbal expression, because it is not concluded by writing from those present, so if he writes 'I married you' and she writes 'I accept,' it is not concluded. As for from the absent, it is like verbal address, and likewise the messenger, so the witnesses must hear the reading of the letter and the speech of the messenger. In al-Muhit: the difference between writing and verbal address is that in verbal address, if he says 'I accept' in another session, it is not valid; in writing, it is valid, because speech as soon as it occurs dissipates, so the offer does not connect with acceptance in another session. As for writing, it remains in another session, and its reading is like the verbal address of the present, so the offer connects with acceptance and is valid, end. Then know that the condition is that the witnesses hear the reading of the letter along with her acceptance or her narration of what is in the letter to them. So if she says 'So-and-so wrote to me proposing, so bear witness that I have married myself to him,' the marriage is valid. Its completion is in the seventeenth chapter on marriage by writing from al-Khulasa. And al-Nahr restricted to the offer: and he did not mention the present tense beginning with noon like 'natzawwajuki' or 'nuzawwijuka min ibni' and it should be like that beginning with hamzah.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates the conditions (shurut) for the validity of the offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) in a marriage contract, expanding on the author's earlier mention. The first condition is unity of the session (ittihad al-majlis): if both parties are present, the offer and acceptance must occur in the same gathering. If one leaves or engages in another action, the offer is nullified. This is to ensure temporal connection; the session is considered a unifier for convenience. Immediacy (al-fawr) is not a condition, so contracting while walking is not valid? Actually, the text says if they contract while walking or riding an animal, it is not permissible (la yajuz), but if on a moving ship, it is permissible. This apparently contradictory ruling is explained by the fact that walking typically involves continuous movement and lack of a single session, whereas a ship can serve as a single setting. The second condition is that the acceptance must not contradict the offer. If the offer is for a specific mahr and the acceptance rejects it, the marriage is invalid. However, if the acceptance specifies a higher or lower amount, there are different rulings: a higher mahr results in only the original amount unless accepted in session; a lower mahr is interpreted as acceptance with a discount. Also, acceptance must occur after the woman explicitly mentions the mahr. The third condition is that each party must hear the other's speech, as not hearing is like absence. Fourth, the contract must be concluded verbally among those present; writing is only valid from an absent person or via a messenger, where witnesses must hear the reading of the letter or the messenger's words. The passage then discusses the difference between writing and speech: speech dissipates, so acceptance must be immediate; writing persists, so acceptance in another session is valid. Finally, it mentions that witnesses must hear the reading of the letter along with the woman's acceptance or narration. This detailed exposition clarifies the practical requirements for a valid marriage contract in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
اتحاد المجلس (ittihad al-majlis): The condition that the offer and acceptance occur in the same gathering or session.
الفور (al-fawr): Immediacy; the requirement that acceptance follow the offer without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, contracting while walking is not permissible, but contracting on a moving ship is permissible. What is the underlying reason?
A) Walking is more distracting than sailing.
B) The ship is considered a single session despite movement, while walking breaks the session.
C) The ship has witnesses, but walking does not.
D) The ship is a type of mobile home.

Key Takeaway
Unity of the session is a key condition for the marriage contract; walking invalidates, but a moving ship is an exception.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Correspondence of Acceptance and Mahr Discrepancies
Explains the condition of correspondence and mahr cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when acceptance contradicts the offer and apply the Hanafi rulings on mahr differences.

Core Idea
Acceptance must match the offer; differences in mahr are interpreted as discounts or require session acceptance.

The acceptance must not contradict the offer, especially regarding the mahr. But what if the amounts differ? The Hanafi school provides nuanced rulings: a higher mahr is only accepted if the increase is agreed in the session, while a lower mahr is seen as a discount. This lesson clarifies these cases.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The acceptance must not contradict the offer. If the acceptance explicitly refuses the mahr, the contract is invalid.
The mahr is considered subsidiary (tabi'), but its rejection invalidates the contract.
2
Analyze the case of a higher mahr
If the woman offers marriage with a specific mahr and the man accepts with a higher mahr, the contract is valid but the mahr is the original amount unless the woman accepts the increase in the same session.
The increase is considered a separate offer if not accepted immediately.
3
Analyze the case of a lower mahr
If the man offers with a specific mahr and the woman accepts with a lower amount, the contract is valid and it is treated as if she accepted the original mahr but remitted the difference as a discount (hitat).
This is a concession to preserve the contract.
4
Note the importance of timing
The acceptance must occur after the offer is fully stated, including the mahr. If the man accepts before the woman mentions the mahr, the marriage is not concluded until he repeats acceptance after the mention.
This ensures the mahr is part of the offer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
