Intra-Madhhab Narrations and the Role of Intention in Nikah Wordings
Resolving internal Hanafi disputes and clarifying the condition of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two narrated positions from Abu Hanifa on valid wordings for nikah, explain why the mashayikh preferred Ibn Rustam's narration, and state the role of intention and witness understanding.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa gave two verdicts: the mashayikh chose the stricter one (Ibn Rustam) because it is decisive, allowing only words that give ownership of the neck.

The earlier lessons presented the rulings on various wordings. But why are there different opinions on wordings like ariyah and ijara? This traces back to two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa himself, transmitted by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Ibn Rustam. This lesson examines that dispute, the tarjih, and the additional requirement of intention or contextual understanding for the witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Al-Hasan narrated: every word by which something is possessed (yumlak bihi shay) concludes nikah. Ibn Rustam narrated: every word by which the necks are possessed (yumlak bihi al-riqab) concludes nikah.
2
Understand the tarjih
The mashayikh preferred Ibn Rustam's narration because it is muhkam (decisive), while al-Hasan's is muhtamal (probable). The probable is interpreted in light of the determined, so the stricter view is adopted.
This principle is a key usul rule in tarjih.
3
Apply to ariyah and ijara
Ariyah and ijara (when woman is not wage) do not give ownership of the neck, so according to Ibn Rustam's narration they are invalid. This is why the correct view is invalidity.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Discuss intention (niyyah) and contextual indication (qarina)
The author notes that he did not qualify with niyyah or qarina. There are different views: some require niyyah only if mahr is not mentioned, some absolutely require it, and the chosen view in Fath al-Qadir is that the witnesses must understand the parties' intent.
5
Note the shubhah effect
When invalid wording is used, the contract is not concluded but shubhah is established, which affects hadd and mahr obligations.

His statement: (And it is only valid with the wording of nikah and tazwij and what is placed for ownership of the 'ayn at the time) is an explanation of the limitation of the two wordings to what is mentioned. As for its conclusion with the wording of nikah and tazwij, there is no dispute about it. And as for its conclusion with what is placed for ownership of the 'ayn, it is our madhhab because ownership is a cause for ownership of enjoyment in its place via ownership of the neck (rahaba), which is established by nikah. So the name of the cause is applied like hibah, and the effect is intended, which is ownership of enjoyment, even though ownership of enjoyment is primary in nikah and implicit in tamlik. And the reason tamlik is not valid with the wording of nikah is because it is established in usul that borrowing the cause for the effect is permissible absolutely, and its opposite is not permissible except with the condition of specificity from both sides. Hence, the metonymy of the word 'itq for talaq is valid, but not its opposite. And the exclusivity in His statement 'khalisatan laka' (al-Ahzab:50) is only regarding the lack of mahr, not regarding conclusion with the word hibah, as is known in the books of khilafiyat. So nikah is concluded with the word hibah, 'atiyyah, sadaqah, milk, tamlik, ja'l, bay', and shira' on the most correct view. As for the word salam: if the woman is made the capital of salam, then it is concluded by consensus. If she is made the one selling salam, there is dispute: some say it is not concluded because salam for animals is not valid; some say it is concluded because it establishes ownership of the neck, and salam for animals is concluded, such that if possession is attached, it gives a corrupt ownership of the neck, and not everything that corrupts the real corrupts its metaphorical. Al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir preferred this view, and it is consistent with what is in the matn. Regarding sarf (currency exchange), there are two narrations and two opinions: some say it is not concluded because it is placed for establishing ownership of unspecified cash, and the object here is specified; some say it is concluded because it establishes ownership of the 'ayn in a general sense, and its preference is recommended because it falls under the general rule in Mukhtasar. Similarly, regarding its conclusion with the word qard, there are two opinions, the more correct being conclusion as in al-Kashf and al-Walwajiyya. In Fatawa al-Sayrafiyya, the more correct is conclusion (end quote). It should be relied upon because it gives ownership of the 'ayn at the time. Similarly, regarding its conclusion with the word sulh, there are two opinions, and in Ghayat al-Bayan it is decisively stated that it does not conclude because it is placed for remission and relinquishment of rights. Similarly, regarding its conclusion with the word rahn, there are two opinions, the more correct being non-conclusion as in al-Walwajiyya, which is apparent because it does not give ownership at all. He qualified with 'what is placed for tamlik' to exclude what does not give ownership, so it does not conclude with the word fida' (ransom), e.g., if she says 'I ransom myself from you' and he accepts, as in al-Khaniyya; also with ibra' (release), faskh (annulment), iqalah (revocation), khul' (divorce by redemption), kitabah (contract of emancipation), tamattu' (temporary marriage?), ibaha (permission), ihlaal (making permissible), rida (consent), ijazah (permission), wadi'ah (deposit), because they do not give ownership at all. And he qualified with 'ownership of the 'ayn' to exclude what gives only ownership of usufruct like 'ariyah (loan for use), so it does not conclude with it on the correct view. As for the word ijara (lease): if the woman is made the wage (ujrah), it concludes by consensus because it gives ownership of the 'ayn at the time in a general sense, provided immediate payment is stipulated or it is paid in advance. But if she is not made a wage, e.g., if he says 'I lease my daughter to you for such-and-such', the correct view is that it does not conclude because it does not give ownership of the 'ayn, and because there is contradiction between them since perpetuity is a condition of nikah and tenporariness is a condition of ijara. And he qualified to exclude what gives ownership of part of the 'ayn like the word shirkah (partnership), so it does not conclude with it as in al-Zuhiriyya. And he qualified with 'at the time' to exclude the word wasiyyah (will), so nikah does not conclude with it because it is ownership deferred to after death. This is the absolute statement of the commentators. In al-Walwajiyya and al-Zuhiriyya it is restricted to when it is absolute or deferred to after death; as for if he said 'I bequeath my daughter's pudendum to you now for a thousand dirhams' and the other accepts, the nikah is concluded because it has become a metonym for tamlik. The relied-upon view is the absolute statement because wasiyyah is a metonym for tamlik, so if it were concluded by it, it would be a metonym for nikah, and a metonym does not have a metonym, as in al-'Inayah from the section on bay'. In al-Mabsut: In every case where it is not concluded with these wordings, uncertainty (shubhah) is established, so the hadd punishment is dropped if intercourse occurs, and the lesser of the specified mahr and the mahr al-mithl is obligatory upon entry. End quote. Then know that the disagreement about 'ariyah and ijara, even though they do not give ownership of the 'ayn at all, is because that principle itself is disputed: al-Hasan narrated from al-Imam that 'every word by which something is possessed concludes nikah,' and these (wordings) indicate conclusion with both. Ibn Rustam narrated from al-Imam: 'every word by which the necks are possessed concludes nikah.' These indicate non-conclusion for both, as in al-Dhakhira. The mashayikh relied on Ibn Rustam's narration because it is decisive (muhkam), while al-Hasan's narration is probable (muhtamal), and the probable is interpreted in light of the determined. And the author did not qualify the word that gives ownership of the 'ayn with niyyah (intention) or qarinah (contextual indication). There is dispute about this: in al-Tabyin, niyyah is not required if the mahr is mentioned; in al-Mabsut it is not required absolutely; in Fath al-Qadir the chosen view is that the two witnesses must understand their intent. And in al-Bada'i: if he adds the hibah to the slave-girl by saying to a man 'I give you my slave-girl this' then... His statement: (And similarly regarding its conclusion with the word rahn there are two opinions) this contradicts what we previously mentioned from al-Fath where he made it among those where there is no dispute about non-conclusion. His statement: (And khul'): from al-Nahr: I say: it should be qualified with if it is not made as a substitute for khul'; if it is made, as when a stranger says 'Divorce your wife in exchange for this daughter of mine,' then it is said to be valid, taken from their statement: 'it does not conclude with the word ijara on the most correct view if the woman is made the lessee (musta'jira); but if she is made the substitute for ijara, as when he says 'I have rented your house with this daughter of mine,' this should not be disputed in its permissibility because it is addition to her with a word by which the necks are possessed.' His statement: 'the nikah is concluded because it has become a metonym for tamlik.' From al-Nahr: and more than one approved it. Al-Fath said:
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the principle that the marriage contract (nikah) is only valid with specific wordings. The author, Ibn al-Humam, explaining Kanz al-Daqaiq, begins by stating the rule: nikah is valid only with the wordings nikah and tazwij, or any wording that is placed for immediate ownership of a specific object (tamlik al-'ayn fi al-hal). The first two (nikah and tazwij) are agreed upon by all schools. For the other wordings, the Hanafi school extends validity to any word that implies ownership of the 'ayn, because tamlik (ownership) is the cause (sabab) for the ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'ah) in marriage. By mentioning the cause, the effect is intended—this is a metonymy (majaz) where the name of the cause is used for the effect. However, the reverse (using the effect for the cause) is not generally allowed without specific indication, which is why the word nikah cannot be used for tamlik. The passage then surveys various wordings: hibah, 'atiyyah, sadaqah, milk, tamlik, ja'l, bay', shira'—all valid on the most correct view. For salam, if the woman is the capital, it's valid by consensus; if she is the seller, there is a dispute, but the preferred view is that it is valid because it establishes ownership of the neck (raqabah), even if only a corrupt ownership. For sarf (currency exchange), there are two narrations and two opinions; the preferred view is that it is valid because it gives ownership of the 'ayn in general. For qard (loan), the more correct view is that it is valid. For sulh (compromise), there is a dispute, but Ghayat al-Bayan declares it invalid because sulh is for remission, not ownership. For rahn (pawn), the more correct view is that it is invalid because it does not give ownership at all. The author then lists wordings that are invalid because they do not give ownership at all: fida', ibra', faskh, etc. He distinguishes between ownership of the 'ayn (which is necessary for nikah) and ownership of usufruct only (like 'ariyah, which is invalid). For ijara (lease), if the woman is the wage (ujrah), it is valid by consensus because immediate ownership is established; otherwise it is invalid due to contradiction between perpetuity (ta'bid) of nikah and tenporariness (ta'qit) of ijara. For wasiyyah (will), the normal case is invalid because it defers ownership to after death. However, if the wording is used for immediate transfer, it may be valid as a metonym for tamlik; but the relied-upon view is that it is invalid because a metonym cannot have a metonym. The passage ends with a note that in cases where nikah is not concluded with these wordings, a shubhah (legal uncertainty) arises, so the hadd penalty for zina is dropped and the lesser of the specified mahr or mahr al-mithl is due. The passage also addresses the difference of opinion regarding 'ariyah and ijara, tracing it to two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (every possession word concludes nikah) and one from Ibn Rustam (only words that give ownership of necks). The mashayikh preferred the latter as decisive (muhkam). Finally, the author notes that the requirement of intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (qarinah) is disputed; the chosen view is that the witnesses must understand the parties' intent.

Key Terms
محكم (muhkam): A text that is clear and decisive, admitting only one interpretation. Here, Ibn Rustam's narration is muhkam because it precisely limits valid wordings.
محتمل (muhtamal): A text that is capable of multiple interpretations, thus not decisive. Here, al-Hasan's narration is muhtamal because 'every word by which something is possessed' is general.
نية (niyyah): Intention, the conscious purpose behind an action. In the context of the marriage contract, some scholars require intention for the wording to be effective.
قرينة (qarina): Contextual indication or circumstantial evidence that clarifies the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the mashayikh prefer Ibn Rustam's narration over al-Hasan's narration?
A) Because al-Hasan's narration was weak in transmission.
B) Because Ibn Rustam's narration is muhkam (decisive) while al-Hasan's is muhtamal (probable).
C) Because Ibn Rustam was a more reliable narrator than al-Hasan.
D) Because the majority of the Sahibayn agreed with Ibn Rustam.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's preferred position on valid marriage wordings is based on Ibn Rustam's decisive narration, which restricts validity to words that give ownership of the neck; intention is required but the witnesses' understanding suffices.

Valid Expressions for Concluding a Marriage Contract
Explains Hanafi criteria for valid marriage expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal expressions validly conclude a marriage contract according to the Hanafi school and explain why expressions like ariyya (loan) and ijara (lease) are invalid in the preferred view.

Core Idea
Marriage requires words that immediately transfer ownership of a physical object; expressions of usufruct or deferred ownership are invalid.

The marriage contract in the Hanafi school is distinguished by its strict requirement on the verbal expression (lafz). Not every word indicating consent suffices; only those that effect an immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik al-ayn) are valid. This lesson explores which expressions work, which do not, and why, including the crucial intra-madhhab dispute between al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Ibn Rustom on the scope of valid expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core requirement for a valid expression
The passage states that the expression must be 'placed for transferring ownership of a physical object immediately' (tamlik al-ayn bi al-hal). Understand that this is the central criterion for validity.
This excludes expressions that convey ownership of a usufruct or deferred ownership.
2
Classify expressions as valid or invalid based on ownership type
Review the list: expressions like fida', ibra', faskh, etc. do not confer ownership at all; ariyya confers only usufruct; ijara when the woman is not the payment also fails. Only words that give full immediate ownership of a physical object (like nikah, tazwij, hiba with context) are valid.
Hiba requires contextual indication (qarina) or intention to be valid.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute over ariyya and ijara
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad transmits from Abu Hanifa that 'every word by which something is owned' concludes marriage, which would include ariyya. Ibn Rustom transmits 'every word by which slaves are owned'—more restrictive. The later scholars (mashayikh) preferred Ibn Rustom's transmission as more definitive (muhkama).
This shows how transmissions from the same Imam can differ and how tarjih is performed.
4
Understand the role of intention (niyya) and contextual indication (qarina)
When an expression like hiba is used, the context (e.g., presence of witnesses, mention of dower) or the explicit intention of the parties can direct the word to marriage. The chosen view is that the witnesses must understand the intent.
For a free woman, hiba automatically indicates marriage because literal ownership is impossible.
5
Recognize additional valid expressions beyond the standard three
Expressions like 'be my wife' (kawn), 'I marry you' (arastuka), and 'I return you' (raja'tuka) are also valid, with conditions for the latter including mention of wealth for a non-related woman.
The author is criticized for omitting these from the original matn.

انعقاده بلفظ الرهن قولان أصحهما عدم الانعقاد كما في الولوالجية وهو ظاهر لأنه لا يفيد الملك أصلاً قيد بما وضع للتمليك احترازاً عما لا يفيده فلا ينعقد بلفظ الفداء كما لو قالت فديت نفسي منك فقبل كما في الخانية والإبراء والفسخ والإقالة والخلع والكتابة والتمتع والإباحة والإحلال والرضى والإجازة - بالزاي - والوديعة لأنها لا تفيد الملك أصلاً. وقيد بتمليك العين احترازاً عما يفيد ملك المنفعة فقط كالعارية فلا ينعقد بها على الصحيح وأما بلفظ الإجارة فإن جعلت المرأة أجرة فينعقد اتفاقاً لأنه يفيد ملك العين للحال في الجملة بأن شرط الحلول أو عجلت وأما إذا لم تجعل أجرة كقوله أجرتك ابنتي بكذا فالصحيح أنه لا ينعقد لأنها لا تفيد ملك العين ولأن بينهما مضادة لأن التأبيد من شرائطه والتأقيت من شرائطها، واحترازاً عما يفيد تمليك بعض العين كلفظ الشركة فإنه لا ينعقد به كما في الظهيرية. وقيد بقوله في الحال احترازاً عن لفظ الوصية فإنه لا ينعقد النكاح بها لأنها تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت. كذا أطلق الشارحون، وقيده في الولوالجية والظهيرية بما إذا أطلق أو أضاف إلى ما بعد الموت، أما إذا قال أوصيت ببضع ابنتي للحال بألف درهم فقبل الآخر انعقد النكاح لأنه صار مجازاً عن التمليك والمعتمد الإطلاق لأن الوصية مجاز عن التمليك فلو انعقد بها لكان مجازاً عن النكاح والمجاز لا مجاز له كما في العناية من البيع. وفي المبسوط : في كل موضع لم ينعقد بهذه الألفاظ فإنه يثبت الشبهة فيسقط الحد لو وطئ ويجب الأقل من المسمى ومن مهر المثل عند الدخول اهـ.
ثم اعلم إنما وقع الاختلاف في العارية والإجارة وإن كانا لا يفيدان ملك العين قطعاً لأن ذلك الأصل مختلف فيه فقد روى الحسن عن الإمام أن كل شيء يملك به شيء ينعقد به النكاح وهذه تدل على الانعقاد بهما، وروى ابن رستم عن الإمام كل لفظ يملك به الرقاب ينعقد به النكاح. وهذه تدل على عدمه فيهما كما في الذخيرة وإنما اعتمد المشايخ رواية ابن رستم لأنها محكمة، ورواية الحسن محتملة، فحمل المحتمل على الحكم. ولم يقيد المصنف اللفظ المفيد لملك العين بالنية ولا بالقرينة وفيه اختلاف ففي التبيين لا تشترط النية مع ذكر المهر، وفي المبسوط لا تشترط مطلقاً، وفي فتح القدير المختار أنه لا بد من فهم الشاهدين مقصودهما، وفي البدائع ولو أضاف الهبة إلى الأمة بأن قال لرجل وهبت أمتي هذه منك فإن
قوله : (وكذا في انعقاده بلفظ الرهن قولان هذا منافٍ لما قدمناه عن الفتح حيث جعله مما لا خلاف في عدم الانعقاد به قوله : ( والخلع) قال في النهر أقول : وينبغي أن يقيد بما إذا لم تجعل بدل الخلع، فإن جعلت كما إذا قال أجنبي اخلع زوجتك ببنتي هذه فقيل صح أخذاً من قولهم لا ينعقد بلفظ الإجارة في الأصح إن جعلت المرأة مستأجرة، أما إذا جعلت بدل إجارة كما إذا قال استأجرت دارك هذه ببنتي . هذه ينبغي أن لا يختلف في جوازه لأنه إضافة إليها بلفظ تملك به الرقاب قوله : انعقد النكاح لأنه صار مجازاً عن التمليك قال في النهر : وارتضاه غير واحد. قال في الفتح :

كان الحال يدل على النكاح من إحضار الشهود وتسمية المهر مؤجلاً ومعجلاً ونحو ذلك ينصرف إلى النكاح، وإن لم يكن الحال دليلاً على النكاح، فإن نوى النكاح وصدقه الموهوب له فكذلك وينصرف إلى النكاح بقرينة النية، وإن لم ينو ينصرف إلى ملك الرقبة اهـ. فلم يشترط مع النية فهم الشهود ولا بد منه كما قدمناه بخلاف ما إذا أضيفت الهبة إلى الحرة فإنه ينعقد من غير هذه القرينة لأن عدم قبول المحل للمعنى الحقيقي وهو الملك للحرة يوجب الحمل على المجازي فهو القرينة فيكتفي بها الشهود حتى لو قامت قرينة على عدمة لا ينعقد به كما في الخانية وغيرها : لو طلب من امرأة الزنا فقالت وهبت نفسي منك فقال الرجل قبلت لا يكون نكاحاً وهو بمنزلة قول أبي البنت وهبتها منك لتخدمك فقال قبلت لا يكون نكاحاً اهـ. قال في الفتاوى : إلا إذا أراد به النكاح. فالحاصل أن النكاح ينعقد بالهبة إذا كان على وجه النكاح. وفي الظهيرية : لو قالت المرأة وهبت نفسي لك فقال الرجل أخذت قالوا لا يكون نكاحاً صحيحاً، وإنما استعيرت الهية للنكاح وإن كانت لا تفيد الملك إلا بالقبض لأنها سبب موضوع للملك وإنما تأخر القبض لضعف السبب لتعريه عن العوض، وينعدم الضعف إذا استعملت في النكاح لأن العوض يجب بنفسه كذا في النهاية. ويرد على المصنف ألفاظ ينعقد بها النكاح غير الثلاثة منها : الكون لما في الذخيرة وغيرها: لو قال لامرأة كوني امرأتي بكذا فقبلت انعقد بخلاف ما لو قالت المرأة أكون زوجة لك فقال نعم لا كما يصح في الظهيرية ومنها ما في الخانية لو قالت المرأة عرستك نفسي فقال قبلت انعقد، وذكره في الظهيرية بلفظ أعرستك ومنها لفظ الرجعة فقد صرح في الواقعات والخانية وكثير أنه ينعقد النكاح إذا قال للأجنبية راجعتك فقبلت كما لو قال للمبانة راجعتك، لكن شرط في الخانية أن يذكر المال وإن لم يذكر مالاً قالوا لا يكون نكاحاً. وشرط في التجنيس ذكر المال ونية الزوج، وفرق بعضهم بين الأجنبية والمبانة فينعقد به في المبانة دون الأجنبية، واستحسنه في فتح القدير .
ذلك
وفي الخانية : وكذا لو قالت المبانة لزوجها رددت نفسي عليك فهو بمنزلة الرجعة ينعقد به النكاح كما في الذخيرة ومنها ارفعها واذهب بها حيث شئت لما في الخانية : لو قال زوج
وينبغي أن لا يختلف في صحته حينئذ وخالفهم في البحر فقال : المعتمد الإطلاق الخ. وأقول: معنى كونها مجازاً عن التمليك إذا قال الآن أي الخاص الذي هو النكاح لا المطلق فلا يرد أن المجاز لا مجاز له ا هـ. أي المراد بكونها مجازاً عن التمليك هو التمليك الخاص الذي هو النكاح لا مطلق التمليك حتى يرد ما ذكر على أنه لا مانع من أن يكون مجازاً بمرتبتين كما في رأيت مشفر زيد» وفي حاشية الرملي قال المقدسي في شرح الكنز المنظوم: وأما مجاز المجاز فيثبت عند من له اطلاع على كتب اللغة كالأساس وغيره، وتمامه فيه، وكتب على هامش نسخته البحر : هذا مردود لأن الوصية تمليك كما
Book of Marriage. There are two opinions on its conclusion with the word pledge (al-rahna), the more correct being that it is not concluded, as in al-Walwaljiyya, and it is apparent because [pledge] does not confer ownership at all. He restricted [the expression] to what is placed for transferring ownership (tamlik) to exclude what does not confer it. So it does not conclude with the word ransom (al-fida'), as if she says 'I ransom myself from you' and he accepts, as in al-Khaniyya; nor with discharge (al-ibra'), annulment (al-faskh), revocation (al-iqala), khul', kitaba, tamattu', ibaha, ihlal, rida, ijaza (with za'), and wadi'a, because they do not confer ownership at all. He restricted [the expression] to transferring ownership of a physical object (tamlik al-ayn) to exclude what only confers ownership of usufruct, like loan (al-ariyya); so it does not conclude with it according to the sound opinion. As for the word lease (al-ijara), if the woman is made the lease payment (ujra), then it does conclude by agreement because it confers ownership of the physical object immediately to some extent, such as if immediate payment is stipulated or she hastens it. But if she is not made the lease payment, like his saying 'I have leased my daughter to you for such-and-such,' then the sound opinion is that it does not conclude because it does not confer ownership of the physical object, and because there is opposition between them since perpetuity is a condition [of marriage] while temporality is a condition of lease. He also restricted to what excludes conferring ownership of part of an object, like the word partnership (al-shirka); so it does not conclude with it, as in al-Zahiriyya. He restricted with 'immediately' to exclude the word bequest (al-wasiyya), so marriage does not conclude with it because it is ownership deferred to after death. The commentators have stated it absolutely, but in al-Walwaljiyya and al-Zahiriyya they restricted it to when he either says it absolutely or adds it to after death. If he says 'I bequeath the sexual organ of my daughter immediately for one thousand dirhams' and the other accepts, the marriage is concluded because [the word] became metaphorical for tamlik, but the relied-upon view is the absolute statement because wasiyya is metaphorical for tamlik; if marriage were concluded with it, it would be a metaphor for marriage, and a metaphor cannot have a second metaphor (al-majaz la majaz lahu), as in al-Inaya from the Book of Sale. In al-Mabsut: In every case where marriage is not concluded with these words, doubt (shubha) is established, so the hadd punishment is dropped if intercourse occurs, and the lesser of the named dower and the proper dower is obligatory upon entry. End. Then know that disagreement occurred over ariyya and ijara, even though they definitely do not confer ownership of the physical object, because the principle itself is disputed. Al-Hasan narrated from the Imam that every word by which something is owned concludes marriage, and these [words] would indicate conclusion with both. Ibn Rustom narrated from the Imam that every word by which slaves are owned concludes marriage, and these indicate non-conclusion with both, as in al-Dhakhira. The later scholars relied on Ibn Rustom's narration because it is definitive (muhkama), while al-Hasan's narration is probable (muhtamala), so the probable is interpreted [in light of] the definitive. The author did not restrict the expression that confers ownership of a physical object to intention (niyya) or contextual indication (qarina), and there is disagreement. In al-Tabyin: Intention is not required when mentioning the dower. In al-Mabsut: It is not required at all. In Fath al-Qadir: The chosen view is that the witnesses must understand their intent. In al-Bada'i: If he adds a gift (hiba) to a slave-woman, such as saying to a man 'I have given you this slave-woman of mine,' then if the context indicates marriage – like bringing witnesses and mentioning dower deferred and advanced – it is directed to marriage. If the context does not indicate marriage, then if he intends marriage and the donee confirms him, similarly it is directed to marriage by the contextual indication of intention. If he does not intend, it is directed to ownership of the slave. End. So he did not require the witnesses to understand the intention; but it is necessary, as we have previously stated, unlike when the gift is added to a free woman, for it concludes without this contextual indication because the subject's lack of acceptance of the literal meaning – ownership of a free woman – necessitates carrying it to the metaphorical; that is the contextual indication, so the witnesses suffice with it. Even if there is a contextual indication to the contrary, it does not conclude with it, as in al-Khaniyya and others: If he solicits a woman for adultery and she says 'I give myself to you' and the man says 'I accept,' it is not marriage; similarly, if the father of the girl says 'I have given her to you to serve you' and the man says 'I accept,' it is not marriage. End. In al-Fatawa: Except if he intends marriage by it. The conclusion is that marriage is concluded by hiba if it is on the basis of marriage. In al-Zahiriyya: If a woman says 'I give myself to you' and the man says 'I took,' they said it is not a valid marriage. The word 'gift' is used metaphorically for marriage, even though it does not confer ownership except by taking possession (qabd), because it is a cause (sabab) established for ownership; the delay of possession is due to the weakness of the cause because it is devoid of consideration. This weakness disappears when it is used in marriage because the consideration becomes obligatory by itself, as in al-Nihaya. The author is criticized for [limiting to] expressions by which marriage is concluded beyond the three [i.e., nikah, tazwij, and hiba?]. Among them: The word 'be' (al-kawn) – as in al-Dhakhira and others: If he says to a woman 'Be my wife for such-and-such' and she accepts, it is concluded; unlike if the woman says 'I am your wife' and he says 'Yes,' it is not valid, as stated in al-Zahiriyya. Among them: In al-Khaniyya: If the woman says 'I have married you myself' (arastuka nafsi) and he says 'I accept,' it is concluded; it is mentioned in al-Zahiriyya with the wording 'arastuka.' Among them: The word 'return' (al-raj'a) – it is explicitly stated in al-Waqi'at, al-Khaniyya, and many [works] that marriage is concluded if he says to a non-related woman (ajnabiyya) 'I return you' and she accepts, just as if he said to a divorced woman during her waiting period (mubana) 'I return you.' However, in al-Khaniyya it is conditioned that he mention the wealth; if he does not mention wealth, they said it is not marriage. In al-Tajnis it is conditioned to mention wealth and the husband's intention. Some differentiated between the non-related woman and the divorced woman, concluding with it for the divorced woman but not the non-related woman, and this was deemed good (istahsana) in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Khaniyya: Similarly, if the divorced woman tells her husband 'I have returned myself to you,' it is like raja', and marriage is concluded by it, as in al-Dhakhira. Among them: 'Lift her and take her wherever you wish' – as in al-Khaniyya: If the husband says...

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed expansion of the rules governing the verbal expression (lafz) required for a valid marriage contract (nikah) in the Hanafi school. The core principle stated at the outset is that a valid marriage contract must be concluded using words that effect an immediate transfer of ownership of a physical object (tamlik al-ayn bi al-hal). The author first distinguishes between expressions that do not confer ownership at all (e.g., fida', ibra', faskh, iqala, khul', kitaba, tamattu', ibaha, ihlal, rida, ijaza, wadi'a) and those that only confer ownership of usufruct (manfa'a) like ariyya (loan) or conditional ownership like ijara (lease) when the woman is not made the payment. Even for ijara, if the woman is made the lease payment (ujra), the contract is valid by consensus because it involves immediate transfer of the physical object in some form. The author then addresses expressions that defer ownership, such as wasiyya (bequest). The relied-upon view (mu'tamad) is that marriage cannot be concluded with wasiyya because it is a metaphorical expression for tamlik, and a metaphor cannot be further metaphorized. However, if the bequest is made for the immediate present, it is treated as a valid expression for marriage. The passage then discusses the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding ariyya and ijara, based on two transmissions from Abu Hanifa: one from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad (that any expression by which something is owned qualifies) and one from Ibn Rustom (that only expressions by which slaves are owned qualify). Later scholars preferred Ibn Rustom's narration as more definitive. The text then addresses the issue of intention (niyya) and contextual indication (qarina). The chosen view (mukhtar) from Fath al-Qadir is that the witnesses must understand the intent of the parties. The discussion of hiba (gift) is extensive: a hiba to a slave-woman can be valid for marriage if the context or intention so indicates, but a hiba to a free woman automatically indicates marriage because a free woman cannot be owned literally. However, if the context contradicts marriage (e.g., in a request for zina), it is not valid. The passage concludes by listing several additional expressions beyond the standard three (nikah, tazwij, hiba?) by which marriage can be concluded: 'kuni imra'ati' (be my wife), 'arastuka' (I marry you), and 'raja'tuka' (I return you) – though for the latter, some conditions apply regarding mentioning wealth and the status of the woman (whether she is a non-relative or a divorced wife during 'idda).

Key Terms
تمليك العين (tamlik al-ayn): The transfer of ownership of a physical object (as opposed to a usufruct or debt). This is the essential quality required of the verbal expression used in a marriage contract according to the Hanafi school.
العارية (al-āriyya): A loan for use without compensation, conferring only ownership of usufruct, not the object itself. It is invalid for concluding marriage in the dominant Hanafi view.
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest, which defers ownership to after death. Marriage cannot be concluded with it in absolute terms, but if made immediate, it becomes metaphorical and valid.
القرينة (al-qarīna): A contextual indication that helps determine the intended meaning of a word (e.g., presence of witnesses, mention of dower). In marriage, it can resolve ambiguity in expressions like hiba.
الشبهة (al-shubha): A doubt regarding the legal permissibility of an act. In the context of an invalid marriage expression, if intercourse occurs, shubha prevents the application of the hadd penalty for zina, and only the lesser dower is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, can marriage be concluded with the word 'loan' (ariyya)?
A) Yes, because all expressions by which something is owned are valid.
B) Yes, because it confers ownership of usufruct.
C) No, because it does not confer ownership of the physical object.
D) No, because it is a deferred expression.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a marriage expression hinges on whether it immediately transfers full ownership of a physical object; words of usufruct or deferral are invalid, but they create a doubt that mitigates the hadd penalty.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage by the Word 'Hibah': Conditions and Proofs
Explains conditions for marriage using 'gift' and role of intention and witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the word 'hibah' constitutes a valid marriage contract in the Hanafi madhhab and identify the role of qarinah, intention, and witnesses.

Core Idea
Marriage by 'gift' is valid only when context or intention and witnesses direct it to marriage.

This lesson explores a specific Hanafi ruling: the word 'hibah' (gift) can be used to form a marriage contract if the circumstances or the parties' intention clearly indicate marriage. Unlike many other schools, the Hanafis do not restrict marriage to specific words like 'nikah' or 'tazwij'. However, this flexibility is not unrestricted; the presence of witnesses and a clear indication (qarinah) are essential. The text also discusses a special case: when the 'gift' is made to a free woman, the impossibility of literal ownership automatically forces a metaphorical interpretation as marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
Determine that the word 'hibah' can form a valid marriage (sahih) under specific conditions: presence of external indication (qarinah) or intention with witnesses.
The ruling is not absolute; it depends on contextual factors.
2
Understand the role of qarinah (external indication)
If the situation (e.g., witnesses, mention of dower) clearly indicates marriage, the word 'hibah' is automatically interpreted as a marriage contract.
Qarinah can be external (witnesses/dower) or intrinsic (impossibility of literal meaning).
3
Distinguish the role of intention (niyyah)
When no external qarinah exists, the parties' mutual intention to marry, along with witnesses, can direct the word to marriage.
Intention alone is not sufficient; witnesses are still required.
4
Analyze the special case of a free woman
When the donee is a free woman, the literal meaning (ownership) is impossible, so the word is automatically carried to its metaphorical meaning (marriage) without need for additional external indication.
This is a linguistic qarinah derived from the inability to own a free person.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and mufta bihi
Imam Muhammad reportedly held a stricter view (e.g., 'akhathtu' does not form marriage), but the relied-upon position allows hibah in the context of marriage with witnesses and intention.
The Fatawa al-Khaniyyah and al-Dhahiriyyah support the relaxed view.

كان الحال يدل على النكاح من إحضار الشهود وتسمية المهر مؤجلاً ومعجلاً ونحو ذلك ينصرف إلى النكاح، وإن لم يكن الحال دليلاً على النكاح، فإن نوى النكاح وصدقه الموهوب له فكذلك وينصرف إلى النكاح بقرينة النية، وإن لم ينو ينصرف إلى ملك الرقبة اهـ. فلم يشترط مع النية فهم الشهود ولا بد منه كما قدمناه بخلاف ما إذا أضيفت الهبة إلى الحرة فإنه ينعقد من غير هذه القرينة لأن عدم قبول المحل للمعنى الحقيقي وهو الملك للحرة يوجب الحمل على المجازي فهو القرينة فيكتفي بها الشهود حتى لو قامت قرينة على عدمة لا ينعقد به كما في الخانية وغيرها : لو طلب من امرأة الزنا فقالت وهبت نفسي منك فقال الرجل قبلت لا يكون نكاحاً وهو بمنزلة قول أبي البنت وهبتها منك لتخدمك فقال قبلت لا يكون نكاحاً اهـ. قال في الفتاوى : إلا إذا أراد به النكاح. فالحاصل أن النكاح ينعقد بالهبة إذا كان على وجه النكاح. وفي الظهيرية : لو قالت المرأة وهبت نفسي لك فقال الرجل أخذت قالوا لا يكون نكاحاً صحيحاً، وإنما استعيرت الهبة للنكاح وإن كانت لا تفيد الملك إلا بالقبض لأنها سبب موضوع للملك وإنما تأخر القبض لضعف السبب لتعريه عن العوض، وينعدم الضعف إذا استعملت في النكاح لأن العوض يجب بنفسه كذا في النهاية.
If the situation indicates marriage, such as the presence of witnesses and specification of dower deferred or prompt, etc., then it (the word) is directed to marriage. If the situation is not an indication of marriage, then if he intends marriage and the donee confirms, likewise it is directed to marriage by the evidence of intention. If he does not intend, it is directed to ownership of the slave. So intention alone without witnesses is not sufficient; witnesses are necessary as we have stated, unlike when the gift is made to a free woman, for it is concluded without this indication because the subject's lack of capacity for the literal meaning, which is ownership for the free woman, necessitates carrying it to the metaphorical, so that is the indication; witnesses suffice, so that if an indication of the opposite exists, it is not concluded. As in al-Khaniyyah and others: If a man asked a woman for zina and she said 'I gift myself to you' and the man said 'I accept,' it is not a marriage. And it is like the statement of the father of the girl: 'I gift her to you to serve you,' and he said 'I accept,' it is not a marriage. The Fatawa says: Except if he intended marriage. The conclusion is that marriage is concluded by gift if it is in the manner of marriage. In al-Dhahiriyyah: If a woman said 'I gift myself to you' and the man said 'I took,' they said it is not a valid marriage. Rather, 'gift' is used metaphorically for marriage, although it does not effect ownership except by possession, because it is a cause placed for ownership, and possession is delayed due to the weakness of the cause because it is free of consideration; the weakness ceases when it is used for marriage because the consideration is obligatory by itself. As in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when the word 'hibah' (gift) can be used to conclude a marriage contract. The author first states that if the situation (qarinah) clearly indicates marriage (e.g., witnesses present, dower mentioned), the word is interpreted as marriage. If there is no such external indication, then intention (niyyah) of the parties can direct it to marriage, but witnesses are still required. However, if the gift is directed to a free woman, the very fact that she cannot be owned forces a metaphorical interpretation as marriage, so witnesses suffice without need for additional indication. The text then gives examples where the word is not effective: a request for zina or for service. The key is that the gift must be intended as a marriage. The Dhahiriyyah source adds that even the word 'akhathtu' (I took) may not suffice unless the intention is clear. The ruling is that hibah can be a valid expression for nikah if it is used in the context of marriage, with witnesses and proper intention. This is an established Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): The word 'gift' used metaphorically for the marriage contract.
القرينة (al-qarinah): An external indication (e.g., witnesses, mention of dower) that directs the meaning to marriage.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention of the parties to enter into marriage; can substitute for external qarinah when no such qarinah exists.
الشهود (al-shuhud): Witnesses present at the marriage contract; a necessary condition for validity.
المعنى الحقيقي (al-ma'na al-haqiqi): Literal meaning of the word 'hibah' – transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, can the word 'hibah' (gift) be used to conclude a valid marriage contract?
A) No, only the words 'nikah' and 'tazwij' are valid.
B) Yes, but only if the woman is a slave.
C) Yes, but only if there is a clear indication (qarinah) of marriage, such as witnesses and mention of dower, and the parties intend marriage.
D) Yes, under any circumstances as long as the word 'gift' is used.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the word 'hibah' can form a valid marriage contract if used with a clear indication of marriage (qarinah) or with the intention of both parties, and witnesses are present.

Valid Expressions for the Marriage Contract Beyond Nikah and Tazwij
Lists additional words effecting marriage and discusses metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least five valid expressions for the marriage contract and state the conditions required for their validity per the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Many words can form a valid marriage if the context, witnesses, and dower clearly indicate intention.

The Hanafi madhhab is known for its flexibility in recognizing marriage contracts formed with words other than the explicit 'nikah' or 'tazwij'. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a variety of such expressions—from 'be my wife' to 'I take you back'—and clarifies the essential conditions that make them effective. Understanding this flexibility is crucial for correctly applying the law when marriage is expressed in non-standard yet clear language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
This passage lists various words (al-kawn, al-raj'a, arastuka, etc.) and states that when used with proper conditions, these conclude a valid marriage.
2
List the conditions
Dower (mahr) must be mentioned; witnesses must be present; the context (qarinah) must clearly indicate marriage.
Some expressions may require additional conditions from specific sources.
3
Note the metaphors
Words like hibah and wasiyyah can be used metaphorically for marriage if the intention is clear.
The author defends the validity of metaphor of metaphor against objections.
4
Recognize intra-madhhab differences
Some Hanafi scholars restrict raj'a to divorced women; others accept it for unmarried women with dower.
The mufta bihi is the wider acceptance.
5
Apply the principle
Any expression that clearly signifies marriage with the required conditions is valid, even if not among the three primary words.
This principle is derived from the overall flexibility of the school.

وفي الخانية : وكذا لو قالت المبانة لزوجها رددت نفسي عليك فهو بمنزلة الرجعة ينعقد به النكاح كما في الذخيرة ومنها ارفعها واذهب بها حيث شئت لما في الخانية : لو قال زوج ابنتك مني على كذا فقال أبوها بمحضر من الشهود ارفعها واذهب بها حيث شئت قال ابن الفضل : يكون نكاحاً. وجزم في الولوالجية بعدمه لاحتماله الوعد. ومنها ما في الخانية: لو قال أبو الصغير اشهدوا أني قد زوجت ابنة أحمد يريد به أبا الصغيرة من ابني فلان وقال لأبيها أليس هكذا؟ فقال أبوها هكذا ولم يزيدا على ذلك قالوا: الأولى أن يجدد النكاح وإن لم يجدد أجاز .اهـ. ومنها ما في الخانية أيضاً : لو قال رجل جئتك خاطباً ابنتك فقال الأب ملكتك كان نكاحاً. وفي الولوالجية : لو قال لها خطبتك إلى نفسي على ألف . فقالت قد زوجتك نفسي فهو نكاح جائز لأنه يراد به الإيجاب، وأما ما روي عن محمد لو قال أخطبك على ألف فقالت قد فعلت لم ينعقد حتى يقول الزوج قبلت فقد قال في المحيط والظهيرية : إنه محمول على ما إذا لم يرد به الحال. وفي الظهيرية : رجل أرسل رجلاً أن يخطب امرأة بعينها فزوجها الرسول إياه جاز لأن الخطبة جعلت نكاحاً إذا صدرت من الأمر فيكون الأمر بها أمراً بالنكاح.
ويشكل عليه ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية معزياً إلى السرخسي أن من قال إن خطبت فلانة أو قال كل امرأة خطبتها فهي طالق أن يمينه لا ينعقد لأن الخطبة عند العقد وهي العقد فلا يكون هذا اللفظ مضيفاً الطلاق إلى الملك. ووقع في بعض النسخ إن خطبت فلانة وتزوجتها فهي طالق ثلاثاً فأجاب على نحو ما ذكرنا فقال : إذا خطبها ثم تزوجها لا تطلق وهذا غلط، لأن مع حرف الواو تصير الخطبة مع الزوج شرطاً واحداً كما في قوله إن أكلت وشربت وأشباه ذلك فلا تنحل اليمين بالخطبة وحدها فإذا تزوجها بعد ذلك تنحل اليمين وهي في نكاحه فتطلق .اهـ. وذكر الولوالجي : إن تزوجت فلانة أو خطبتها فهي طالق فخطبها
وينبغي أن لا يختلف في صحته حينئذ وخالفهم في البحر فقال : المعتمد الإطلاق الخ. وأقول: معنى كونها مجازاً عن التمليك إذا قال الآن أي الخاص الذي هو النكاح لا المطلق فلا يرد أن المجاز لا مجاز له ا هـ.
وفي حاشية الرملي قال المقدسي في شرح الكنز المنظوم: وأما مجاز المجاز فيثبت عند من له اطلاع على كتب اللغة كالأساس وغيره، وتمامه فيه، وكتب على هامش نسخته البحر : هذا مردود لأن الوصية تمليك كما أن البيع والهبة كذلك وقد صح النكاح بلفظهما اتفاقاً فما الموجب لأن تجعل الهبة مجازاً عن التمليك ثم التمليك عن النكاح، بل نقول التمليك الذي هو وصية يجعل ابتداء عبارة عن النكاح، وكونها تمليكاً غني عن البيان، غايته أنه تمليك مخصوص بالأداء إلى ما بعد الموت فتجرد عن قيد الإضافة بالتقييد بالخال، فالظاهر ما ذكره في الظهيرية وقوله المجاز لا مجاز له مردود يعرف ذلك من طالع أساس البلاغة ا هـ . وفي شرح تنوير الأبصار صرح الجلال السيوطي في الإتقان بأن المجاز يكون له مجاز ومثل له بمثل ثمة فارجع إليه ا هـ . :قلت لكن قوله المصنف وما وضع لتمليك العين في الحال يخرج الوصية فإنها موضوعة لتمليك العين بعد الموت لا لمطلق التمليك فالفرق بينها وبين الهبة ظاهر، فإذا أريد من الوصية التمليك في الحال كان مجازاً، ثم إذا استعملت للنكاح كان مجازاً مبنياً على مجاز فلم يشمله قوله وضع لتمليك العين في الحال لأن إرادة التمليك في الحال بطريق المجاز لا بطريق الوضع إلا أن يقال : إنه مبني على أن المجاز موضوع أيضاً ويراد بالوضع ما يشمل الوضع الحقيقي والمجازي كما أجاب به بعضهم أو يقال : المراد بالوضع الاستعمال وهو شامل للمجاز أيضاً قوله : (ويشكل عليه ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية) قال في الرمز أقول : يدفع بأنها إنما تحمل على النكاح للقرينة الواضحة على ذلك بأن يكون في مجلس سبقه إشارة إلى الخطبة .
It is objected to the author that there are words besides these three by which marriage is concluded. Among them is 'kunna' (be): it is in al-Dhakhirah and others: if a man says to a woman 'be my wife for such-and-such' and she accepts, it is concluded, unlike if the woman says 'I am your wife' and he says 'yes', it is not valid. Also from al-Khaniyyah: if a woman says 'I have made you my husband' and the man accepts, it is concluded. Also the word 'raj'a' (return): in al-Waqi'at and al-Khaniyyah it is stated that marriage is concluded if he says to an unrelated woman 'I take you back' and she accepts, just as if he says to a divorced woman 'I take you back'. But al-Khaniyyah stipulates mention of dower; if no dower is mentioned, they said it is not a marriage. In al-Tajnis, mention of dower and intention of the husband are stipulated. Some differentiate between the unrelated woman and the divorced woman: it is concluded in the case of the divorced woman but not the unrelated woman, and this was deemed good in Fath al-Qadir. Also in al-Khaniyyah: likewise if the divorced woman says to her husband 'I have returned myself to you', it is like raj'a and marriage is concluded. Also the word 'irfa'ha wa dhhab biha haythu shi'ta' (take her and go with her wherever you wish). Ibn al-Fadl said it is a marriage. Al-Walwaljiyyah says it is not, because it may imply a promise. Also from al-Khaniyyah: if the minor's father says 'bear witness that I have married the daughter of Ahmad' meaning the minor girl to my son, and says to her father 'is it not so?' and he says 'it is so' and no more, they said it is better to renew the marriage, but if not renewed it is permitted. Also from al-Khaniyyah: if a man says 'I have come to propose your daughter' and the father says 'I have made you master', it is a marriage. In al-Walwaljiyyah: if he says to her 'I propose to you for a thousand' and she says 'I have married you to myself', it is a valid marriage because it is meant as an offer. As for what is narrated from Muhammad that if he says 'I propose to you for a thousand' and she says 'I have done it' it is not concluded until the husband says 'I accept', this is interpreted as the case when the situation does not indicate. In al-Dhahiriyyah: a man sent a man to propose a particular woman, and the messenger married her to him, it is valid because the proposal is made a marriage when it comes from the commander, so commanding it is commanding marriage. This is contradicted by the Fatawa al-Sayrafiyyah, attributed to al-Sarkhasi, that if one says 'if I propose to so-and-so' or 'every woman I propose to is divorced', his oath is not effective because the proposal at the time of the contract is the contract itself, so this word does not add divorce to ownership. In some copies, it is 'if I propose to so-and-so and marry her, she is thrice divorced'. He answered as we mentioned: if he proposes then marries, she is not divorced. This is a mistake, because with the conjunction 'and', the proposal and marriage become one condition, as in 'if I eat and drink', so the oath is not dissolved by proposal alone; then if he marries after that, the oath is dissolved and she is in his marriage and is divorced. Al-Walwaljiyyah mentioned: if he says 'if I marry so-and-so or propose to her, she is divorced', then he proposes... (fragment). Then: 'One should not differ on its validity then. In al-Bahr he contradicted them, saying the relied-upon is absolute. I say: the meaning of it being metaphorical for transfer of ownership is when he said 'now', i.e., the specific (marriage) not the absolute, so it is not objected that a metaphor cannot have a metaphor. In the Hashiyah of al-Ramli, al-Maqdisi said in Sharh al-Kanz al-Manzum: 'As for metaphor of metaphor, it is established for those familiar with language books like al-Asas etc.' On the margin of his copy of al-Bahr, it is written: 'This is rejected because the will is a transfer of ownership just as sale and gift are, and marriage is valid with their words by agreement. So what necessitates making gift metaphorical for transfer of ownership, and then transfer for marriage? Rather we say: transfer of ownership which is a will is made originally an expression for marriage. That it is a transfer is too obvious to need explanation. At most, it is a specific transfer that is executed after death, so it is stripped of the condition of immediacy by restricting it to the present. So the correct view is what is in al-Dhahiriyyah. His statement 'a metaphor cannot have a metaphor' is rejected; this is known by one who studies Asas al-Balagha. In Sharh Tanwir al-Absar, al-Suyuti explicitly states in al-Itqan that a metaphor can have a metaphor, and he gives an example, so refer to it. I say: But the author's statement 'and what is placed for transfer of ownership of an object immediately' excludes the will, because it is placed for transfer after death, not for absolute transfer. If the will is intended for immediate transfer, it is metaphorical. Then if it is used for marriage, it is a metaphor based on a metaphor. So it is not included in his statement. Unless it is said that it is based on the view that metaphor is also a linguistic usage, and 'placed' includes metaphorical usage, as some have answered, or it is said that by 'placed' he means 'used', which includes metaphor. His statement: 'It is contradicted by what is in the Fatawa al-Sayrafiyyah' and the response.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of valid expressions for marriage. It lists several words from other Hanafi sources that can form a marriage contract: 'be my wife', 'I have made you my husband', the word 'raj'a' for a divorced woman, 'take her and go', etc., each with conditions. It also addresses the issue of metaphor when using words like 'hibah' and 'wasiyyah' (will) for marriage. The author defends the validity of using such metaphorical expressions, citing language scholars and previous Hanafi authorities. The core point is that any expression that clearly indicates marriage with the appropriate conditions (such as witnesses, mention of dower, and intention) can be valid, even if it is not one of the three standard words (nikah, tazwij, and tamlik). The discussion about metaphor shows that the Hanafi school is flexible in recognizing contractual language as long as the intended meaning is clear.

Key Terms
الكون (al-kawn): The word 'be' used as an offer of marriage, e.g., 'be my wife'.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The word 'I take you back' used for marriage; originally it refers to taking back a divorced wife.
التمليك (al-tamlik): Making someone a master (e.g., 'I have made you master'); used metaphorically for marriage.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, e.g., using 'gift' to mean marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Any word that the parties agree on, without any conditions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab validates marriage contracts with a variety of expressions, provided the conditions of dower, witnesses, and clear context are met.

Validity of Marriage Contracts with Metaphorical and Indirect Wordings
Examines which wordings effectively create a valid marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which wordings can constitute a valid marriage contract in the Hanafi school and state the conditions that determine validity.

Core Idea
Marriage can be contracted with any wording that clearly indicates mutual consent, even without explicit 'zawwajtu' and 'tazawwajtu'.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on the validity of marriage contracts using words like 'gift', 'possession', or 'proposal'. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents several case studies and disagreements among Hanafi scholars, emphasizing that the intention and context determine whether a contract is formed. You will learn to apply the principle that the meaning prevails over literal wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
What wordings are sufficient for a valid marriage contract in Hanafi fiqh?
The answer is not limited to explicit 'zawwajtu'.
2
Examine the scenarios
List each scenario presented and note the scholar's ruling and reasoning.
Scenarios include 'take her and go', 'mallaktu', 'khatabtu' etc.
3
Distinguish between direct and metaphorical expressions
Understand why words like 'tamlik' (possession) are accepted as direct expressions, not mere metaphors.
The author argues that 'tamlik' can be a direct term for marriage.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Why do some scholars reject certain wordings (e.g., 'take her and go')?
Disagreement centers on whether the wording indicates immediate consent or a promise.
5
Apply the general principle
A valid marriage requires clear offer and acceptance; the specific words are secondary.
The mufta bihi position favors flexibility based on clear intention.

أخطبك على ألف فقالت قد فعلت لم ينعقد حتى يقول الزوج قبلت فقد قال في المحيط والظهيرية : إنه محمول على ما إذا لم يرد به الحال. وفي الظهيرية : رجل أرسل رجلاً أن يخطب امرأة بعينها فزوجها الرسول إياه جاز لأن الخطبة جعلت نكاحاً إذا صدرت من الأمر فيكون الأمر بها أمراً بالنكاح. ويشكل عليه ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية معزياً إلى السرخسي أن من قال إن خطبت فلانة أو قال كل امرأة خطبتها فهي طالق أن يمينه لا ينعقد لأن الخطبة عند العقد وهي العقد فلا يكون هذا اللفظ مضيفاً الطلاق إلى الملك. ووقع في بعض النسخ إن خطبت فلانة وتزوجتها فهي طالق ثلاثاً فأجاب على نحو ما ذكرنا فقال : إذا خطبها ثم تزوجها لا تطلق وهذا غلط، لأن مع حرف الواو تصير الخطبة مع الزوج شرطاً واحداً كما في قوله إن أكلت وشربت وأشباه ذلك فلا تنحل اليمين بالخطبة وحدها فإذا تزوجها بعد ذلك تنحل اليمين وهي في نكاحه فتطلق .اهـ. وذكر الولوالجي : إن تزوجت فلانة أو خطبتها فهي طالق فخطبها تسبق أن البيع والهبة كذلك وقد صح النكاح بلفظهما اتفاقاً فما الموجب لأن تجعل الهبة مجازاً عن التمليك ثم التمليك عن النكاح، بل نقول التمليك الذي هو وصية يجعل ابتداء عبارة عن النكاح، وكونها تمليكاً غني عن البيان، غايته أنه تمليك مخصوص بالأداء إلى ما بعد الموت فتجرد عن قيد الإضافة بالتقييد بالخال، فالظاهر ما ذكره في الظهيرية وقوله المجاز لا مجاز له مردود يعرف ذلك من طالع أساس البلاغة ا هـ . وفي شرح تنوير الأبصار صرح الجلال السيوطي في الإتقان بأن المجاز يكون له مجاز ومثل له بمثل ثمة فارجع إليه ا هـ . :قلت لكن قوله المصنف وما وضع لتمليك العين في الحال يخرج الوصية فإنها موضوعة لتمليك العين بعد الموت لا لمطلق التمليك فالفرق بينها وبين الهبة ظاهر، فإذا أريد من الوصية التمليك في الحال كان مجازاً، ثم إذا استعملت للنكاح كان مجازاً مبنياً على مجاز فلم يشمله قوله وضع لتمليك العين في الحال لأن إرادة التمليك في الحال بطريق المجاز لا بطريق الوضع إلا أن يقال : إنه مبني على أن المجاز موضوع أيضاً ويراد بالوضع ما يشمل الوضع الحقيقي والمجازي كما أجاب به بعضهم أو يقال : المراد بالوضع الاستعمال وهو شامل للمجاز أيضاً قوله : (ويشكل عليه ما في الفتاوى الصيرفية) قال في الرمز أقول : يدفع بأنها إنما تحمل على النكاح للقرينة الواضحة على ذلك بأن يكون في مجلس سبقه إشارة إلى الخطبة .
The Book of Marriage: (A man said) 'Give your daughter to me in marriage for a dowry of so many dirhams,' and her father said in the presence of witnesses, 'Take her and go wherever you wish.' Ibn al-Fadl said: It is a valid marriage. Al-Walwaliji affirmed its absence (of validity) because it is possible it was a promise. Among them is what is in al-Khaniyya: If the father of a young girl said, 'Bear witness that I have given in marriage the daughter of Ahmad' — meaning the father of the young girl to my son so-and-so — and said to her father, 'Is that not so?' and her father said 'Yes,' and they did not say more, they said: It is preferable to renew the marriage, but if they do not renew, it is permissible. And among them is also in al-Khaniyya: If a man said, 'I have come to you as a suitor for your daughter,' and the father said, 'I have given you possession,' it is a marriage. And in al-Walwalijiyya: If he said to her, 'I propose to marry you for one thousand,' and she said, 'I have married you to myself,' it is a valid marriage because what is meant is the offer. As for what is narrated from Muhammad, if he said, 'I propose to marry you for one thousand,' and she said, 'I have done so,' it does not take effect until the husband says 'I accept.' It is said in al-Muhit and al-Zahiriyya: It is interpreted as when the present tense is not intended. And in al-Zahiriyya: A man sent a man to propose to a specific woman, and the messenger married him to her; it is permissible because the proposal becomes a marriage when it comes from the command, so the command for it is a command for marriage. It is problematic with what is in al-Fatawa al-Sayrafiyya attributed to al-Sarakhsi, that if a man said, 'If I propose to so-and-so,' or said, 'Any woman I propose to is divorced,' his oath does not take effect because the proposal at the time of contract is the contract, so this wording does not attach divorce to ownership. And in some copies it occurs: 'If I propose to so-and-so and marry her, she is thrice divorced.' He answered in the manner we mentioned: if he proposes and then marries her, she is not divorced. This is an error, because with the conjunction 'and', the proposal and marriage become one condition, as in his statement 'If I eat and drink' and the like, so the oath is not dissolved by the proposal alone. If he marries her after that, the oath is dissolved and she is in his marriage, so she is divorced. And al-Walwaliji mentioned: If he said, 'If I marry so-and-so or propose to her, then she is divorced,' then he proposed to her — that sale and gift are similar, and marriage is valid by their wording by agreement. So what makes it necessary to treat gift as a metaphor for possession and then possession for marriage? Rather we say: possession that is bequest is made initially an expression for marriage, and its being possession is clear without explanation. At best it is a specific possession leading to after death; it is freed from the condition of addition by being restricted by the uncle (for example). So what is apparent is what al-Zahiriyya mentioned, and his statement 'a metaphor has no metaphor' is rejected; that is known to one who has studied 'Asas al-Balagha'. And in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar, Jalal al-Suyuti stated in al-Itqan that a metaphor can have a metaphor, and he gave an example of that, so refer to it. I say: But the author's statement 'And what is placed for possession of the eye immediately' excludes bequest, for it is placed for possession after death, not for absolute possession. So the difference between it and gift is clear. If from bequest is meant immediate possession, it is a metaphor; then if it is used for marriage, it is a metaphor based on a metaphor, so the author's statement 'placed for possession of the eye immediately' does not include it, because intending immediate possession through metaphor is not through original usage. Unless it is said: It is based on the idea that metaphor is also considered 'placed', and by 'placed' is meant what includes real and metaphorical placement, as some have answered. Or it is said: What is meant by 'placed' is usage, which includes metaphor. His statement: 'And it is problematic with what is in al-Fatawa al-Sayrafiyya.' He said in al-Ramz: I say the rebuttal is that it is interpreted as marriage due to the clear indication that it was preceded by a reference to the proposal.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of marriage contracts using various wordings, beyond the explicit 'zawwajtu' or 'tazawwajtu'. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq is expanding on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, using multiple Hanafi sources. The key issue is whether words like 'mallaktu' (I give possession), 'hibtu' (I give as gift), or 'khatabtu' (I propose) can establish a valid marriage when accompanied by appropriate intention and context. The passage presents several scenarios: (1) A father says 'Take her wherever you wish' – Ibn al-Fadl considers it a valid marriage, but al-Walwaliji rejects it as possibly a promise. (2) A father says 'I have given possession' to a suitor – that is a marriage. (3) A woman says 'I have married you to myself' in response to a proposal – valid because the proposal is the offer. However, if the man only says 'I propose' and the woman says 'I have done,' without explicit acceptance, the marriage is not complete until he says 'I accept' – this is interpreted as when the present tense is not intended. The passage also discusses the use of 'hiba' and 'wasiyya' as metaphors for marriage. The author argues that since sale and gift are valid for marriage by consensus, we treat possession (tamlik) as a direct expression for marriage, not as a metaphor. The claim that 'a metaphor has no metaphor' is rejected, citing Suyuti. The author concludes that the intention and clear indication matter most; words that convey the meaning of marriage are sufficient. The discussion emphasizes that in Hanafi law, the validity of marriage depends on the meaning conveyed, not just specific formulaic words.

Key Terms
خطبة (khitbah): In this passage, a proposal of marriage that can itself constitute part of the marriage contract if intended as such.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, here referring to using words like 'gift' to mean marriage.
تمليك (tamlik): Possession; in marriage context, it metaphorically refers to the transfer of marital authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the ruling when a man says to a woman's father 'I have come to you as a suitor for your daughter' and the father says 'I have given you possession' (mallaktu)?
A) The marriage is not valid because 'mallaktu' is not a proper word for marriage.
B) The marriage is valid because 'mallaktu' indicates possession, which in context means marriage.
C) The marriage is valid only if the daughter is present and consents.
D) The marriage is invalid because the father's response is considered a promise, not a contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a marriage contract depends on clear mutual consent and intention, not on the use of specific Arabic words.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Conditions of Witnesses for Marriage: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Conditions of witnesses and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for marriage witnesses and explain why the Hanafi school accepts sinners and those flogged for false accusation (qadhf) as valid witnesses, as opposed to the Shafi'i requirement of uprightness (adalah).

Core Idea
Hanafi allows sinners and those flogged for qadhf as marriage witnesses; Shafi'i requires uprightness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the qualifications of witnesses for a valid marriage contract according to the Hanafi school. It lists the conditions (free, sane, adult, Muslim) and explicitly includes open sinners (fasiq) and those who have been flogged for false accusation of adultery (mahdud). The author then engages in a dispute with the Shafi'i school, which requires witnesses to be upright. The core of the debate lies in the interpretation of the hadith 'La nikaha illa bi shuhud' and the scope of witness eligibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi conditions for witnesses
From the text, list the required attributes: free (two men or one man and two women), sane, adult, Muslim. Note that sinners and those flogged for qadhf are explicitly included.
The phrase 'wa law fasiqin aw mahdudin' indicates permissibility even before repentance, contrary to some later scholars.
2
Trace the dalil for witness requirement
The author cites the hadith 'No marriage except with witnesses' (لا نكاح إلا بشهود) narrated by Muhammad ibn al-Hasan. Understand that this is the primary evidence for the condition of witnesses.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but is acted upon by the Hanafi school.
3
Recognize the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school requires witnesses to be upright (adl). This restricts eligibility: sinners and those flogged for qadhf are not accepted unless they repent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The author rebuts the claim that the flogged must have repented. He argues that the general term 'fasiqin' already includes the specific 'mahdudin', and mentioning the specific after the general is not repetition but clarification, as occurs in the Quran.
Hanafi usul: عند اجتماع العام والخاص، يحمل العام على ما عدا الخاص if the specific is introduced by 'aw' (or), as in the text.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference: In Hanafi, a marriage witnessed by an unrepentant sinner or flogged person is valid; in Shafi'i, such marriage is invalid unless the witnesses repent beforehand.
This affects real-world nikah contracts, especially in communities where openly sinful behavior exists.

العين في الحال عند حرين أو حر وحرتين عاقلين بالغين مسلمين ولو فاسقين أو محدودين أو أعميين أو ابني العاقدين وصح تزوج مسلم ذمية عند ذميين ومن أمر رجلاً وتزوجها لم تطلق لأنه حين خطبها حنث لوجود الشرط فحين تزوجها تزوجها واليمين غير باقية اهـ. ومنها ما في الخلاصة : لو قال صرت لي أو صرت لك فإنه نكاح عند القبول وقد قيل بخلافه اهـ . ومنها ما في التتارخانية : لو قال لها يا عروسي فقالت لبيك انعقد لكن في في العقود للمعاني حتى في النكاح كما صرحوا به وهذه الألفاظ تؤدي معنى النكاح وهذا مما ظهر لي من فضله تعالى . زوجي (1) مني فقال قذف . قوله : (عند حرين أو حر وحرتين عاقلين بالغين مسلمين ولو فاسقين أو محدودين أو أعميين أو ابني العاقدين متعلق بـ«ينعقد بيان للشرط الخاص به وهو الإشهاد فلم يصح بغير شهود لحديث الترمذي البغايا اللاتي ينكحن أنفسهن من غير بينة» ولما رواه محمد بن الحسن مرفوعاً لا نكاح إلا بشهود فكان شرطاً ولذا قال في مآل الفتاوى : لو تزوج بغير شهود ثم أخبر الشهود على وجه الخبر لا يجوز إلا أن يجدد عقداً بحضرتهم اهـ. وفي الخانية والخلاصة : لو تزوج بشهادة الله ورسوله لا ينعقد ويكفر لاعتقاده أن النبي يعلم الغيب. وصرح في المبسوط بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان مخصوصاً بالنكاح بغير شهود ولا يشترط الإعلان مع قوله : (والجواب أن العبرة في العقود للمعاني الخ) يعني أن المصنف أراد لفظ النكاح والتزويج وما يؤدي معناهما. قال في النهر وفيه ما لا يخفى قول المصنف : (أو محدودين) أي في وقيده في النهر بقوله «وقد تابا قال : وهذا القيد لا بد منه وإلا لزم التكرار وفيه نظر، أما أولاً فلأن قوله «لا بد من هذا القيد ممنوع لأن المقصود من إطلاق المصنف الإشارة إلى خلاف الشافعي في الفاسق المظهر والمحدود قبل التوبة، وأما المستور والمحدود بعد التوبة فلا خلاف له فيهما كما في شرح المجمع والحقائق فظهر أن قوله لا بد من القيد فرية بلا مرية بل لا بد من اعتبار عدمه ومن ثم قال في البرهان : أو محدودين في قذف غير تائبين وأما ثانياً فلأن قوله «وإلا لزم التكرار» ممنوع أيضاً لأن المحدود في القذف أخص مطلقاً من الفاسقين ولم يقل أحد إن ذكر الخاص بعد العام تكرار كيف وهو واقع في كلام الله تعالى الذي هو في غاية الإعجاز على أنه قد صرح في الحواشي السعدية من كتاب الإكراه بأنه إذا قوبل الخاص بالعام يراد بالعام ما عدا الخاص. هذا ولا يخفى أن في عبارة قال المصنف عطف الخاص على العام بـ «أو» وهو مما تفردت به الواو و«حتى» كما في المغني حموي 6 (۱) رواه الترمذي في كتاب النكاح باب ١٥.
The eye immediately, in the presence of two free men, or one free man and two free women, sane, adult, Muslims, even if they are sinners, or flogged (for false accusation), or blind, or sons of the contracting parties. It is valid for a Muslim to marry a dhimmi woman in the presence of dhimmis. And if a man commanded another, and he married her, she is not divorced because when he proposed to her, he violated the oath due to the existence of the condition, so when he married her afterward, he married her while the oath was no longer in place. And among them is what is in al-Khulasah: If he said, 'You are for me' or 'You are for you,' it is a marriage upon acceptance, though some have said otherwise. And among them is what is in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If he said to her, 'O my bride,' and she said 'At your service,' it takes effect, but in al-Sayrafiyya it is contrary to the apparent narration. And among them is by hearing and obedience: if he said, 'Yourself by hearing and obedience,' it is a marriage, as in al-Khulasah. And among them is what is in al-Dhakhira: If he said, 'I have established my right over the benefits of your private part for one thousand,' and she said 'Yes,' the marriage is valid. The answer is that consideration in contracts is for meanings, even in marriage, as they have stated, and these words convey the meaning of marriage, and this is from His bounty upon me. My husband (?) from me, then he said slander. His statement: (in the presence of two free men, or one free man and two free women, sane, adult, Muslims, even if they are sinners, or flogged, or blind, or sons of the contracting parties) is attached to 'it takes effect' as a clarification of the specific condition, which is the witnessing. So it is not valid without witnesses, due to the hadith of al-Tirmidhi that 'the prostitutes are those who marry themselves without evidence' and due to what Muhammad ibn al-Hasan narrated as a marfu' hadith: 'No marriage except with witnesses.' So it is a condition. Hence it is said in Ma'al al-Fatawa: If a man marries without witnesses, then informs the witnesses by way of news, it is not valid unless he renews the contract in their presence. And in al-Khaniyya and al-Khulasah: If a man marries with the testimony of Allah and His Messenger, it does not take effect and he becomes an infidel because he believes that the Prophet knows the unseen. And it is stated in al-Mabsut that the Prophet was special in marrying without witnesses. And publicity is not a condition, along with his statement: (And the answer is that consideration in contracts is for meanings, etc.) Meaning that the author intended the word 'nikah' and 'tazwij' and what conveys their meaning. He said in al-Nahr: In it is what is not hidden. The author's statement: (or flogged) meaning those who have been flogged for false accusation. And in al-Nahr he restricted it with his statement 'if they have repented.' He said: This restriction is necessary, otherwise there would be repetition. There is a flaw in this: Firstly, because his statement 'this restriction is necessary' is not allowed, because the purpose of the author's absolute mention is to indicate the disagreement with al-Shafi'i regarding the open sinner and the one flogged before repentance. As for the concealed sinner and the one flogged after repentance, there is no disagreement about them, as in Sharh al-Majma' and al-Haqa'iq. So it becomes clear that his statement 'the restriction is necessary' is a falsehood without doubt; rather it is necessary to consider its absence. Hence it is said in al-Burhan: Or those flogged for false accusation who have not repented. Secondly, his statement 'otherwise there would be repetition' is also not allowed, because the one flogged for false accusation is absolutely more specific than sinners, and no one has said that mentioning the specific after the general is repetition; how can that be when it occurs in the speech of Allah, which is the pinnacle of inimitability? Moreover, it is stated in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya from the book of al-Ikrah that when the specific is contrasted with the general, the general is intended to exclude the specific. This is clear, and the author's wording uses the conjunction 'or' to connect the specific to the general, which is exclusive to 'waw' and 'hatta' as in al-Mughni by Hamawi. (1) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Marriage, Chapter 15.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions of witnesses (shuhud) for a valid marriage contract. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq is commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, which states the marriage must be 'in the presence of two free men or one free man and two free women, sane, adult, Muslims, even if they are sinners (fasiq) or have been flogged for false accusation (mahdud), or blind, or even if they are sons of the contracting parties.' The passage then clarifies that this is a condition for the contract: marriage without witnesses is invalid. It supports this with hadith evidence: 'No marriage except with witnesses' and a hadith about prostitutes marrying without evidence. It notes that even if witnesses are sinful or have been punished for slander, their testimony is acceptable. There is a special mention that a Muslim can marry a dhimmi woman in the presence of dhimmis. The passage also includes other rulings about marriage wordings, but the main focus is on witnesses. The author engages in an inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding the acceptability of open sinners and those flogged before repentance. The Shafi'i school requires uprightness in witnesses, while the Hanafi school allows the testimony of open sinners and those punished for qadhf, as long as they are not disqualified by disbelief. The commentary in al-Nahr raises a point about whether the flogged person must have repented; the author rebuts this, arguing that the absolute mention of 'mahdudin' is intended to include those who have not repented, contrary to al-Shafi'i view. He argues that the phrase 'or those flogged' is a specific mention after a general one (sinners), which is not repetition but emphasis, as occurs in the Qur'an. The passage ends with a note about the Prophet's special permission to marry without witnesses, but this is not applicable to the Ummah.

Key Terms
محدود (mahdud): A person who has been legally flogged for false accusation of adultery (qadhf).
فاسق (fasiq): A Muslim who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; an open sinner.
إشهاد (ishhad): The act of having witnesses present at the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage, is a marriage valid if the witnesses are two blind men?
A) No, because blindness disqualifies them from witnessing.
B) Yes, as long as they are sane, adult, Muslim, and free.
C) Yes, only if they are also upright in character.
D) No, because they cannot see the contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts sinners and those flogged for false accusation as marriage witnesses without requiring repentance, distinguishing itself from the Shafi'i school.
