beginner
8 min
Conditions of Marriage Witnesses in Hanafi Fiqh
States the qualifications required for marriage witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid marriage witnesses in Hanafi fiqh and identify which individuals qualify despite apparent disqualifications.

Core Idea
Marriage witnesses need only be free, sane, mature Muslims; even sinners and those punished for slander are accepted.

The validity of a marriage contract hinges on proper witnessing. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the baseline qualifications: freedom, sanity, maturity, and Islam. Importantly, it clarifies that apparent defects like open sinfulness, blindness, or being a son of the parties do not disqualify, and for a Muslim marrying a dhimmi, dhimmi witnesses suffice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential conditions
The matn lists the absolute requirements: free (or one free male and two free females), sane, mature, Muslim.
These are the baseline.
2
Notice the exceptions introduced by 'wa law'
The phrase 'ولو' indicates that sinners, those punished for slander, blind individuals, and sons of the parties are acceptable.
These are not disqualifying.
3
Apply the dhimmi exception
When a Muslim marries a dhimmi woman, the witnesses may be dhimmi (non-Muslim subjects of the Islamic state).
This is a specific exception to the requirement of Muslim witnesses.
4
Contrast with other schools
Unlike the Shafi'i school, which requires witnesses to be 'adl (upright), the Hanafi school allows even open sinners and those with a slander penalty.
The practical consequence is that more marriage witnesses are acceptable.
5
Summarize the outcome
If these conditions are met, the marriage contract is sahih (valid).
Witnesses are a condition (shart), not a pillar (rukn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العين في الحال عند حرين أو حر وحرتين عاقلين بالغين مسلمين ولو فاسقين أو محدودين أو أعميين أو ابني العاقدين وصح تزوج مسلم ذمية عند ذميين ومن أمر رجلاً وتزوجها لم تطلق لأنه حين خطبها حنث لوجود الشرط فحين تزوجها تزوجها واليمين غير باقية اهـ.
The condition in the present moment is: at two free men or one free man and two free women, sane, mature, Muslims, even if they are sinners or have been punished for slander, or are blind, or are sons of the two contracting parties. And the marriage of a Muslim to a dhimmī woman is valid with [the testimony of] two dhimmīs. And if a man commands [someone] and he marries her, it is not a divorce because when he proposed to her he broke his oath due to the existence of the condition, so when he married her, he married her while the oath was no longer remaining.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (matn) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It sets forth the conditions for valid witnesses in a marriage contract: they must be free (not slaves), of sound mind, mature (baligh), Muslim. The normal requirement is two free males, or one free male and two free females (the females standing in place of one male, as in other testimony). However, the matn clarifies that these witnesses are acceptable even if they are open sinners (fasiq), or have been punished for slander (hadd al-qadf), or are blind, or are the sons of the two parties contracting the marriage. It also rules that a Muslim man may marry a dhimmī woman with two dhimmī witnesses. The passage then addresses a specific case: if a man swore an oath to marry a woman secretly, but later proposed publicly (fulfilling the condition of the oath) and then married her with witnesses, the oath is broken at the proposal, so the marriage is not invalidated by the oath. The key point is that the witnesses need not be perfectly upright; even sinners suffice, which is a Hanafi position contrary to some other schools.

Key Terms
حر (ḥurr): A free person, not a slave. A slave's testimony is not accepted in marriage.
عاقل (ʿāqil): Sane, of sound mind. An insane person cannot be witness.
بالغ (bāligh): Reached puberty, mature. A child's testimony is invalid.
مسلم (muslim): Muslim. Non-Muslims cannot testify for a Muslim's marriage except in the case of a Muslim marrying a dhimmī.
فاسق (fāsiq): An open sinner who commits major sins or persists in minor sins. Their testimony is accepted for marriage in the Hanafi school.
محدود في القذف (maḥdūd fī al-qadhf): A person who has been punished (hadd) for slander (qadhf). Such a person's testimony is rejected in most matters, but accepted for marriage in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in the matn, which of the following sets of witnesses would make a marriage contract invalid?
A) Two free Muslim men who are blind and have been punished for slander.
B) One free Muslim man and two free Muslim women.
C) Two free Muslim men who are slaves.
D) Two free Muslim men who are the sons of the groom.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, marriage witnesses need only be free, sane, mature Muslims; even sinners and those with a slander penalty are acceptable, and for a Muslim-dhimmi marriage, dhimmi witnesses suffice.

Ruling on a Command to Marry and its Effect on an Oath
Ruling on a command to marry and its effect on an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a marriage performed after an oath-condition is fulfilled does not break the oath.

Core Idea
If a man's oath condition is met at proposal, the oath is already broken before marriage.

The matn concludes with a nuanced case: a man commands another to marry a woman, then later marries her himself. The text explains that when he proposed (khataba) to her, the condition of his oath was fulfilled, thus breaking the oath. Therefore, the subsequent marriage does not violate the oath because the oath no longer exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A man swore an oath about marriage under certain conditions, then commanded someone else to marry the woman, but later proposed to her himself.
The proposal is key.
2
Understand the condition fulfillment
The matn says: 'لأنه حين خطبها حنث لوجود الشرط' – he broke the oath at the moment of proposal because the condition of the oath existed.
The condition is the proposal itself.
3
Trace the consequence
When the oath is broken (ḥanath), the oath becomes null and void.
No further penalty or obligation.
4
Apply to the marriage
The later marriage contract does not break the oath because the oath was already extinguished.
The marriage is valid and unaffected.
5
Extract the principle
Fulfilling the condition of an oath at an earlier moment discharges the oath before the main act.
This prevents anachronistic violation.

Unusual Expressions in Nikah: The Maxim of Meanings
Lists unusual marriage expressions and gives the governing principle of meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ʿibra fī al-ʿuqūd li-l-maʿānī' to determine the validity of marriage contracts formed with unusual expressions.

Core Idea
Any expression that conveys the meaning of marriage forms a valid contract — even if not the explicit words 'nikah' or 'tazweej'.

In Hanafi fiqh, marriage contracts are not limited to specific verbal formulae. This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq lists several unusual expressions that scholars have ruled valid, grounding their validity in the broader principle that contracts are judged by their intended meanings. We will examine these expressions and the maxim that governs them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unusual expressions
List the four expressions mentioned: 'You have become mine' / 'I have become yours', calling her 'my bride' with response, 'Yourself by hearing and obedience', and 'My right in the usufruct of your private part is established for one thousand'.
Each expression conveys the idea of marriage without using the standard terms.
2
Understand the ikhtilaf
Note that some of these expressions are disputed; al-Ṣīrafiyya considers them against Ẓāhir al-Riwāya (the well‑known transmitted position).
The dispute is intra‑Hanafi, not across schools.
3
Extract the governing principle
The author states the maxim 'al-ʿibra fī al-ʿuqūd li-l-maʿānī' (consideration in contracts is given to meanings). This is the dalīl for validity.
This maxim is a fundamental tool in Hanafi contract law.
4
Apply the maxim to each expression
For each expression, confirm that it conveys the meaning of marriage (mutual consent to marriage, right to intimacy, etc.). The maxim renders the contract valid.
The author attributes this insight to divine grace.
5
Evaluate the authority level
The position is fatawa mutakhkhirin (later scholars). The earlier transmitted texts (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya) may not have included these expressions, but the principle of meanings overrides.
Cross‑madhhab: Shafi'is generally reject indirect expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما في الخلاصة : لو قال صرت لي أو صرت لك فإنه نكاح عند القبول وقد قيل بخلافه اهـ . ومنها ما في التتارخانية : لو قال لها يا عروسي فقالت لبيك انعقد لكن في الصيرفية أنه خلاف ظاهر الرواية ومنها بالسمع والطاعة لو قال نفسك بالسمع والطاعة فهو نكاح كما في الخلاصة ومنها ما في الذخيرة: لو قال ثَبَتَ حقي في منافع بضعك بألف فقالت نعم صح النكاح .اهـ والجواب أن العبرة في العقود للمعاني حتى في النكاح كما صرحوا به وهذه الألفاظ تؤدي معنى النكاح وهذا مما ظهر لي من فضله تعالى .
And among them is what is in al-Khulāṣa: If he said 'You have become mine' or 'I have become yours', it is a marriage upon acceptance, and it has been said contrary to it. And among them is what is in al-Tātārkhāniyya: If he said to her 'O my bride' and she said 'At your service', it is concluded, but in al-Ṣīrafiyya it is contrary to Ẓāhir al-Riwāya. And among them is by 'hearing and obedience': if he said 'Yourself by hearing and obedience', it is a marriage as in al-Khulāṣa. And among them is what is in al-Dhakhīra: If he said 'My right in the usufruct of your private part is established for one thousand' and she said 'Yes', the marriage is valid. And the answer is that consideration in contracts is given to meanings, even in marriage, as they have clearly stated, and these expressions convey the meaning of marriage, and this is what has appeared to me from His grace, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn, listing various unusual expressions that have been ruled to constitute a valid marriage contract according to Hanafi jurists. The expressions include: 'You have become mine' or 'I have become yours'; calling the woman 'my bride' and her responding 'at your service'; saying 'I give myself to you in hearing and obedience'; and a more explicit statement about establishing a right over the usufruct of her private parts for a dower. Some of these have disputed rulings, with some scholars considering them contrary to Ẓāhir al-Riwāya (the well-known transmitted position). The author then states the general principle: contracts are based on meanings, not just literal words. Therefore, any expression that conveys the meaning of marriage is valid, even if it is not the explicit words 'nikah' or 'tazweej'. This is a key Hanafi legal maxim that allows flexibility in contract formation. The author attributes this insight to divine grace.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya): The well-known transmitted position of the Hanafi school from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
العبرة في العقود للمعاني (al-ʿibra fī al-ʿuqūd li-l-maʿānī): The maxim that consideration in contracts is given to the intended meanings, not just the literal words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) I make you my wife in exchange for this dower

Key Takeaway
Marriage contracts in Hanafi fiqh are valid if they convey the meaning of marriage, regardless of the literal expression used.

Requirement of Witness Hearing and Understanding in Marriage
Discusses intra-Hanafi disagreement on witness hearing in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority position on witness hearing in marriage and identify the practical consequence of the disagreement with al-Sughdī.

Core Idea
Presence alone is insufficient; witnesses must hear and understand the contract for a valid marriage.

This passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq explores an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether witnesses in a marriage contract need to actually hear (samāʿ) and understand (fahm) the exchange, or if mere presence suffices. The author weighs the views of the majority against the lone dissenter, Qāḍī al-Imām ʿAlī al-Sughdī, and clarifies the relied-upon position. Understanding this ruling is crucial for determining the validity of a marriage when witnesses are sleeping, deaf, or do not understand the language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the specific dispute? Whether witnesses must hear the marriage utterance (ījāb/qabūl) or if presence alone suffices.
2
State the two positions
The majority (ʿāmma) requires hearing (samāʿ). Al-Sughdī says presence (ḥuḍūr) is enough.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? Deaf or sleeping witnesses: majority says no marriage, al-Sughdī says valid.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
Which view is stronger and why? The majority view is preferred because the purpose of presence is publicity and the ability to testify later.
5
Identify additional conditions
Both witnesses must hear simultaneously and understand the speech. This is part of the Hanafi madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صرح الانعقاد الشهود لما في التبيين أن النكاح بحضور الشاهدين يخرج عن أن يكون سراً ويحصل بحضورهما الإعلان .اهـ. ويستثنى منه مسألة اليمين لما في عدة الفتاوى : إذا حلف ليتزوجن سراً فتزوج بثلاثة شهود يحنث وبالشاهدين لا يحنث .اهـ. وأفاد المصنف أن الشهادة تشترط في الموقوف عند العقد لا عند الإجازة كما في المحيط، وأنَّ الحضور كافٍ لتعبيره بكلمة «عند» فلا يشترط السماع وفيه خلاف ; ففي الخانية وعامة المشايخ شرطوا السماع، والقائل بعدمه القاضي الإمام علي السغدي .اهـ. وثمرة الاختلاف تظهر في النائمين والأصمين فعلى قول العامة لا ينعقد النكاح بحضورهما، وعلى قول السغدي ينعقد وصحح قاضيخان في شرحه أنه لا ينعقد بحضرة الأصمين وجزم بأنه لا ينعقد بحضرة النائمين، وجزم في فتاواه بأنه لا ينعقد بحضرة النائمين إذ لم يسمعا كلامهما، فثبت بهذا أن الأصح ما عليه العامة كما به في التجنيس إذ المقصود من الحضور السماع، فقول الزيلعي ينعقد بحضرة النائمين على الأصح ولا ينعقد بحضرة الأصمين على المختار ضعيف بل لا فرق بينهما في عدم على الأصح لعدم السماع، ولقد أنصف المحقق الكمال حيث قال : ولقد أبعد الفقه الحكمة الشرعية من جوزه بحضرة النائمين .اهـ. واختلف في اشتراط سماع الشاهدين معاً فنقل في الذخيرة روايتين عن أبي يوسف وجزم في الخانية بأنه شرط فكان هو المذهب، فلو سمعا كلامهما متفرقين لم يجز ولو اتحد المجلس، فلو كان أحدهما أصم فسمع صاحب السمع يسمع الأصم حتى صاح صاحبه في أذنه أو غيره لا يجوز النكاح حتى يكون السماع معاً. كذا في الذخيرة واختلف أيضاً في فهم الشاهدين كلامهما فجزم في التبيين بأنه لو عقد بحضرة هنديين لم يفهما كلامهما لم يجز وصححه في الجوهرة. وقال في الظهيرية : والظاهر أنه يشترط فهم أنه نكاح واختاره في الخانية فكان هو المذهب . فالحاصل أنه يشترط سماعهما معاً مع الفهم على الأصح لكن في الخلاصة : إذا تزوج امرأة بالعربية والزوج والمرأة يحسنان العربية والشهود لا يعرفون العربية اختلف المشايخ فيه،
He stated that the contract is concluded with witnesses because, in al-Tabyīn, the marriage in the presence of two witnesses removes it from being secret and publicity is achieved by their presence. An exception is the case of the oath: as in ʿUddat al-Fatāwā: if he swore to marry secretly and then married with three witnesses, he breaks his oath; with two witnesses, he does not break the oath. The author indicates that testimony is required at the time of the contract, not at the time of ratification, as in al-Muḥīṭ, and that presence is sufficient due to his use of the word 'at' (ʿinda), so hearing is not required, but there is disagreement: in al-Khāniyya and the majority of scholars, hearing is required, while the one who says it is not required is Qāḍī al-Imām ʿAlī al-Sughdī. The fruit of the disagreement appears in the case of sleeping and deaf persons: according to the majority's view, marriage is not concluded in their presence; according to al-Sughdī, it is concluded. Qāḍīkhān corrected in his commentary that it is not concluded in the presence of deaf persons, and he asserted that it is not concluded in the presence of sleeping persons. He asserted in his Fatāwā that it is not concluded in the presence of sleeping persons because they did not hear the speech. So it is established that the correct view is that of the majority as in al-Tajnīs, for the purpose of presence is hearing. So the statement of al-Zaylaʿī that it is concluded in the presence of sleeping persons as the most correct view and not concluded in the presence of deaf persons as the chosen view is weak; rather, there is no difference between them in not concluding as the most correct view due to lack of hearing. And how just was al-Muḥaqqiq al-Kamāl when he said: 'How far from legal wisdom is the jurist who permits it in the presence of sleeping persons.' There is disagreement about the condition of both witnesses hearing together. In al-Dhakhīra, two narrations from Abū Yūsuf are transmitted, and he asserted in al-Khāniyya that it is a condition, so it is the madhhab. So if they heard the speech separately, it is not permissible even if the session was one. So if one of them was deaf and the hearing one made him hear until the other shouted in his ear or something, the marriage is not permissible until hearing occurs together. Likewise in al-Dhakhīra. They also disagreed about the witnesses' understanding of the speech. In al-Tabyīn, he asserted that if he contracted in the presence of two Indians who did not understand his speech, it is not permissible, and al-Jawhara corrected it. And in al-Ẓuhayriyya: it appears that understanding that it is a marriage is required, and al-Khāniyya chose it, so it is the madhhab. So the conclusion is that hearing together along with understanding is required on the most correct view. However, in al-Khulāṣa: if a man married a woman in Arabic and the husband and woman know Arabic but the witnesses do not know Arabic, the scholars differed:
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement for witnesses in marriage. While the basic condition is presence (ḥuḍūr), there is disagreement about whether the witnesses must actually hear (samāʿ) and understand (fahm) the marriage utterance. The majority of Hanafi scholars, including the authors of al-Khāniyya and the general authorities (ʿāmma), require hearing. The dissenter is Qāḍī al-Imām ʿAlī al-Sughdī, who holds that presence alone suffices. The practical consequence (thamaratu al-khtilāf) appears in the case of sleeping or deaf witnesses: according to the majority, no marriage is concluded because they did not hear; according to al-Sughdī, it is concluded because presence is sufficient. Qāḍīkhān and others reject al-Sughdī's view and assert that even the more correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that sleep and deafness both prevent the marriage. The passage also discusses a further requirement that both witnesses hear simultaneously (samāʿ maʿan). There are two narrations from Abū Yūsuf, and the relied-upon position (al-madhhab) is that simultaneous hearing is a condition. Additionally, the witnesses must understand (fahm) the speech; if they do not understand the language, the marriage is not valid. This is the preferred view. The passage ends with a reference to a difference of opinion when the witnesses do not know Arabic but the couple does. The overall thrust is that the Hanafi school requires not just physical presence, but actual hearing and understanding, for valid witness testimony in marriage.

Key Terms
السماع (al-samāʿ): Hearing by the witnesses of the spoken marriage contract (ījāb and qabūl).
الفهم (al-fahm): Understanding of the witnesses of the meaning of the contract, i.e., that it is a marriage.
الإعلان (al-iʿlān): Publicity of the marriage, which is achieved by the presence and hearing of witnesses.
النائمون (al-nāʾimūn): Sleeping persons. If witnesses are sleeping, they cannot hear, so marriage is invalid.
الأصمان (al-aṣammān): Deaf persons. Like the sleeping, they cannot hear, so marriage is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, if a marriage contract is concluded in the presence of two witnesses who are sleeping, the marriage is:
A) Valid, because their presence suffices
B) Invalid, because they did not hear the contract
C) Valid, but requires repeating the contract
D) Suspended until they wake up and confirm

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, valid witness testimony in marriage requires not just presence, but actual and simultaneous hearing and understanding of the contract.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Must Witnesses Hear the Marriage Contract?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on witness hearing requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi majority requiring hearing and al-Sughdi's minority view, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to scenarios involving deaf or sleeping witnesses.

Core Idea
Witnesses must hear the marriage contract; presence without hearing invalidates the marriage.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a subtle difference within the Hanafi school: does the condition 'presence of witnesses' in the marriage contract require them to actually hear the offer and acceptance? The majority say yes; a minority says no. The practical consequence is critical when witnesses are asleep or deaf. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What is the exact point of disagreement between al-Sughdi and the majority? Note that both agree witnesses must be present, but they differ on whether hearing is required.
Al-Sughdi takes the literal wording of 'at' ('inda) to mean physical presence only.
2
Understand the majority's proof
The majority use the oath exception as a proof: if someone vows to marry secretly, marrying with three witnesses breaks the oath but with two does not. This shows that two witnesses are the minimum for a valid marriage but three are needed for publicity (i'lan). Hearing is essential for i'lan.
This is a dalil aqli based on the maqasid of shari'ah.
3
Apply the thamarah
Consider witnesses who are sleeping or deaf. According to the majority, the marriage is invalid because they do not hear. According to al-Sughdi, it is valid because they are present.
Qadikhan and later authorities confirm no difference between sleeping and deaf witnesses – both fail to hear.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
Why does the majority view prevail? Because the purpose (maqsad) of witnesses is i'lan, and i'lan requires hearing. Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam and others strongly criticize the minority view as departing from legal wisdom.
The mufta bihi is the majority view; it is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
5
Formulate the ruling
State the mufta bihi: For a marriage contract to be valid, the witnesses must hear the offer and acceptance simultaneously. If they do not hear, the marriage is not contracted.
This applies whether witnesses are asleep, deaf, or otherwise unable to hear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستثنى منه مسألة اليمين لما في عدة الفتاوى : إذا حلف ليتزوجن سراً فتزوج بثلاثة شهود يحنث وبالشاهدين لا يحنث .اهـ. وأفاد المصنف أن الشهادة تشترط في الموقوف عند العقد لا عند الإجازة كما في المحيط، وأنَّ الحضور كافٍ لتعبيره بكلمة «عند» فلا يشترط السماع وفيه خلاف ؛ ففي الخانية وعامة المشايخ شرطوا السماع، والقائل بعدمه القاضي الإمام علي السغدي .اهـ. وثمرة الاختلاف تظهر في النائمين والأصمين فعلى قول العامة لا ينعقد النكاح بحضورهما، وعلى قول السغدي ينعقد وصحح قاضيخان في شرحه أنه لا ينعقد بحضرة الأصمين وجزم بأنه لا ينعقد بحضرة النائمين، وجزم في فتاواه بأنه لا ينعقد بحضرة النائمين إذ لم يسمعا كلامهما، فثبت بهذا أن الأصح ما عليه العامة كما به في التجنيس إذ المقصود من الحضور السماع، فقول الزيلعي ينعقد بحضرة النائمين على الأصح ولا ينعقد بحضرة الأصمين على المختار ضعيف بل لا فرق بينهما في عدم على الأصح لعدم السماع، ولقد أنصف المحقق الكمال حيث قال : ولقد أبعد الفقه الحكمة الشرعية من جوزه بحضرة النائمين .اهـ.
And an exception is made from it the issue of the oath, as stated in several fatwas: If one swore to marry secretly and then married with three witnesses, he breaks his oath; with two witnesses, he does not break his oath. End. And the author indicated that testimony is required at the time of the suspended contract, not at the time of permission, as in al-Muhit, and that presence is sufficient because he used the word 'at' so hearing is not required, but there is disagreement: In al-Khaniyyah and the majority of the scholars, they required hearing, and the one who said it is not required is al-Qadi Imam Ali al-Sughdi. End. And the practical consequence of the difference appears in the cases of sleeping and deaf persons: according to the majority, marriage is not contracted by their presence; according to al-Sughdi, it is contracted. Qadikhan in his commentary corrected that it is not contracted in the presence of deaf persons, and he firmly stated that it is not contracted in the presence of sleeping persons, and he firmly stated in his Fatwas that it is not contracted in the presence of sleeping persons because they did not hear their speech. So it is established by this that the correct view is what the majority hold, as in al-Tajnis, for the purpose of presence is hearing. Therefore, the statement of al-Zayla'i that it is contracted in the presence of sleeping persons according to the most correct view, and not contracted in the presence of deaf persons according to the chosen view, is weak; rather, there is no difference between them in non-contraction according to the most correct view due to lack of hearing. And the verifier al-Kamal was fair when he said: 'How far from legal wisdom is the one who permitted it in the presence of sleeping persons.' End.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the condition of witnesses in marriage. The base text (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that presence of witnesses is sufficient, but the commentator explains that the majority of Hanafi scholars require hearing (sama') the contract, not just physical presence. The dissenter is al-Qadi Ali al-Sughdi, who says presence alone is enough. The practical difference (thamara) appears in cases where witnesses are sleeping or deaf: according to the majority, the marriage is invalid because they did not hear; according to al-Sughdi, it is valid. Qadikhan and others favor the majority view, emphasizing that the purpose of having witnesses is to achieve publicity (i'lan), which requires hearing. The commentator strengthens this by quoting al-Kamal ibn al-Humam, who criticizes those who permit marriage with sleeping witnesses. Thus, the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that witnesses must hear the contract simultaneously.

Key Terms
السماع (al-sama'): Hearing the words of offer and acceptance by the witnesses directly and simultaneously.
الحضور (al-hudur): Physical presence of witnesses at the location of the contract, regardless of hearing.
ثمرة الاختلاف (thamarat al-ikhtilaf): The practical legal consequence that arises from the juristic disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it is valid because presence is sufficient.
B) No, it is invalid because hearing is required.
C) It is valid only if the witnesses are sleeping.
D) It depends on whether the witnesses are deaf.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a marriage contract in the Hanafi school requires that witnesses actually hear the offer and acceptance; mere physical presence is insufficient.

Witness Conditions: Simultaneous Hearing and Understanding
An expansion on witness conditions for nikah: simultaneous hearing and understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two additional witness conditions for a valid Hanafi nikah (simultaneous hearing and understanding) and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement regarding understanding.

Core Idea
Both witnesses must hear simultaneously and understand the contract — without this, the nikah is invalid.

In the previous lesson, we learned that witnesses must be free, sane, Muslim adults. But even with such witnesses, the marriage contract may be invalid if they do not hear the offer and acceptance simultaneously or if they do not understand that the words constitute a marriage. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores these two additional conditions and the internal Hanafi disagreements about them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage focuses on two specific witness requirements: (1) both witnesses must hear the offer and acceptance at the same moment (al-sama' ma'an), and (2) they must understand that the words constitute a marriage (al-fahm).
2
Analyse the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on simultaneous hearing
Al-Dhakhirah reports two narrations from Abu Yusuf — one requiring simultaneity and one possibly not. However, al-Khaniyyah selects the requirement, making it the relied-upon view. This shows how later Hanafi authorities resolved differences within the school.
The example of a deaf witness being made to hear by shouting does not achieve simultaneity because the hearing events are not truly simultaneous.
3
Analyse the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on understanding
Most authorities (al-Tabyin, al-Jawharah, al-Khaniyyah) require that witnesses understand they are witnessing a marriage. However, al-Khulasah reports a contrary view: if the parties speak Arabic and witnesses do not, the marriage may still be valid. The commentator resolves this by noting the most correct position (al-asahh) is that understanding is required.
The dispute hinges on whether the purpose of testimony (i'lan) can be achieved without linguistic comprehension.
4
Consider the exception for intoxicated witnesses
The passage states that if drunk witnesses understand the marriage at the time, their testimony is valid even if they forget later. This reinforces the principle that the key is comprehension at the moment of contracting, not subsequent memory.
If a drunk witness does not understand, they are likened to the insane — their presence is ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في اشتراط سماع الشاهدين معاً فنقل في الذخيرة روايتين عن أبي يوسف وجزم في الخانية بأنه شرط فكان هو المذهب، فلو سمعا كلامهما متفرقين لم يجز ولو اتحد المجلس، فلو كان أحدهما أصم فسمع صاحب السمع يسمع الأصم حتى صاح صاحبه في أذنه أو غيره لا يجوز النكاح حتى يكون السماع معاً. كذا في الذخيرة واختلف أيضاً في فهم الشاهدين كلامهما فجزم في التبيين بأنه لو عقد بحضرة هنديين لم يفهما كلامهما لم يجز وصححه في الجوهرة. وقال في الظهيرية : والظاهر أنه يشترط فهم أنه نكاح واختاره في الخانية فكان هو المذهب . فالحاصل أنه يشترط سماعهما معاً مع الفهم على الأصح لكن في الخلاصة: إذا تزوج امرأة بالعربية والزوج والمرأة يحسنان العربية والشهود لا يعرفون العربية اختلف المشايخ فيه، والأصح أنه ينعقد اهـ. فقد اختلف التصحيح في اشتراط الفهم وفي الخلاصة وغيرها ينعقد بحضرة السكارى إذا فهمو النكاح وإن لم يذكروا بعد الصحو، وينبغي أن لا يشترط فهمهم على القول بعدم اشتراطه إلا أن يقال : إنه عند عدم الفهم ملحق بالمجنون في حق هذا الحكم لعدم التمييز.
And there is disagreement about the condition of the two witnesses hearing together. It is narrated in al-Dhakhirah two narrations from Abu Yusuf, and it is firmly stated in al-Khaniyyah that it is a condition, so that is the madhhab. If they hear their speech separately, it is not valid even if the session is one. If one of them is deaf and the hearing person causes the deaf person to hear even by shouting in his ear or otherwise, the marriage is not valid until the hearing is together. Similarly in al-Dhakhirah. And there is also disagreement about the two witnesses understanding their speech. It is firmly stated in al-Tabyin that if a contract is made in the presence of two Indians who do not understand their speech, it is not valid, and it was corrected in al-Jawharah. And in al-Zahiriyyah: 'The apparent view is that understanding that it is a marriage is required,' and it was chosen in al-Khaniyyah, so that is the madhhab. The conclusion is that both hearing together and understanding are required according to the most correct view. However, in al-Khulasah: If a man marries a woman in Arabic, and the husband and woman know Arabic, but the witnesses do not know Arabic, the scholars differed, and the most correct view is that it is valid. End. So there is difference in correction about the condition of understanding. In al-Khulasah and elsewhere, it is valid in the presence of drunk people if they understand the marriage even if they do not remember after sobriety. It should be that their understanding is not required according to the view that it is not required, unless it is said that when there is no understanding, they are attached to the insane in this ruling due to lack of discernment.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on witness conditions, now focusing on two additional requirements: (1) both witnesses must hear the contract simultaneously, and (2) they must understand that the words constitute a marriage. The commentator cites a transmission from Abu Yusuf that simultaneous hearing is required — if one hears before the other, it is invalid even in the same session. For example, if one witness is deaf and the other helps him hear by shouting, the marriage is still invalid because the hearing is not truly simultaneous. Regarding understanding, the majority of Hanafi scholars (as in al-Tabyin, al-Jawharah, and al-Khaniyyah) require that the witnesses comprehend that the speaker is contracting marriage, even if they do not understand the exact language. However, there is a contrary view in al-Khulasah that if the witnesses do not know Arabic but the parties do, the marriage is valid. The commentator notes that the most correct position is that understanding is required. Drunk witnesses are considered as long as they understand at the time, otherwise they are like insane persons who lack discernment. Thus, the Hanafi school holds that witnesses must have the capacity to understand the nature of the contract.

Key Terms
السماع معاً (al-sama' ma'an): Both witnesses hearing the offer and acceptance at the same moment.
الفهم (al-fahm): Understanding that the words spoken constitute a marriage contract.
السكارى (al-sukara): Intoxicated persons; their presence as witnesses is considered if they understand the contract at the time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The marriage is invalid because they did not hear together.
D) The marriage is valid if there are more than two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
For a valid Hanafi marriage, both witnesses must hear the offer and acceptance simultaneously and understand that a marriage contract is being made; a mere presence is insufficient.

Witness Understanding and Bride Identification in Hanafi Nikah
Ruling on witness understanding and bride identification for marriage validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on witness understanding and identify the conditions for bride identification that eliminate jahala in marriage contracts.

Core Idea
A marriage is valid only if witnesses can identify the bride, eliminating ignorance (jahala).

Marriage witnesses must not only be present but also have sufficient awareness and knowledge of the bride. This lesson examines when intoxicated witnesses are acceptable and, crucially, how witnesses must be able to identify the bride—whether she is present, veiled, hearing her voice, or absent. The Hanafi school provides detailed scenarios to ensure the contract is free from uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue of witness understanding
The passage first addresses whether witnesses must understand the proceedings. Note the correct view: marriage is valid with intoxicated witnesses if they understood at the time, even if they later forget. Understanding is not a separate condition, but complete lack of discrimination (like a madman) invalidates.
The key is tamyiz (discrimination) at the time of contract.
2
Examine the requirement of tamayyuz al-mankuha
The witnesses must know the identity of the bride to prevent jahala (ignorance). This is a condition for validity. The passage then gives various scenarios to illustrate what suffices.
The principle: any identification that removes uncertainty fulfills the condition.
3
Analyze scenarios: bride present and veiled
If the bride is present but veiled, pointing to her suffices. Caution suggests uncovering her face, but pointing is legally sufficient.
Physical presence allows visual identification even if veiled.
4
Analyze scenarios: bride not seen but heard
If the bride is in a house alone and witnesses hear her voice, identification is established. But if another woman is present, voice alone does not remove jahala.
The critical factor is uniqueness of identification.
5
Analyze scenarios: bride absent with proxy
If the bride is absent and her proxy marries her, the witnesses must know her. If they know her, name alone suffices; otherwise, full name (her father's and grandfather's names) is required. Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with al-Khasaf.
Al-Khasaf's lenient view is noted but mufta bihi is stricter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصح أنه ينعقد اهـ. فقد اختلف التصحيح في اشتراط الفهم وفي الخلاصة وغيرها ينعقد بحضرة السكارى إذا فهمو النكاح وإن لم يذكروا بعد الصحو، وينبغي أن لا يشترط فهمهم على القول بعدم اشتراطه إلا أن يقال : إنه عند عدم الفهم ملحق بالمجنون في حق هذا الحكم لعدم التمييز. ولا بد من تمييز المنكوحة عند الشاهدين لتنتفي الجهالة، فإن كانت حاضرة متنقبة كفى الإشارة إليها والاحتياط كشف وجهها، فإن لم يروا شخصها وسمعوا كلامها من البيت إن كانت المرأة في البيت وحدها جاز النكاح لزوال الجهالة، وإن كان معها امرأة أخرى لا يجوز لعدم زوالها. وكذا إذا وكلت بالتزويج فهو على هذا التفصيل، وإن كانت غائبة ولم يسمعوا كلامها بأن عقد لها وكيلها فإن كان الشهود يعرفونها كفى ذكر اسمها إذا علموا أنه أرادها، وإن لم يعرفوها لا بد من ذكر اسمها واسم أبيها وجدها. وجوز الخصاف النكاح لو وكلته فقال بحضرتهما زوجت نفسي من موكلتي أو من امرأة جعلت أمرها بيدي فإنه يصح عنده قال قاضيخان والخصاف كان كبيراً في العلم يجوز الاقتداء به وذكر الحاكم الشهيد في المنتقى كما قال الخصاف اهـ وفي الخلاصة: إذا زوجها أخوها فقال زوجت أختي ولم يسمها جاز إن كانت له أخت واحدة فإن كان له أختان فسماها جاز .
And the most correct is that it is concluded. So verification differed about stipulating understanding. In al-Khulasa and others: it is concluded in the presence of drunkards if they understood marriage even if they did not remember after sobriety, and it is appropriate that their understanding should not be stipulated according to the view that it is not stipulated, unless it is said that when there is no understanding, he is like a madman in this ruling due to lack of discrimination. And identification of the married woman before the witnesses is necessary to eliminate ignorance. If she is present and veiled, pointing to her suffices, and caution is to uncover her face. If they did not see her person but heard her voice from the house, if the woman is alone in the house, the marriage is valid due to removal of ignorance; if another woman is with her, it is not valid because ignorance is not removed. Similarly, if she appoints a proxy for marriage, the same detail applies. If she is absent and they did not hear her voice, and her proxy contracted on her behalf, then if the witnesses know her, mentioning her name suffices if they know that he intended her; if they do not know her, it is necessary to mention her name, her father's name, and her grandfather's name. Al-Khasaf permitted the marriage if a man was her proxy and said in their presence: I married myself from my client or from a woman whose affair was put in my hand, for it is valid according to him. Qadi Khan said: al-Khasaf was great in knowledge, it is permissible to follow him. Al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned in al-Muntaqa [the same] as al-Khasaf said. And in al-Khulasa: if her brother married her and said 'I married my sister' without naming her, it is valid if he has only one sister; if he has two sisters and he named her, it is valid.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the conditions related to witnesses' understanding and identification of the bride in marriage. The author begins by noting a difference of opinion on whether witnesses must understand the proceedings. The correct view is that marriage is valid even with intoxicated witnesses if they understood at the time, even if they forget later; understanding is not a separate condition. However, if they completely lack understanding, they are like a madman and the marriage would not be valid due to lack of discrimination. The core requirement is that the witnesses must know the identity of the bride (tamayyuz al-mankuha) to eliminate jahala (ignorance). If the bride is present and veiled, pointing suffices, but caution is to uncover her face. If they hear her voice from a room alone, it suffices; if another woman is present, identification is uncertain and marriage invalid. For an absent bride, if witnesses know her, mentioning her name is enough; otherwise, full name and lineage. Al-Khasaf allowed marriage with minimal identification in certain proxy cases, and Qadi Khan considers his opinion reliable enough to follow. The brother case illustrates: if a brother marries his sister without naming her, it is valid only if he has one sister; if multiple, naming is necessary. All these rules aim to ensure witnesses can identify the bride, removing jahala.

Key Terms
الجهالة (al-jahala): Ignorance of the identity of the bride or groom, which would invalidate the marriage contract due to uncertainty.
تمييز المنكوحة (tamayyuz al-mankuha): Distinguishing and identifying the woman being married, so that the witnesses know specifically who she is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Her name only
B) Her name and her father's name
C) Her name, her father's name, and her grandfather's name
D) A physical description

Key Takeaway
Witnesses must be able to identify the bride uniquely to eliminate jahala; the required detail depends on whether she is present, heard, or absent and known.

Marriage by Correspondence: Witnessing Requirements
Ruling on marriage by letter with intra-Hanafi nuance on witnessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi views on witnessing requirements for marriage by correspondence and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A marriage by letter is valid; witnesses need not hear the letter if the command is tawkil.

Marriage by correspondence raises a unique question: must the witnesses hear the letter being read, or is the woman's acceptance in their presence enough? The answer depends on how we interpret the man's written command. This passage from Bahr al-Raiq unpacks the nuance between treating the letter as an agency (tawkil) or a direct offer (ijab), and shows how the preferred Hanafi view simplifies the witnessing requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage addresses marriage contracted through a letter (kitab) and whether witnesses must hear its reading.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
Note the two views: (1) the written command is a tawkil (agency) – the woman acts as agent; (2) it is an ijab (direct offer). The ruling on witnessing differs accordingly.
3
Link interpretation to witnessing requirement
If tawkil, the woman's acceptance in front of witnesses suffices; the letter need not be read aloud. If ijab, witnesses must hear the reading for the offer to be established.
The author leans towards tawkil as the mufta bihi.
4
Apply the mufta bihi position
In practice, the relied-upon view (tawkil) means that as long as the woman accepts in the presence of two witnesses, the marriage is valid even if the letter is not read to them.
This eases the witnessing process.
5
Compare with other schools
Note that the Shafi'i school requires witnesses to hear both offer and acceptance directly, rejecting this distinction.
Cross-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد المصنف أن انعقاد النكاح بكتاب أحدهما يشترط فيه سماع الشاهدين قراءة الكتاب مع نفسك قبول الآخر كما قدمناه لكن في الظهيرية وفي النكاح سواء كتب زوجي الكتاب فقالت زوجت أو كتب تزوجتك وبلغها الكتاب فقالت زوجت نفسي جاز، لكن في الوجه الأول لا يشترط إعلامها الشهود وفي الوجه الثاني يشترط اهـ. فقولهم يشترط حضورهما وقت قراءة الكتاب ليس على إطلاقه وهو مبني على أن صيغة الأمر توكيل، مطلقاً فقولها زوجت نفسي منه قائم مقام الإيجاب والقبول فاكتفي بسماعه ولا يشترط الإشهاد على التوكيل، وأما على قول من جعل الأمر إيجاباً فلا بد من سماع قراءة الكتاب كما لا يخفى.
The author [of Kanz al-Daqaiq] informed that the conclusion of marriage by the book of one of them requires the witnesses to hear the reading of the book along with the acceptance of the other, as we previously presented. But in al-Zahiriyyah and in [the book] al-Nikah: whether he wrote 'I married my wife' and she said 'I married', or he wrote 'I married you' and the book reached her and she said 'I married myself', it is valid. However, in the first case it is not required to inform the witnesses, while in the second case it is required. So their saying that their presence at the time of reading the book is not absolute; it is based on that the command formula is tawkil (agency) absolutely. Thus her saying 'I married myself from him' stands in place of offer and acceptance, so hearing from him suffices, and witnessing upon the agency is not required. But according to the view that the command is an offer (ijab), it is necessary to hear the reading of the book, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage addresses marriage through correspondence (kitab). The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that if one party writes a letter proposing marriage, the witnesses must hear the reading of the letter along with the acceptance by the other party. However, the author then notes a nuance from al-Zahiriyyah: if the man writes 'I married you' and the woman writes back 'I married myself', or if the man writes 'I married my wife' and she verbally accepts, the ruling depends on whether the written command is considered tawkil (agency) or ijab (direct offer). If it is tawkil, the woman's acceptance in front of witnesses suffices without them hearing the reading again; if it is ijab, they must hear the reading. The author concludes that the prevailing view is that the command form is tawkil, so witnesses do not need to hear the reading, but the conservative view requires it. The commentary clarifies that the requirement of witnesses present at reading is not absolute.

Key Terms
التوكيل (al-tawkil): Agency: the written command is treated as appointing the woman as agent to accept the marriage on her own behalf.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): Offer: treating the written command as a direct proposal of marriage from the man.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position in this passage, what is required for the validity of a marriage contracted by a letter from the husband?
A) The witnesses must hear the letter being read aloud and understand it
B) The woman's acceptance in the presence of witnesses is sufficient, without hearing the letter
C) The letter must be written in the presence of witnesses
D) The woman must verbally accept in the presence of the husband

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers to interpret a written marriage command as tawkil, so witnesses need only hear the woman's acceptance, not the letter.

Conditions of Witnesses in Marriage: Validity vs. Proof
Explaining conditions for witnesses and the two rulings of marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions required for witnesses in a marriage contract and distinguish between the rulings of validity (in'aqad) and proof (izhar).

Core Idea
A marriage is valid with minimal witnesses but only provable with reliable ones.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the conditions for witnesses in marriage and introduces a critical distinction: the validity of the contract (in'aqad) and its proof in court (izhar). You will learn the four required qualities (freedom, sanity, puberty, Islam) and why even sinful or blind witnesses can make the contract valid—but not provable in case of denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions for witnesses
List the four shurut that the witnesses must possess for the marriage to be valid (in'aqad): freedom, sanity, puberty, and Islam. Note that the author did not require speech, as a deaf-mute who understands qualifies.
These conditions apply at the time of the contract.
2
Distinguish between the two rulings
Understand that the marriage has two separate legal aspects: hukm al-in'aqad (validity) and hukm al-izhar (proof/testimony). The first relates to the contract itself, the second to its establishment in court when one party denies it.
The passage states that for izhar, only witnesses whose testimony is generally accepted in other rulings are accepted.
3
Apply the principle of analogy
Learn the principle: anyone fit to be a guardian (wali) by his own right is fit to be a witness. This excludes the mukatab (who has authority derived from his master).
This principle helps determine who can be a witness for validity.
4
Examine the purpose of witnessing
The goal of witnesses is to repel suspicion of adultery, not to preserve the contract against denial. Their presence makes the marriage publicly known, which later allows hearsay testimony.
This explains why even inadmissible witnesses can fulfill this purpose.
5
Apply the distinction to specific cases
Work through examples from the text: sinful persons, blind persons, those punished for slander, sons of the contracting parties, and enemies—all can make the marriage valid, but their testimony is not accepted for izhar.
The exception: yet if the marriage becomes well-known, reliable witnesses can testify by hearsay.

Four conditions are stipulated in witnesses: freedom, sanity, puberty, and Islam. So marriage is not concluded in the presence of slaves, insane persons, children, and disbelievers in the marriage of Muslims because they have no authority. There is no difference in the slave between a qinn, mudabbar, and mukatab. If the slaves are freed or the children reach puberty after bearing [the testimony] and then testify, if there was someone else at the time of the contract from whom the contract was concluded, their testimony is valid because they are eligible for bearing and the contract was concluded by others; otherwise not, as in al-Khulasa and others. The author [of Kanz] did not stipulate speech of the witnesses because it is concluded in the presence of a deaf-mute if he [understands?], as in al-Khulasa. The principle in this chapter is that whoever is fit to be a guardian in marriage by his own authority is fit to be a witness in it; thus the mukatab is excluded because although he owns the marriage of his slave-girl, it is by authority derived from the master, not by his own authority. Then marriage has two rulings: ruling of manifestation (izhar) and ruling of conclusion (in'aqad). The ruling of conclusion is as we mentioned. As for the ruling of manifestation, it is only in case of denial. So in manifestation, only the testimony of those whose testimony is accepted in other rulings is accepted. Thus it is concluded in the presence of sinful persons, blind persons, those who had been punished for slander even if they have not repented, and the sons of the contractors even if their testimony is not accepted by the judge, just as it is concluded in the presence of two enemies. And in al-Bada'i': witnessing in marriage is to repel the accusation of adultery, not to preserve the contract in case of denial; the accusation is repelled by their presence even if their testimony is not accepted, meaning preservation occurs due to their presence although their testimony is not accepted, because marriage becomes apparent and well-known by their presence. When it appears and becomes known, testimony regarding it through hearsay is accepted, so preservation is achieved. Its apparent meaning is that one whose testimony is not accepted, if the marriage is concluded in his presence and then someone whose testimony is accepted informs of it, he may testify through hearsay; so memorize this. And in Fatawa al-Nasafi: The judge may send to a Shafi'i to nullify the contract if it was with the testimony of a sinful person, and the Hanafi may do that. Similarly, if it was without a guardian and he divorced her three times, then sent to a Shafi'i to marry her to him without a tahlil then judge...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the conditions required for witnesses in marriage and distinguishes between two rulings: the ruling of in'aqad (validity) and the ruling of izhar (proof/declaration). For validity, four conditions are necessary: freedom, sanity, puberty, and Islam. Slaves (of any type), insane, children, and non-Muslims cannot be witnesses for a Muslim marriage because they lack authority (wilaya). However, if a slave is freed or a child reaches puberty after bearing witness, their testimony may be accepted if the contract was already valid with others. The author did not require the witnesses to be able to speak; a deaf-mute may serve if he understands. The principle is that anyone capable of being a marriage guardian by his own right can be a witness, except the mukatab (who has authority over his slave but derived from his master). For the ruling of izhar (manifestation), only witnesses whose testimony is generally accepted (i.e., reliable) can prove the marriage in case of denial. Therefore, marriage can be valid (in'aqad) in the presence of sinful, blind, or punished-for-slander individuals, as well as sons of the contractors and personal enemies, even though their testimony would not be accepted in court to establish the marriage. The purpose of witnessing is to repel suspicion of adultery, and this is achieved by their presence and the subsequent spread of knowledge, allowing hearsay testimony later. The passage concludes with a practical note: if a marriage is witnessed only by sinful persons, a judge may involve a Shafi'i judge to nullify it, according to some fatwas.

Key Terms
حكم الإظهار (hukm al-izhar): The ruling related to proving the marriage in court when one party denies it.
حكم الانعقاد (hukm al-in'aqad): The ruling related to the validity of the marriage contract itself, which can be satisfied with witnesses meeting minimal conditions.
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness – a witness who is fasiq (commits major sins) is not acceptable for testimony in general rulings, but can make the marriage valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following statements is correct?
A) A marriage witnessed by two blind persons is invalid (batil).
B) A marriage witnessed by two sinful persons (fasiq) is valid but not provable in court.
C) A marriage witnessed by two non-Muslims is valid if they are free and sane.
D) A marriage may be witnessed by a deaf-mute only if he can speak.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between validity of marriage (sahih with four witness conditions) and its proof (izhar) which requires reliable witnesses; sinful or blind witnesses make the marriage valid but not provable.

Conditions for Witnesses and the Two Rulings of Marriage
States the four conditions for witnesses and distinguishes between rulings of validity and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for witnesses in a Hanafi marriage contract and distinguish between the rulings of validity (in'iqad) and proof (izhar).

Core Idea
Witnesses for marriage must be free, sane, adult, Muslim; validity does not require uprightness, but proof does.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two distinct aspects of witnesses in a marriage contract: their role in making the contract valid (in'iqad) and their role in proving the marriage when denied (izhar). The conditions for witnesses differ depending on whether we are considering validity or proof. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi marriage law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four conditions for witnesses in in'iqad
Read the list of conditions: freedom, sanity, adulthood, Islam. Note that the author explicitly states the contract is not concluded (la yan'aqid) in the absence of any of these.
The condition of adulthood distinguishes minors; freedom excludes slaves of all types; sanity excludes the insane; Islam excludes non-Muslims for a Muslim marriage.
2
Understand the principle behind the conditions
The text gives a principle: whoever is fit to be a guardian in marriage with his own guardianship is fit to be a witness. This explains why slaves and minors are excluded—they lack full guardianship.
The mukatib (slave with a contract of manumission) is excluded because his guardianship is derivative, not own.
3
Distinguish between the two rulings: in'iqad and izhar
In'iqad concerns the validity of the contract itself; izhar concerns proof when the marriage is denied. For in'iqad, the four conditions suffice; for izhar, the witnesses must be those whose testimony is generally accepted in courts.
The text says the ruling of izhar only applies 'when there is denial' (ʿinda al-tajāḥud).
4
Analyze the implications for various types of witnesses
The text gives examples: sinners (fāsiqūn), blind persons, those punished for slander, and sons of the parties can be witnesses for validity but their testimony may not be accepted for proof. The contract is still valid because the purpose is to repel accusations of zina, not to secure judicial proof.
Later testimony by hearsay (tasaamuʿ) is possible if the marriage became known through their presence.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a marriage witnessed by two upright Muslim men. It is valid and can be proven. Consider one witnessed by a sinner and a blind person: valid for in'iqad but proof requires additional evidence.
The mufta bihi position is that the four conditions are necessary for validity; mute witnesses are valid if they can communicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ثم النكاح له حكمان حكم الإظهار وحكم الانعقاد فحكم الانعقاد على ما ذكرنا ، وأما حكم الإظهار فإنما يكون عند التجاحد فلا يقبل في الإظهار إلا شهادة من تقبل شهادته في سائر الأحكام. كذا في شرح الطحاوي. فلذا انعقد بحضور الفاسقين والأعميين والمحدودين في قذف وإن لم يتوبا وابني العاقدين وإن لم يقبل أداؤهم عند القاضي كانعقاده بحضرة العدوين وفي البدائع إن الإشهاد في النكاح لدفع تهمة الزنا لا لصيانة العقد عند الجحود والإنكار والتهمة تندفع بالحضور من غير قبول على أن معنى الصيانة تحصل بسبب حضورهما وإن كان لا تقبل شهادتهما لأن النكاح يظهر ويشتهر بحضورهما، فإذا ظهر واشتهر تقبل الشهادة فيه بالتسامع فتحصل الصيانة .اهـ. وظاهره أن من لا تقبل شهادته إذا انعقد بحضوره ثم أخبر به من تقبل شهادته جاز له الشهادة به بالتسامع فليحفظ هذا.
And [the author] stipulated four conditions for witnesses: freedom, sanity, adulthood, and Islam. So the [marriage] contract is not concluded in the presence of slaves, insane persons, minors, and disbelievers in the marriage of Muslims because these have no guardianship. There is no difference between an absolute slave, a mudabbar, and a mukatib. If the slaves are freed or the minors reach puberty after bearing [witness] and then testify, if there were others at the time of the contract by whose presence [the contract] is concluded, their testimony is permissible because they are qualified for bearing and the contract was concluded by others; otherwise no, as in al-Khulasa and others. And the author did not stipulate that the witnesses must speak [aloud], because it is concluded in the presence of a mute if he is [competent] as in al-Khulasa. The principle in this chapter is that whoever is fit to be a guardian in marriage with his own guardianship is fit to be a witness in it; thus the mukatib is excluded, for although he has permission to marry his slave-woman, it is through guardianship acquired from the master, not his own guardianship. Then marriage has two rulings: the ruling of manifestation (izhar) and the ruling of validity (in'iqad). The ruling of validity is as we mentioned. As for the ruling of manifestation, it is only in case of denial; so only the testimony of one whose testimony is accepted in other legal matters is accepted in manifestation. This is from Sharh al-Tahawi. Therefore, the contract is concluded in the presence of sinners, blind persons, those hadd-punished for slander even if they have not repented, and the sons of the contracting parties even if their testimony is not accepted before the judge, just as it is concluded in the presence of enemies. And in al-Bada'i: 'Indeed, taking witnesses in marriage is to repel the accusation of zina, not to safeguard the contract against denial and repudiation. The accusation is repelled by [their] presence even if not accepted, because the meaning of safeguarding is achieved by their presence even if their testimony is not accepted, because marriage becomes apparent and known through their presence. When it becomes apparent and known, testimony by hearsay (tasaamu') is accepted, thus safeguarding is achieved.' End. And its apparent meaning is that if one whose testimony is not accepted witnesses a marriage and then informs one whose testimony is accepted, he may testify to it by hearsay – so preserve this.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the conditions for witnesses in a marriage contract (nikah) and distinguishes between two legal rulings: the ruling of validity (hukm al-in'iqad) and the ruling of proof (hukm al-izhar). The author, Imam Ibn Nujaym, explains that valid witnesses must be free, sane, adult, and Muslim. The contract is not concluded in the presence of slaves, insane persons, minors, or disbelievers because they lack the necessary guardianship (wilaya) over themselves or others. If a slave is later freed or a minor reaches puberty after witnessing the contract, their testimony may be accepted only if there were other qualified witnesses present at the time of the contract. The text also notes that a mute person can be a valid witness if he can communicate meaningfully. The principle is that anyone qualified to act as a guardian in marriage (with his own guardianship) is qualified as a witness. Then the passage explains the two rulings: (1) hukm al-in'iqad – the validity of the contract itself requires witnesses meeting the four conditions; (2) hukm al-izhar – when there is denial (tajahud), only the testimony of those whose testimony is generally accepted can prove the marriage. However, for validity, the presence of even those whose testimony is normally rejected (like sinners, blind persons, etc.) suffices, because the purpose is to repel the accusation of zina, not to provide judicial proof. The presence of such witnesses makes the marriage known and public, and later testimony by hearsay (tasaamu') can be accepted. This is a crucial distinction: witnesses for validity are not the same as witnesses for proof.

Key Terms
شهود (shuhūd): Witnesses present at the marriage contract; their presence is a condition for validity (in'iqad) and for proof (izhar).
انعقاد (in'iqād): The validity of the marriage contract itself; depends on the presence of witnesses meeting the four conditions.
إظهار (izhār): The legal manifestation or proof of the marriage when there is denial; requires witnesses whose testimony is generally accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Sanity
C) Wealth
D) Islam

Key Takeaway
Witnesses for validity must be free, sane, adult, and Muslim; witnesses for proof must additionally be acceptable in general testimony.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Testimony of Sons in Marriage
Presents the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding testimony of sons in marriage cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on accepting testimony of sons in marriage cases, apply the rule to analogous scenarios, and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Core Idea
Testimony of sons in marriage: accepted if father has no benefit; Abu Yusuf differs on alignment.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether the testimony of a father's sons is admissible in marriage-related cases when the father supports one party. The disagreement hinges on the nature of suspicion (tuhma) and whether direct personal benefit is required to disqualify a witness. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the core case, analogous extensions, and the tarjih in favor of Muhammad's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario and default rule
Read the first part of the passage describing remarriage in presence of sons. Understand that when sons are children of both spouses, testimony is never accepted because it favors their parents.
This default rule establishes that sons cannot testify in favor of a parent.
2
State the specific dispute
Focus on the case: father marries his daughter with his sons as witnesses, then spouses deny. If father supports the denier, testimony is accepted. If father supports the claimant, Abu Yusuf rejects, Muhammad accepts.
The dispute is about the role of the father's alignment.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each Imam
Abu Yusuf considers the father's alignment with the claimant creates suspicion (tuhma) regardless of benefit. Muhammad focuses on absence of direct benefit, so no suspicion.
Tuhma (suspicion) is the key concept.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, if father supports claimant, sons' testimony is rejected; under Muhammad, it is accepted. This affects whether the marriage is proven.
5
Note the tarjih and its basis
Al-Bada'i states Muhammad's view is correct because suspicion must arise from actual benefit. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's view.
The tarjih is based on the principle that tuhma is linked to naf' (benefit).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصورة التزويج بحضرة ابنيهما أن تقع الفرقة بين الزوجين ثم يعقدا بحضور ابنيهما، ولو تجاحدا لا تقبل شهادة ابنيهما مطلقاً لأنه لا يخلو عن شهادتهما لأصلهما، فلو كانا ابنيه وحده تقبل شهادتهما عليه لا له ولو كانا ابنيها وحدها قبلت عليها لا لها، ولو كان أحدهما ابنها والآخر ابنه لم تقبل أصلاً. ومن زوج بنته بشهادة ابنيه ثم تجاحد الزوجان، فإن كان الأب مع الجاحد منهما أيهما كان تقبل شهادتهما لأنها شهادة عليه، وإن كان الأب مع المدعي منهما أيهما كان لم تقبل شهادتهما عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد: تقبل. فأبو يوسف نظر إلى الدعوى والإنكار ومحمد نظر إلى المنفعة وعدمها . وهنا لا منفعة للأب.
قال في البدائع : والصحيح نظر محمد لأن المانع من القبول التهمة وأنها تنشأ عن النفع، وكذلك على هذا الاختلاف فيما إذا قال رجل لعبده . كلمك زيد فأنت حر، ثم قال العبد كلمني زيد وأنكر المولى فشهد للعبد ابنا زيد أن أباهما قد كلمه والمولى ينكر، تقبل عند محمد، ادعى زيد الكلام أو لا، لعدم منفعته، وعند أبي يوسف إن كان زيد يدعي الكلام لا تقبل، وإن كان لا يدعي تقبل وكذا على هذا الاختلاف فيمن توكل عن غيره في عقد ثم شهد ابنا الوكيل على العقد فإن كان حقوق العقد لا ترجع إلى العاقد تقبل عند محمد مطلقاً لعدم المنفعة، وعند أبي يوسف إن كان يدعي لا تقبل وإن كان ينكر تقبل اهـ. ولو زوج بنته وأنكرت الرضا فشهد أخواها وهما ابناه لم تقبل في قولهم لأن الرضا شرط الجواز فكان فيه تنفيذ قول الأب مقصوداً فتكون شهادة له كذا في المحيط. وجعل في الظهيرية قول الإمام في المسألة الأولى كأبي يوسف ولو كانت البنت صغيرة لا تقبل اتفاقاً إلا إذا كان الأب جاحداً والآخر مدعياً فمقبولة كما في فتح القدير
And the scenario of marriage in the presence of their [the couple's] two sons is that separation occurs between the spouses, then they contract in the presence of their two sons. If they deny, the testimony of their two sons is absolutely not accepted because it cannot be free from [the sons testifying] in favor of their ancestor. If the two sons are [only] the husband's sons, their testimony is accepted against him but not for him. If they are [only] the wife's sons, it is accepted against her but not for her. If one is her son and the other is his son, it is not accepted at all. And if a man marries his daughter with the testimony of his two sons, then the spouses deny: if the father is with the denier (whichever one it is), his testimony is accepted because it is testimony against [the daughter]. If the father is with the claimant (whichever one it is), his testimony is not accepted according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: It is accepted. Abu Yusuf considered the claim and denial; Muhammad considered benefit and its absence. Here the father has no benefit. It is stated in al-Bada'i: The correct view is that of Muhammad, because the impediment to acceptance is suspicion (tuhma) and it arises from benefit. The same difference [applies] regarding a man who says to his slave, 'If Zayd speaks to you, you are free,' then the slave says, 'Zayd spoke to me,' and the master denies. Zayd's two sons testify for the slave that their father spoke to him, and the master denies: It is accepted according to Muhammad, whether Zayd claims [the speaking] or not, because there is no benefit for them. According to Abu Yusuf, if Zayd claims [the speaking], it is not accepted; if he does not claim, it is accepted. And similarly regarding one who acts as an agent (wakil) for another in a contract, then the agent's two sons testify about the contract: if the rights of the contract do not revert to the agent, it is accepted according to Muhammad absolutely due to no benefit; according to Abu Yusuf, if he claims [rights], it is not accepted, if he denies, it is accepted. End. And if a man marries his daughter and she denies consent, then her two brothers who are his sons testify: it is not accepted in their [all] view because consent is a condition of validity and it would implement the father's statement intentionally, so it would be testimony for him, as in al-Muhit. And in al-Zahiriyya, the Imam's [Abu Hanifa's] statement in the first issue is made like Abu Yusuf. And if the daughter is a minor, it is not accepted by agreement, except if the father is a denier and the other is a claimant, then it is accepted, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the acceptance of testimony from sons in marriage-related cases. The core issue is whether the father's alignment with a claimant or denier affects the admissibility of his sons' testimony. The text begins by describing the scenario where a couple remarries after divorce in the presence of their two sons. If they later deny the marriage, the sons' testimony is generally not accepted because it necessarily involves testifying in favor of their parent. If the sons are only the husband's sons, their testimony is accepted against him but not for him; similarly for the wife. If one son is from each parent, it is not accepted at all. Then the specific case: a father marries his daughter with his sons as witnesses, and later the couple denies. If the father supports the denier (i.e., denies the marriage), the sons' testimony is accepted because it is against the daughter (not in favor of the father). If the father supports the claimant (i.e., affirms the marriage), Abu Yusuf rejects the testimony due to suspicion of partiality (the father benefits from affirming the marriage), while Muhammad accepts it because the father has no direct benefit from the marriage itself. Al-Bada'i declares Muhammad's view correct because the impediment is suspicion (tuhma) which arises from benefit, and here there is no benefit to the sons. The same principle applies to other cases, such as a man's conditional freeing of his slave upon Zayd's speech, and an agent's sons testifying about a contract. The passage also notes that if the daughter is a minor, the testimony is not accepted except when the father is the denier. This discussion highlights the Hanafi school's careful balancing of procedural rules and substantive justice.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwīj): The act of giving in marriage, especially a father marrying off his daughter.
شهادة (shahāda): Testimony in court; here it refers to the statement of witnesses regarding a marriage contract.
منفعة (manfa'a): Benefit or personal interest that may create suspicion of bias in testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a father marries his daughter with his sons as witnesses and the spouses later deny, what is Muhammad's view on accepting the sons' testimony if the father supports the claimant?
A) Accepted, because the father has no benefit
B) Not accepted, because the father benefits
C) Accepted only if the daughter is a minor
D) Not accepted at all

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's view is the relied-upon position: testimony of sons in marriage is accepted when the father has no direct benefit, irrespective of his alignment with claimant or denier.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Recommendation to Write Marriage Contract
Ruling statement with qiyas expanding recommendation to marriage contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on writing the marriage contract and explain the qiyas that supports it.

Core Idea
Writing the marriage contract is recommended to prevent denial, just like emancipation and debts.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why documenting the marriage contract is recommended (mustahabb) rather than obligatory. By drawing an analogy to the kitābah contract of emancipation and the Quranic recommendation to document debts, the author shows that writing prevents future denial while avoiding hardship. The ruling is a sunnah ghayr muakkadah, meaning it is commendable but not required for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses writing a marriage contract. The ruling given is 'yustahabb' (it is recommended). Determine the hukm level: mustahabb/sunnah ghayr muakkadah.
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes mustahabb from wajib.
2
Locate the original case (asl) and the new case (far')
The asl is the emancipation contract (kitābah) where writing is recommended. The far' is the marriage contract (nikāh) being analogized.
Qiyas requires a clear asl with a known ruling.
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is prevention of denial (saving from denial). This is explicitly mentioned for emancipation and debt, and applied to marriage.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear) from the text.
4
Apply the qiyas
Since marriage also involves rights that could be denied, and writing does not cause hardship (unlike frequent transactions), the ruling of recommendation transfers from emancipation to marriage.
The exception: frequent transactions are excluded because writing would cause hardship.
5
Note the hukm level and its implications
The ruling is mustahabb, not wajib. Thus, a marriage contract is valid without writing, but it is better to document it.
The Hanafi position is that writing is recommended, not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العتق : ويستحب للعبد أن يكتب للعتق كتاباً ويشهد عليه شهوداً توثيقاً وصيانة عن التجاحد كما في المداينة بخلاف سائر التجارات لأنه مما يكثر وقوعها فالكتابة فيها تؤدي إلى الحرج ولا كذلك العتق .اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون النكاح كالعتق لأنه لا حرج فيها .
Chapter of Emancipation: It is recommended for the slave to write a contract of emancipation and have witnesses testify to it for documentation and prevention of denial, just as in the case of debt, unlike other transactions because they occur frequently so writing in them leads to hardship, and not so in emancipation. End. And marriage should be like emancipation because there is no hardship in it.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the sharh. It first states a recommendation (istihbab) for a slave to write a kitaba contract for emancipation, with witnesses, to prevent denial. This is analogized to the practice in debt transactions (mudayanah). The author notes that writing is not recommended for other transactions because they occur frequently and writing would cause hardship. Then, by analogy, marriage contracts should also be written to avoid denial, as marriage is not a frequent transaction causing hardship. The hukm level is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitābah): A contract of emancipation between a slave and master where the slave pays for freedom in installments.
مداينة (mudāyanah): Debt transaction or loan, where writing is recommended to avoid disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is writing the marriage contract recommended by analogy to emancipation?
A) Because marriage is a frequent transaction
B) Because writing prevents denial of the contract
C) Because the Quran commands it
D) Because it is obligatory to document marriages

Key Takeaway
Writing the marriage contract is a recommended practice (mustahabb) to safeguard against denial, not a condition of validity.
